Note from the editor 
 
The root verses  are quoted in their entirety from A Guide to the Bodhis attva’s Way of Life , with the 
permission of the translator, Stephen Batchelor. (Published: Library of Tibetan Works and 
Archives, Dharamsala, 1979)  
Two changes to the original terminology have  been made throughout the translation: sentient 
beings replaces creatures , while child replaces son in epithets, for example, ‘Conqueror’s son’, or 
‘Buddha’s son’. Other slight amendments to th e root verses (made for the Basic Program at 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre) have b een marked with an asterisk.
 
 
The root verses are centered, italicized, and mark ed by the chapter and verse numbers in square 
brackets. 
 
The outlines are from The Subject Headings from Gyaltsab Je’s  Commentary to Shantideva’s ‘Engaging 
in the Bodhisattva Deeds’, translated by Ven. Losang Sopa (Bob Miller), August 2006. They were 
used for the transcripts with permission  from the FPMT Education Department.  
 
The outlines are indented, in bold, and have been  slightly amended by the editor and Amitabha 
Buddhist Centre’s interpretor, Ven. Tenzin Gyurme, for the Basic Program at ABC. The 
amendments are also marked with an asterisk. 
 Students and staff of Amitabha Buddhist Centre produced the transcripts; the oral commentary 
was transcribed by Phuah Soon Ek, Vivien Ng and Angie Xiao; the transcripts were proofread 
and checked by Fiona O’Shaughnessy, Cecilia Tson g, Tara Dhar, Tara Hasnain, and Yap Siew 
Kee.  The transcripts were further prepared for FP MT Basic Program Homestudy by the editor. 
 Olga Planken,  FPMT Education Department, August 2006.  
 
1 Transcripts of the Commentary by Geshe Chonyi 
on Shantideva’s  
Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
 
Lesson 1– chapter 1, verse 1 
Introduction 
The teaching based on the text Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds  authored by the great bodhisattva 
Shantideva is an unmistaken teaching. The bodhisattva Shantideva himself had generated 
bodhicitta in his mental continuum and on this  basis he engaged in the practice of the six 
perfections. This text was composed based on his own experiences and realizations.  
 
In the great treatises composed by the holy mast ers of the past, there is always a promise of 
composition at the beginning of the text. The purp ose of including such a promise is to carry out 
the fulfilment to complete the composition of the text. In the same way, we should make a 
promise to complete this module (which  covers the first half of the text Engaging in the Bodhisattva 
Deeds ) and bring it to fulfilment through setting a st rong determination. It is very important to 
make very strong aspirational prayers to the guru and meditational deities for the success of 
completing this course. It is also very importan t to dedicate the accumulated merit for the benefit 
of all sentient beings at the end of every session. If we do these continuously, we will definitely be 
of benefit to sentient beings after some time.   
The ten chapters of Shantideva’s text Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds  are a commentary 
explaining the meaning of the often-recited de dication prayer for bodhicitta attributed to 
Nagarjuna: 
May the precious bodhi mind 
Not yet born arise and grow, 
May that born have no decline, 
But increase forever more. 
 The ten chapters of the Engaging in the Bodhisattvas’ Deeds  (Bodhisattvacaryavatara ) are: 
1. Explaining the benefits of bodhicitta [The Benefits of the Awakening Mind] 
2. Confessing negativities [Disclosure of Evil] 
3. Thoroughly embracing bodhicitta [Fu ll Acceptance of the Awakening Mind] 
4. Conscientiousness  
5. Relying on vigilance [Guarding Alertness] 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
2 6. Patience 
7. Joyous perseverance [Enthusiasm] 
8. Concentration [Meditation] 
9. Wisdom 
10. Dedication  
 
The first two lines of the prayer explain the first three chapters of  the text, presenting the method 
to generate bodhicitta in our mind. Not yet born arise and grow – this indicates the generation of 
bodhicitta which we have not ye t generated before. There must be a means in order for us to 
generate such a bodhicitta mind. The means is that we must first contemplate the benefit of 
having such a mind before we can generate  bodhicitta in our mental continuum. The 
contemplation on the benefits of bodhicitta will increase enthusiasm for developing the 
bodhicitta mind.  
The first chapter, Explaining the benefits of bodhicitta , talks about the advantag es of generating this 
bodhicitta (the altruistic to achieve enlightenment for the benefit of all sentient beings).  When 
we understand the advantages of having the bodh icitta mind, it will inspire us to go for it.  
 The second chapter, Confessing negativities , is to get rid of all obstructing factors, obstacles or 
accumulated negativities before we can generate bo dhicitta in our mental continuum. This is like 
cleaning our house and keeping it neat before we invite a very important person, such as king, to 
our house. If the house is not clean, the invited guest will probably not come at all. In the same 
manner, if we do not purify those negativities accumulated in the past, it will be difficult for us to 
generate bodhicitta in our mental continuum. Theref ore it is important for us to purify our mind 
of these negativities. 
 
The third chapter, Thoroughly embracing bodhicitta , explains how to gather favourable conditions 
for developing bodhicitta in our mind. Favourable  conditions mean accumulating the collections 
of merit such as making offerings. 
 
The third line of the prayer, May that born have no decline , is explained from chapter 4 to 6 on 
how to prevent the bodhicitta in the mental co ntinuum from degenerating. These three chapters 
indicate the method for generating the antidotes. It is insufficient just to prevent the generated 
bodhicitta from degenerating; we must also strive to increase it as well. This is being mentioned 
in the last line of the prayer: But increase fore ver more. For this to happen, we must rely on 
joyous perseverance, concentration & wisdom as in chapters 7 to 9 of this text.  
 
In summary, the text Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds  is divided into three main sections along 
the lines of the prayer a ttributed to Nagarjuna: 
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
3 The first three chapters are:  
1.  Explaining the benefits of bodhicitta,   
2.  Confessing negativities  
3.  Thoroughly embracing bodhicitta  These are the methods for generating bodh icitta that has not yet been generated 
 The following three chapters are: 4.  Conscientious    5.  Relying on vigilance 6.  Patience  These are the methods to prevent bodhicitta from degenerating. 
 The next three chapters:  7.  Joyous perseverance 8.  Concentration  9.  Wisdom  These are the methods to increase bodh icitta that had been degenerated. 
 The last chapter:  10.  Dedication  This is a concluding prayer for dedication.  The bodhisattva deeds summarise the practice of the 6 perfections. The fi rstst two perfections, 
the perfection of generosity and perfection of et hics are not treated as separate chapters. Some 
commentaries mention that generosity is being taug ht in chapter five (Relying on vigilance) but 
some other commentaries mention that generosity is being taught in chapter ten: dedication. 
(Geshela thinks that it should be covered in ch apter five.) The remaining perfections (patience, 
joyous perseverance, concentration and wisdom) ar e covered in separate chapters in this text. 
 Due to our limited minds, we may feel that it is difficult for us to generate the bodhicitta mind. 
When we recite this dedication prayer, bodhicitta seems to be just an object of the prayer. But by 
using scriptural authority, through valid quotat ions, and through logic and reasoning, we can 
conclude that it is possible to generate the mi nd of bodhicitta. This depends on many factors 
such as the amount of effort we put in and how we approach this practice. As explained in the 
previous module [Lam rim], the methods for genera ting bodhicitta are the seven cause-and-effect 
personal instruction and exchanging self and others.  The main body of this commentary is divided into four outlines:

2   The translator's homage May the precious bodhi min d

4 3   The meaning of the text 
4   The conclusion 
1  The meaning of the title 
The original Sanskrit title of this text is Bodhisattvacaryavatara . 
In English this can be rendered as Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds . 
 
Of the word “bodhi”, which is byang chub  (jang chub) in Tibetan, byang  means to purify – this 
indicates that all the obstructions to liberati on and obstructions to omniscience have been 
completely purified in the state of enlightenment. Chub  means to realise all good qualities. 
“Sattva” is sems dpa  (sem pa) in Tibetan; sems dpa  means the heroic one, which shows that the 
state of mind of the bodhisattva is very cour ageous, of strong determination and with great 
compassion for sentient beings. 
2  The translator's homage 
Homage or prostration to all the buddhas and bodh isattvas - such homage is in accordance with 
the tradition laid down by the gr eat Dharma kings with the intentio n for the reader to identify to 
which “basket” [pitaka] of Buddha’s teachings the or iginal text belonged. In this case homage has 
been made to the buddhas an d bodhisattvas because the Bodhisattvacaryavatara  belongs to the 
sutra pitaka. 
 
Texts are classified in this way as follows: 
• vinaya pitaka – dealing mainly with the higher training in ethics, then homage is paid to 
the omniscient one 
• sutra pitaka – dealing mainly with the higher training in  concentration, then homage is 
paid to the buddhas and bodhisattvas 
• abhidharma pitaka – dealing mainly with the higher training in wisdom, then homage is 
to be paid to youthful Manjughosha, the embodiment of enlightened wisdom. 
 
The purpose of the translator paying homage to  the buddhas and bodhisattvas is to remove 
obstacles and ensure completion of their work. 
3  The meaning of the text  
A The act of introducing the explanation of the stages of the Mahayana path 
B The actual explanation of the stages of the path 
3A The act of introducing the explanation of the stages of the Mahayana path 
1  Expression of worship 2  The pledge to explain [this material] 
3  Expressing humility and how Shantideva was inspired to compose [this text] 
3A1 Expression of worship 
A The purpose

5 B A short expression of worship 
C The meaning of these words 
[1,1a] 
Respectfully I prostrate myself to the Sugatas 
Who are endowed with the Dharmakaya 
As well as to their Noble Children 
And to all who are worthy of veneration 
 
The actual text begins with Shantideva paying homage to the Three Jewels - the Buddha, Dharma 
and Sangha. We should go for refuge and make  offerings to the Three Jewels and entrust 
ourselves to the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha for success in whatever we do; whether standing, 
walking, sitting etc. If we do th is at the beginning of each activi ty, this will help to pacifying any 
potential obstacles that might arise. This is the uncommon practice of the Mahayana refuge.  
3A1A The purpose 
The purposes of Shantideva paying homage to the three jewels are threefold: to accord with the 
tradition of the past masters, to eliminate the ob stacles that might arise while composing the text 
and as a cause for attaining definite goodness. 
3A1B  A short expression of worship 
Besides making prostration to the Three Jewels, one should also make prostrations to all suitable 
objects of veneration; including one’s abbot, pr eceptor and all those worthy of veneration.  
3A1C   The meaning of these words 
Su – bliss, goodness, excellent; gata – gone ; sugata  means “one who has gone to bliss”. One can 
explain the word sugata  from the perspective of the qualitie s of abandonment or the qualities of 
realisation. The qualities of abandonment have  three characteristics: good abandonment, 
irreversible abandonment and thorough aban donment. Abandonment means exhaustion of 
afflictions. The qualities of realisation have th ree characteristics: knower, stable knower and 
complete knower. The dharmakaya – the truth body of the Sugata refers to  the natural purity of 
the Buddha’s mind and the mind of the Buddha th at is free of all adventitious obscurations. 
Sugata refers to the Buddha Jewel, dharmakaya refe rs to the truth body of the Buddha and is the 
Dharma Jewel, noble children refers to the arya bodhisattvas and is the Sangha Jewel.  
 After paying homage to the Three Jewels, next comes the promise of composition:  
 
[1,1b] 
Here I shall explain how to engage in  the vows of the Buddha’s Children, 
The meaning of which I have condensed in accordance with the scriptures.

6 Lesson 2 – chapter 1, verse 1-4 
3A1C The meaning of these words 
Su – bliss, goodness, excellent, gata – gone; s ugata  means ‘one gone to bliss’. One can examine 
the word sugata from the perspective of the qualities of abandonment or the qualities of 
realisation.  
 When we examine the word sugata  from the perspective of the qu alities of abandonment, this 
refers to the Buddha having achieved the st ate of abandonment of the two obstructions 
(obstructions to liberation and obstructions to omniscience). The qualities of abandonment have 
three characteristics: good or excellent abandonm ent, irreversible abandonment and thorough or 
complete abandonment.  Good abandonment  refers to the Buddha having  completely achieved the state of 
abandonment, which is the exhaustion of all the a fflictions (obstructions to liberation). This is 
called good abandonment because it is supe rior to the non-Buddhists. This state of 
abandonment can never be achieved by the non-Buddhist. Irreversible abandonment  means that 
the Sugata has achieved the state that will neve r fall back to cyclic existence again. This 
irreversible abandonment is supe rior to the state that the stre am-enterer and once-returner are 
abiding in. (Stream- enterer refers to a superior being on the Hinayana path  of seeing, who  will 
never  be reborn in the three lower realms again.  Once-returner refers to a superior being on the 
Hinayana path of meditation who will return to the desire realm on ly one more time). Thorough 
abandonment  refers to the state of abandonment achi eved by the Buddha that is characterised 
by abandonment of the two obstructions –obstr uctions to liberation and obstructions to 
omniscience. This thorough aban donment is superior to the Hi nayana arhat or foe-destroyer. 
(Obstructions to liberation refers to obstruct ions which are afflictions; obstructions to 
omniscience refers to obstructio ns which are not afflictions) 
 When we examine the word sugata from the perspect ive of the qualities of realisation, this refers 
to the omniscient wisdom that realises all phen o m e n a  d i r e c t l y .   T h i s  i s  s u p e r i o r  t o  t h e  n o n -
Buddhists. The qualities of realisation have th ree characteristics: knower, stable knower, 
complete knower.   Knower  refers to the omniscient wisdom of the Buddha that directly sees the meaning of 
selflessness. This knower is superior to the non-Buddhists. Stable knower  refers to the stable 
wisdom of the Buddha that directly sees the mean ing of selflessness and is the antidote to the 
innate afflictions and their seeds
1. This stable knower is superior because this characteristic is not 
                                              
1 All the faults we possess are in cluded in the two obstructions, obstructions to liberation  (also known as afflictive 
obstructions ) and obstructions to omniscience . Obstructions to liberation such as ignorance, anger and 
attachment prevent us from attaining liberation from sams ara. The imprints of the afflictions are obstructions to 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

7 possessed by the superior beings of the Hinayana path. Complete knower  refers to the wisdom 
possessed by a buddha that completely understand s all the three paths (the  paths of the hearer, 
solitary realiser and bodhisattva) an d that is also able to teach it di rectly to others.  The Sugata [or 
buddha] is thus superior to an arhat or foe-destroyer. 
 If we look at the meaning of “sugata” from the vi ew point of the path, then  the path to bliss is 
the Mahayana path. The goal is en lightenment.  As bliss is  achi eved as the result of having 
completed the Mahayana path, the Sugata is known as the One Gone to Bliss.  
 The two qualities (qualities of abandonment and qualities of realisation) as possessed by the Sugata are posited as the Buddha Jewel and the Dharma Jewel. From the perspective of the 
Prasangika-Madhyamika school, the natural abando nment, which is one portion of the state of 
abandonment of the Sugata, refers to the fact that  the Buddha’s mind is empty of true existence. 
T h i s  m i n d  i s  a l s o  s e p a r a t e d  o r  f r e e  f r o m  a l l  a d ventitious defilements or afflictions. These two 
make up the state of abandonment achieved by th e Sugata and this is posited as true cessation. 
 True cessation refers to the Buddha’s mind bein g empty of true existence and the Buddha’s mind 
being completely free from all afflictions and its imprints.  
[1,1a] 
Respectfully I prostrate myself to the Sugata 
Who are endowed with the Dharmakaya 
As well as to their Noble Children 
And to all who are worthy of veneration 
                                                                                                                                                          
 
omniscience; they  prevent us from realising all objects of knowledge dire ctly and simultaneously. 
 
Obstructions to liberation can be divided into: acquired  obstructions to liberation (acquired afflictive 
obstructions), wich can be seeds or manifest, and innate  obstructions to liberation (innate afflictive obstructions); 
these can be in the form of seeds, or manifest. 
 
Acquired afflictive obstructions are intellectually acquired  and arise from  the influence of wrong tenets or wrong 
beliefs. For example, following wrong tenets or wrong be liefs, and supported by wrong reasoning, the conception 
of a person as being truly existent can be generated . A mind that follows this wrong reasoning has intellectually 
acquired afflictive obstructions. The seed that is being plac ed by such a conception is also an acquired afflictive 
obstruction. 
 Innate afflictive obstructions have been in our minds si nce beginningless time and arise due to our fami liarity with 
and predisposition to the conception of grasping at the person as being truly or inherently existent. The innate 
afflictive obstructions arise naturally due to our familiarity with them; they  do not come from wrong tenets or 
wrong beliefs. Such innate grasping at a self of persons is an innate afflictive obstruction. The seed that is placed by such an obstruction is also an innate afflictive obstruction. 
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
8 This verse refers to  the three objects of refuge : the ultimate Buddha Jewe l, the ultimate Dharma 
Jewel and the ultimate Sangha Jewel. 
 
• ultimate Buddha Jewel: the nature tr uth body and the wisdom truth body 
• conventional Buddha Jewel: the complete enjoyment body & the supreme emanation 
body 
 
• ultimate Dharma Jewel: true paths and true cessations 
• conventional Dharma Jewel: scri ptural teachings of the Buddha 
 
• ultimate Sangha Jewel: arya bodhisattvas 
• conventional Sangha Jewel: a group of four or more fully ordained monks   
 
The wisdom truth body is the mind that is comp letely free from the two obstructions; the nature 
truth body is the emptiness of a buddha’s mind ; the supreme emanation body  is a buddha’s 
form body that can be perceived by fortunate ordinary beings. (eg. Buddha Shakyamuni); the 
complete enjoyment body is a buddha’s subtle form body that can be perceived only by  superiors bodhisattvas.   Those who are endowed with the dharmakaya ar e the sugatas (dharmakaya refers to the truth 
body of a buddha). The Sugata is posited as the Bu ddha Jewel.  This also implies that the Sugata 
includes the complete enjoyment body and the supreme emanation body.  These two bodies are 
posited as the final Sangha Jewel. These two bodies are also the Buddha Jewel. Buddha and 
Buddha Jewel are synonymous.  When we say buddha, it does not necessarily refer to any particular person. This is in accordance 
with the great treatises and the explanation of the texts. The word buddha here refers to the true 
path and true cessation in the mental continuum of the enlightened ones. It also refers to the 
bodies possessed by that mental continuum.   A buddha or an enlightened one refers to th e state of complete abandonment of the two 
obstructions and their imprints. Therefore the true  cessation and true path in the continuum of 
a buddha superior are  posited as buddha. The bo dy possessed by that continuum is posited as 
buddha as well. The true paths and true cessations possessed by the Sugata can also be posited as 
the final Dharma Jewel and the ul timate Buddha Jewel. In the co ntext of Shantideva’s homage, 
the Sangha Jewel refers to the superior beings  on the Mahayana path or arya bodhisattvas, but 
they are not the final Sangha Jewel.    After paying homage to the Three Jewels, next comes the promise of composition:

9 3A2 The pledge to explain [this material] 
A Identifying the subject matter that  is to be explained 
B Rejecting the fault [of this material being of Shantideva's] own fabrication 
C Rejecting [the  idea that it is unnecessary] repetition 
D Identifying four [elements of the explanation] such as its purpose  
 [1,1b] 
Here I shall explain how to engage in  the vows of the Buddha’s children, 
The meaning of which I have condensed in accordance with the scriptures. 
 
“Here I shall explain how to engage in the vows of  the Buddha’s children” indicates the pledge to 
compose. 
 “The Buddha’s children” refer to the sons and daug hters of the Sugata who have gone to bliss - 
the bodhisattvas who are  travelling on the Maha yana path to enlightenment by practicing the six 
perfections.   
“The vows of the Buddha’s children” is a translation of the Tibetan term dom. Some 
commentaries interprete dom as the vows of the Buddha’s childr en (or the bodhisattva vows), but 
Gyaltsab Je says this interpretation is too limite d. He interpreted the word  as “to include”, which 
means dom refers to all the essential practices of the bodhisattvas which are summarised in the 
practices of the six perfections. 
 
However, in another commentary, Gyaltsab Je s ays that it is alright to interpret the word dom as 
the bodhisattva vows if we look at  the bodhisattva vows from the pe rspective of the three types of 
the bodhisattvas’ morality. Gyaltsab Je said that if we look at the word dom from the perspective 
of these three types of morality, then it is alright because these three types of morality encompass 
the entire practice of the six perfections. 
 
Morality or ethical discipline is the wish to abandon harming other sentient beings. The three 
types of morality are: 1. the ethi cs of restraining from activities that are the basis of harm, 2. the 
ethics of engaging in virtuous actions, and 3. the ethics of benefiting sentient beings 
3A2A Identifying the subject matter that is to be explained 
“The vows of the Buddha’s children” shows the subj ect matter of the text th at is to be composed, 
which deals with the practices of the bodhisattvas, the practice of bodhicitta and the practice of 
the six perfections. 
 Shantideva is trying to explain how the bodhisattvas, upon generating the altruistic intention to 
attain enlightenment for the benefit of all sentie nt beings (bodhicitta), then practice the six 
perfections in order to achieve the stat e of enlightenment to become buddhas 
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
10 3A2B Rejecting the fault [of this material being of Shantideva's] own fabrication 
How is Shantideva going to explain the practices of the bodhisattvas? This  is mentioned in the 
line: “The meaning of which I have condensed in accordance with the scriptures”. The line “In 
accordance with the scriptures” shows how Shantideva is going to compose the text. Shantideva 
mentioned that he is going to compose the subject matte r of this text in accordance with the 
scriptures and not fabricate it  himself. 
3A2C Rejecting [the idea that it is unnecessary] repetition 
Someone may ask “Why bother to compose this te xt if it is written in accordance with the 
scriptures?” and accuse Shantideva of repeating  su bject matter that is alread y in the scriptures of 
the Buddha. 
 
Shantideva refuted this  with “The  meaning of which I have condensed ” which means that he is 
summarising the bodhisattvas’ practice in accordance  with the scriptures and, therefore, there is 
no fault of repetition.  
 “In accordance with the scriptures” – the scriptures  referred to here are the translated words of  
sutra and tantra of the Buddha (the Kangyur) and probably efers to the Perfection of Wisdom Sutras 
which show the practices of the six perfection s. The actual sutras spoken by the Buddha, 
especially those sutras that contain the practices of the six perfections are very profound and very 
extensive. For the benefit of sentient beings, one needs to internalise and  understand these 
practices from these sutras.  Shantideva said that  he is condensing the meaning of the scriptures 
into this text to benefit sentient beings as  he mentions “The meaning of which I have 
condensed”. 
3A2D Identifying four [elements of the explanation] such as its purpose 
The subject matter is the Maha yana path, the fruit is comple ting the Mahayana path. The 
purpose of this composition is realising the meaning by putting it into practi ce.  The end result is 
achieving the state of Buddhahood.  The main point is to achieve the state of fu ll enlightenment by practicing the path to 
enlightenment and internalising  it to realise it completely.   In order to realise it, we need to identify th e subject matter that is being practised by the 
bodhisattva. In order to identify the subject matter, we need a text that clearly shows the practice.  
3A3 Expressing humility and how Shantideva was inspired to compose [this text] 
A With humility [Shantideva says that he] did not compose this primarily for the benefit of others 
B The reasons he was inspired to compose this treatise 
C Showing that it [might] also be of benefit to others of equal fortune 
Shantideva expresses humility in verse 3 because he has to counter whatev er pride may arise and 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
11 this may create obstacles for him when he is co mposing the text. He is also encouraging himself 
and generating enthusiasm to complete the comp osition of this text. If he is down with 
depression, it will also pose  an obstac le for him to complete his composition.  
 
He is composing this text to accomplish his ow n purpose and for the purpose of other sentient 
beings.  We can also apply this attitude in our daily life. If we are full of pride, then it will be an obstacle for us to complete our activities. At the other extreme, if we are depressed, then it will 
also become an obstacle to fulfilling our  goals. We should behave like Shantideva to decrease our pride and to increase our enthusiasm for virtue. 
 
[1,2] 
There is nothing here that ha s not been explained before 
And I have no skill in  the art of rhetoric; 
Therefore, lacking any intention to benefit others, 
I write this in order to acquaint it to my mind. 
3A3A With humility [Shantideva says that he] did not compose this primarily for the benefit of 
others 
With humility, Shantideva says he has no intentio n of benefiting others by composing this text 
because he admits that this text will not contai n anything that has not already been explained 
before by the buddhas and bodhisattvas. Also, he s ays he has no skill in the art of rhetoric or 
poetry. His  purpose in composing this text is to ensure that the qualities that he has already 
achieved in the past will not degenerate and also to increase his own virtue: “I write this in order 
to acquaint it to my mind.”   
 Discussion: if Shantideva had not yet internalized  the subject matter of th e text he was going to 
compose, he would not have been in any position to compose it. If he had already internalised  
the subject matter, then there would have been no reason for him to compose this text to 
acquaint it with his mind. Why did he feel the need to compose this text? 
 
[1,3] 
For due to acquaintance wi th what is wholesome, 
The force of my faith may for a short while increase because of these (words). 
If, however, these (words) are seen by others 
Equal in fortune to myself, it may be meaningful (for them). 
3A3B The reasons he was inspired to compose this treatise 
“For due to acquaintance with what is wholesom e, The force of my faith may for a short while 
increase because of these (words).” Shantideva said  that by composing this text, it will act as a 
condition to help himself to increase his virtue and also to increase his faith. 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
12 3A3C Showing that it [might] also be of benefit to others of equal fortune 
“If, however, these (words) are seen by others eq ual in fortune to myself, it may be meaningful 
(for them).” He hoped that  others, equal in fortun e to himself, having seen this text, will  benefit 
from this text as well  
3B The actual explanation of the stages of the path 
1 Encouraging [the reader] to take the essence from this basis of  leisures and endowments 
2 Explaining how to take the essence 
3B1 Encouraging [the reader] to take the essence from this basis of leisures and endowments 
[1,4] 
Leisure and endowment are very hard to find 
And, since they accomplish what is meaningful for humanity, 
If I do not take advantage of them now, 
How will such a perfect opportunity come about again? 
 
“Leisure and endowment are very hard  to find”: this human life is pr ecious as it has eight leisures 
and ten opportunities that make it  an ideal opportunity for training  the mind in all the stages of 
the path to enlightenment. This human life eq uipped with these leisures and opportunities is 
e x t r e m e l y  d i f f i c u l t  t o  f i n d  a s  i t  i s  v e r y  r a r e  f o r anyone to create the causes for achieving such 
leisures and opportunities. Once found, one mu st make full use of it to practice and to 
accomplish the holy Dharma.   In general, the main cause of a human rebirth is the practice of morality. In order to achieve the 
human rebirth with leisures and opportunities, on e must practice stainless morality accompanied 
by the practice of the other perfections. One al so needs to make stainless prayers to gain a 
human rebirth. Training in these three causes is very difficult  and, therefore, attaining a human 
form with leisures and opportunities will al s o  b e  d i f f i c u l t .  A s  e x p l a i n e d  i n  t h e  L a m  R i m  
Chenmo, the difficulties in obtaining human re birth with leisures and opportunities are 
explained:  1) through analogy, 2)  in  terms of numbers and 3) through causes. 
 Shantideva is encouraging the reader to take  the essence from this basis of leisure and 
opportunities as we now have a most rare and pr ecious fully-endowed human form. The first line 
of verse 4 indicates the difficulties of obtaining a human rebirth with all the leisures and opportunities. The next three lines indicate the gr eat purposes that can be achieved on the basis 
of a human rebirth with such leisures and opportunities.  On the basis of a human rebirth with these leis ures and opportunities, one can achieve higher 
rebirth as a human and god. One can also achieve the definite goodness of liberation or enlightenment on the basis of a human rebirth wi th these leisures and opportunities. One needs 
to put in effort to achieve this higher goal inst ead of sitting around idly and putting off the time 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
13 to practice dharma by thinking that one will not die.  One’s time of death is uncertain and if one 
was to die suddenly without practicing the dharma, th en it will be very difficult to obtain another 
human rebirth with such leisures and opportunities again in the future.  
 
Obtaining such a human rebirth again in the futu re is almost impossible because most of the 
time, in cyclic existence, we have been taking rebirth in situations where we do not posses the 
leisures and opportunities to practice the Dhar ma. As the precious human rebirth is so rare, 
therefore, it is very precious. Therefore, we sh ould use it for the great purposes once we have 
obtained it as it does not last forever.   A perfect human rebirth  is endowed with two unique sets of privileges: the leisures and 
opportunities (endowments).  Leisure  means freedom from rebirth in  any of the eight conditions 
that lack leisure, and opportunity (endowment)  refers to the good conditions amenable to the 
practice of Dharma. If we have a human life with all these eight leis ures, we shall find it relatively 
easy to overcome any other unfavourable co nditions that we might experience. The ten 
opportunities are all necessary conditio ns for our practice of the Dharma. 
 The eight leisures  are characterised by being born free from  certain kinds of existence that lack 
the conditions to be able to practice Dharma. There are four human states  and four nonhuman 
states  that lack the leisure to be able to practice Dharma. 
 The four nonhuman states that lack leisure  for the study and practice the Dharma are: rebirth 
as a hell being, rebirth as an animal, rebirth as  a hungry ghost, and rebirth as a long-life god. 
The four human states that lack leisure  to be able to study and practice the Dharma are: rebirth 
in a land where there is no dharma (as barbar ians), rebirth in a place where a buddha has not 
descended, meaning a place where a buddha has not come and taught, rebirth with extreme 
mental or physical defects, and rebirth with strong  wrong views, particularly in relation to cause 
and effect, past and future lives, or the three objects of refuge.  The second set of privileges that we experience , having gained a perfect human rebirth, are the 
ten opportunities , five of which pertain to ourselves, and five of which pertain to external 
conditions.  The five opportunities pertaining to oneself  are: 
1. being born as a human being 
2. born in a central place where the dharma flourishes 
3. having perfect and complete senses and sense organs 
4. not having committed one of the five heinous crimes, and thus having the potential to 
change one's karma.  
5. having faith in the Lamrim, and in the thr ee vast collections of Buddha's teaching 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                I n t r o d u c t i o n  
 
 
14 The five opportunities pertaini ng to external conditions  are: 
1. having been born in a time when a buddha has descended into the world  
2. having been born in a time when  a buddha has taught the dharma 
3. the teachings of the Buddha still remain in the world 
4. there are still people practicing the teachings 
5. that, through the kindness and compassion of ot hers, it is possible that practitioners have 
the opportunity to receive material support that enables them the freedom to practice. 
 
Thus it is very important for us to recognise that the eight leisur es and ten opportunities are very 
difficult to find and we need to check whether we  posses all these 18 char acteristics. If we do 
have most of them, we should practice the Dharma as what we have obtained is very rare indeed. 
But if we do not make use of our present existenc e to the fullest for practicing dharma, it will be 
extremely difficult to gain such a beneficially endowed form again.  From Basic Program Module 1 – Middle Lam Rim:

3B The actual explanation of the stages of the path 
1 Encouraging [the reader] to take the essence from this basis of  leisure and endowments 
2 Explaining how to take the essence 
3B1 Encouraging [the reader] to take the essence from this basis of leisures and endowments 
[1,4] 
Leisure and endowment are very hard to find 
And, since they accomplish what is meaningful for humanity, 
If I do not take advantage of them now, 
How will such a perfect opportunity come about again? 
 
“Leisure and endowment are very hard to find” - Th is line refers to the fact that a human life 
qualified with leisure and endowment (or opportunitie s) is difficult to obtain. Once human life is 
endowed with these characteristics, one can achieve great purposes on this basis by practicing the 
teaching of the Buddha. However if we were to postpone our practice and hope to do it later, we 
have to understand that death is certain but the time of our death is uncertain and there are so 
many conditions that contribute to our death. Our body is very fragile and is not stable.  
 
Verse 4 reminds us of how to take this opportun ity since we have already obtained this human 
life endowed with leisures and opportunities. We  should not leave it empty handed or not doing 
anything with it. If we do not practice this te aching, then this whole life is empty and without 
any meaning after we die. If we do think about this from different perspectives we understand 
how special is the opportunity that we are ha ving now as a human life endowed with eight 
leisures and ten opportunities, which is very diffi cult to find. On top of this, we have met with 
the teaching of the Buddha, we have also met with the Mahayana teaching and many teachers 
who are able to show us the prac tices that relate to things that  we need to abandon and things 
that we need to cultivate. We must move our mind in the direction of the dharma after having these conditions; at least we should start the practice of dharma from now onwards. However 
small the virtue might seem, we should start doing it now.  We should investigate into when we spend our life  in meaningless or in non-dharmic ways. If we 
find that we are wasting our time by not practici ng the teachings, we sh ould then remember to 
recite this verse to remind ourselves of the opportunities that we have now and encourage 
ourselves to do the dharma practice.   Going by the outline on the commentary composed by Gyaltsab Je, this particular verse 4 falls 
into the outline: Encouraging [the reader] to ta ke the essence from this basis of leisures and 
endowments. There are also different commentar ies on this text and different commentaries 
have different outlines. Some commentaries explain this verse from the perspective of it being a
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
16 suitable basis for developing bodhicitta, that human rebirth is the best basis for developing 
bodhicitta. Based on the Buddhist cosmology that de scribes  the four continents, it is said that 
human rebirth in the southern continent Jambudvipa (which we are in) is th e best basis of all the 
human rebirths in the four continents.  We have  to understand that we have now achieved the 
basis and the best basis for developing bodhicitta.

3B2B 1A1 Instructions on the reasoning behind abandoning negativities and practicing virtue 
[1,5] 
Just as a flash of lightning on a dark, cloudy night 
For an instant brightly illuminates all, 
Likewise in this world, through the might of Buddha, 
A wholesome thought rarely  and briefly appears. 
 
There are three things shown in verse 5: the practi ce of refraining from negativities, the practice 
of accomplishing virtue, the practice of  subduing or restraining one’s mind  
 “Just as a flash of lightning on a dark, cloudy night”  – night, as opposed to day; dark night  refers 
to the night when we don’t see the moon and th e sky is covered with clouds. When there is a 
flash of lightning in such a night, in that inst ant, one might be able to see the forms of the 
buildings that are being illuminated by the lightn ing. The night refers to  the time where the sun 
of the Buddha’s wisdom is not shining. Dark refe rs to the ignorance rega rding what is to be 
abandoned and what is to be cultivated. Clouds that obscure the sky is like the three mental 
poisons in our mind.  This is an analogy that describes the situation th at we are in. Our mind is obscured by the three 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
17 mental poisons and is ignorant of the practices to be abandoned and what is to be cultivated. 
Sometimes, but rarely, due to the prayers made by  the buddhas when they develop bodhicitta for 
the benefit of sentient beings combined with the merit we have accumulated in the past, a 
wholesome thought does appear in our mind. So metimes, the wish to engage in virtue or 
aspiration does arise in our mind. This kind of rare occurrence of virtuous  thought or aspiration 
for virtue that arises in our mind is brief and tran sient; like the flash of lightning that appears in 
the sky in the dark cloudy night that illuminate s the environment for a br ief instant. When this 
kind of virtuous thought does arise in our mind, it is not going to last as it will invariably fade 
even if we do generate a strong powerful thought of virtue. So it is important that we seize this 
opportunity to practice the dharma teaching wh en virtuous thoughts arise in our mind, as 
virtuous thought rarely occur. This  is what has happened to us - we rarely entertain the virtuous 
thought that arises in our mind and it does not last for a long  time when it does arise.  
 
At times we may have some aspiration for virtue but we just ignore it and postpone it until the 
aspiration starts to disappear as time goes on. We should not allow this to happen as it is rare for us to generate any virtuous thought and when it  does arise, it does not last. We need to 
investigate by looking into our own mind and ex amine what we are doing with our body, speech 
and mind in each and every day and night, and count the number of virtuous thoughts and the 
number of negative thoughts that arise. Needle ss to say, we probably have more non-virtuous 
thoughts. Even when a seemingly virtuous thou ght arises, we need to examine whether this 
virtuous thought in reality is pure in nature or not. As we are always under the control of self 
cherishing thoughts and the eight worldly dharmas, it is difficult to see some sort of virtue in 
whatever things we do everyday. This verse is desc ribing the situation that we are in; it is like 
someone walking around in the dark cloudy night.  When we are doing our prayers or even when 
in retreat, if we look for virtuous states of mind, we will find them very rare.  
When we are talking about virtue; virtuous action s, virtuous thoughts, dharma practices- what 
makes something that is virtue or what makes an action that is dhar ma? If that virtuous action or 
virtuous thought is an antidote, either partially or  fully, to the afflictions in our mind or negative 
emotions or if that action will benefit us in our future life, then this part icular thought or action 
can be considered as virtue. Other than that it is not virtue. When this  virtuous thought does 
arise in our mind, the first thing is to seize it and not let it degenerate. We should try to do 
something to enhance it.  According to Gyaltsab Je’s commentary for this te xt, the commentary that we are using, the first 
five verses are summarizing the following: abando ning negative actions, accumulating virtue and 
subduing the mind. In some commentaries, verse 5 is placed under the he ading: the mind basis 
for developing bodhicitta. The previous verse de scribes how a human life endowed with leisures 
and opportunities is the best rebirth on a physical  basis for developing bo dhicitta. Verse 5, being 
placed under the outline: the mind basis for de veloping bodhicitta, mean s that before we can 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
18 develop bodhicitta, our mind must be in a virtuo us state. On this basis, we can develop the 
altruistic intention of this bodhicitta.  
 
These verses are saying that we have both suitable body and mind for developing bodhicitta. We 
have obtained a human life endowed with leisur es and opportunities whic h is the supreme basis 
f o r  d e v e l o p i n g  b o d h i c i t t a .  W e  a l s o  h a v e  a  s u i t a b l e  b a s i s  o f  m i n d  t h a t  c a n  b e  u s e d  t o  d e v e l o p  bodhicitta because virtue (in the form of virtuous  thought or aspiration of virtue) does arise in 
o u r  m i n d ;  e v e n  t h o u g h  i t  i s  r a r e .  T h e r e f o r e  w e  h a v e  a  s u i t a b l e  b a s i s  o f  b o d y  a n d  m i n d  f o r  developing bodhicitta. After obtaining such a wo nderful basis, we should strive to develop 
bodhicitta and train in the six pe rfections. By doing this, we will achieve enlightenment soon.  
 As a human being it is already hard enough to generate virtuous though t which is completely 
pure in nature; it is very rare. We will be finished if we were to be born as an animal or hungry g h o s t  o r  i n  h e l l  a s  i t  i s  a l m o s t  im p o s s i b l e  t o  develop bodhicitta in the lower realms. At times

difficult to generate any virtuous thoughts as th e suffering would be more intense, hundreds and 
thousands of times more than the suffering that we have when we are sick in the human realm.

3B2B 1A2A 1A Destroying great negativities 
[1,6] 
Hence virtue is perpetually feeble, 
The great strength of immorali ty being extremely intense, 
And except for a Fully Awakening Mind 
By what other virtue will it be overcome? 
 
Verse 6 talks about the power of  bodhicitta. Once it is generated, it has the potential to destroy 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
19 or purify those powerful negative actions done in the past.  
 
“Hence virtue is perpetually feeble”, this is becaus e virtue occurs rarely. If it does occur, it will 
not last for a long time. The negativities that we  have accumulated are very powerful as shown in 
“The great strength of immorality  being extremely intense”. The nega tive action is likened to the 
dark cloudy night which is strong and powerful that can throw us in to the lower realm. It is very 
difficult to stop these.  
 If we were to develop bodhicitta-the altruistic in tention to attain enlightenment for the benefit of 
all sentient beings, it can overcome the powerful  negative actions that were accumulated in the 
past. Thus bodhicitta is likened to the sun and wh en the sun rises, it will dispel the darkness in 
the sky. Except for bodhicitta, by  what other virtue will all th ese negativities be completely 
overwhelmed or suppressed? Only bodhicitta can.   From verse 6 onward, we are talking about the bene fit of bodhicitta. Bodhicitta can even purify 
all those powerful negativities resulting from th e negative actions that been accumulated in the 
past. Once we develop this bodhicitta, not only can the negativities accumulated in the past be 
purified,  future rebirth in the lower realm will be  cut off as well. There are many ways to practice 
to purify negativities that were accumulated in the past, but in this context,  bodhicitta is the most 
powerful antidote.   “ A n d  e x c e p t  f o r  a  F u l l y  A w a k e n i n g  M i n d ,  B y  w h a t  o t h e r  v i r t u e  w i l l  i t  b e  o v e r c o m e ? ”  –  T h i s  means that of all the different type of methods to purify nega tivities, developing bodhicitta 
should be the principal method. 
3B2B 1A2A 1B The potential to accomplish supreme bliss 
[1,7] 
All the Buddhas who have cont emplated for many aeons 
Have seen it to be beneficial; 
For by it the limitless masses of beings 
Will quickly attain the supreme state of bliss. 
 Verse 7 describes that once we develop bodhicitta , we can for sure accomplish the supreme state 
of bliss (full enlightenment).   “All the buddhas” refers to all those who have co mpleted the accumulation of two collections of 
merit and wisdom over the three great countless eo ns. All this was done with the sole intention 
of benefiting living beings by giving them te mporal and ultimate happiness. These buddhas had 
thought about it for a long time and had seen that it  is only bodhicitta that will enable anyone to 
really achieve the goal of benefiting the limitless masses of sentient beings. It is bodhicitta that 
will cause one to lead all these sentient beings to the supreme state of bliss, the state of full 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
20 enlightenment. When we develop bodhicitta, we are able to take care of the welfare of the 
limitless number of sentient beings. In fact, it is  basically bodhicitta that enables anyone to do 
that. The solitary realizers and hearers that en ter the path cannot work for the benefit of all 
sentient beings. It is only the bodhisattvas wh o can work for the limitless number of sentient 
beings. What makes the bodhisattvas able to do th is? It is bodhicitta. As we can see, people who 
have a good heart, who have pure intentions, can benefit more sentient beings and even animals. 
Bodhicitta is the state of mind that works for the limitless number of sentient beings. If we were 
to develop the bodhicitta mind, we definitely ca n achieve this goal. Whoever develops bodhicitta 
will be able to work for the benefit of the limitle ss number of sentient beings and be able to lead 
all sentient beings to the state of full enlightenm ent – this is one benefit of bodhicitta and the 
essence of this verse. 
3B2B 1A2A 1C The potential to accomplish whatever one wishes 
[1,8] 
Those who wish to destroy the many sorrows of (their) conditioned existence, 
Those who wish (all beings) to ex perience a multitude of joys, 
And those who wish to experience much happiness 
Should never forsake the Awakening Mind. 
 
If we were to develop bodhicitta, we will be able to destroy the suffering that is found in samsara; birth, aging, sickness and death. If we have bodhicitta, we will be  able to eliminate the suffering 
that sentient beings are experiencing in this life and in their future lives. We will also be able to help all sentient beings to achieve all the diffe rent kinds of joy and bliss. Therefore those who 
wish this to happen should ne ver give up on bodhicitta. 
 Verse 8 is saying that if we want all our wishes to be fulfilled and al so want to fulfill all the wishes 
of living beings, then we should never forsake bodhicitta. We should de velop bodhicitta and it 
will be wonderful if we are able to do so. In short, if we have bodhic itta, we will accomplish 
whatever we wish.

[1,9] 
The moment an Awakening Mind arises 
In those fettered and weak in the jail of cyclic existence, 
They will be named a 'Child of the Sugatas', 
And will be revered by both humans and gods of the world. 
 Regardless of whether you are female or male, old or young, rich or poor, once you have develop 
bodhicitta, you will be called a “child of the su gatas’ or bodhisattva (attaining the special name). 
Upon generation of bodhicitta in one’s mind, on e will be revered and respected by both humans 
and gods of the world (attaining the special purp ose). The bodhisattva is a worthy object of

21 veneration and worship even by the buddhas themse lves. This is because bodhicitta is like the

respected and honored by people. Needless to say that those who develop bo dhicitta will become 
a suitable object of veneration even by the bu ddhas themselves because a bodhisattva is someone 
who possess bodhicitta, the mind of enlightenment which seeks en lightenment for the benefit of 
all sentient beings without exception.

3B2B 1A2A 3A An analogy of the inferior becoming the supreme 
[1,10] 
It is like the supreme gold-making elixir, 
For it transforms the unclean body we have taken 
Into the priceless jewel of a Buddha-Form. 
Therefore firmly seize this Awakening Mind. 
 
Like an elixir that has the power to transform ba se metal into gold, bodhicitta can transform this 
contaminated body of ours into the form body of the Buddha. If we have bodhicitta, we will be able to achieve the unsurpassed state of full enli ghtenment. On the basis of this human body, we 
can develop bodhicitta. If we medita te on the instruction, we will be able to achieve the state of 
full enlightenment. Hence this analogy is called  “the analogy of the inferior becoming the 
supreme”. “Therefore firmly seize this Awakening Mind” - hold on to it in a stable way without 
giving up. In another commentary, this verse falls under the headline called “Showing the 
achievement of Buddhahood through the analogy of th e gold-making elixir”. If we have the elixir 
of bodhicitta, we can even transform our current st ate of body into the state of full and complete 
enlightenment.

For it transforms the unclean body we have taken 
Into the priceless jewel of a Buddha-Form.

Like a special elixir that has the power to tr ansform base metal into gold, bodhicitta can 
transform this unclean body of ours into the form body of the Buddha. If we have bodhicitta, we 
will be able to achieve the unsurpassed state of full enlightenment. On the basis of this human 
body, we can develop bodhicitta. If we meditate on  the instruction, we will be able to achieve the 
state of full enlightenment. Hence this analogy is called “the analogy of the inferior becoming the 
supreme”. “Therefore firmly seize this awakening mind” advises us to firmly hold on to this 
awakening mind - bodhicitta.  It is not very clear in the commentary of this verse how bodhicitta, when likened to the supreme 
gold-making elixir, can transform the unclean body of ours into the form body of a buddha. This 
verse is literally saying that bodhicitta can tran sform this unclean body of ours into the form 
body of a buddha; whether this can happen in reality or not, we need to check out.  
 In the tantric teaching of the Buddha, it does explain that there are methods to transform our 
impure body into the pure body of a buddha. If we look at it from the viewpoint that “through the power of generating bodhicitta, we will ac hieve the form body of a buddha”, then there 
should not be any problem. On the basis of taking  on the unclean body of ours and on the basis 
of this rebirth, through the forc e of generating bodhicitta, we wi ll be able to achieve the form 
body of the Buddha in the future. It is definitely alright to say this.  In another commentary, this verse is placed unde r the headline called “Showing the achievement 
of buddhahood through the analogy of supreme gold -making elixir”. If we have the elixir of 
bodhichitta, we can even transform our current stat e of body into the state of full and complete 
enlightenment.

23 3B2B 1A2A 3B An analogy of the diffic ulty in obtaining it and its great value 
[1,11] 
Since the limitless mind of the Sole Guide of the World 
Has upon thorough investigati on seen its preciousness, 
All beings wishing to be free from worldly abodes 
Should firmly take hold of this precious Awakening Mind. 
 
Imagine that there is a skillful trader, whose only thought is  benefiting others, acting as a guide 
leading a group of people to an island to find  precious gold and gems . Upon finding the gold 
and gems, this group of people will cherish it.  The trader is likened to the Buddha, who is endo wed with intelligence and with the motivation 
to benefit others, has skillful means and benefits  sentient beings through many activities. The 
Buddha, as sole guide of the world, with his inte lligence, has seen that the best means or method 
to benefit all the destitute and suffering sentient beings is this precious  bodhicitta. The Buddha, 
having seen for himself that it is bodhicitta that  can accomplish all the purposes and wishes of all 
the sentient beings, taught this to all his disciple s. Therefore it says that one should firmly take 
hold of this bodhicitta, cherish it and never give it up. 
3B2B 1A2A 3C An analogy of an inexhaustible effect and [an effect that] is magnified 
[1,12] 
All other virtues are like plantain trees; 
For after bearing fruit, they simply perish. 
Yet the perennial tree of the Awakening Mind 
Unceasingly bears fruit and th ereby flourishes without end 
 In this analogy, bodhicitta is likened to the pe rennial tree  which always bears fruit unceasingly; 
unlike the plantain tree which only bears fruit once. We can understand this by looking at it 
from the perspectives of the three types of pe rsons with different spiritual capacities:   
• The motivation of the person of small capacity in  his dharma practice is to achieve the higher 
rebirth of human and god. Once he has achieved  his goal, all those actions upon bearing fruit 
will cease to give rise to effects again in the future. 
• The motivation of the person of  medium capa city in his dharma practice is to achieve 
liberation from  cyclic existence. Upon achievin g that goal, all the virtues that the person has 
accumulated in the past cease to give rise to effects again in the future.  
• The motivation of the person of great capaci ty in his dharma practice is to achieve 
enlightenment for the benefit of all sentient beings. Thus the actions that he performs 
towards achieving this goal will continuously give rise to positive effects until enlightenment is achieved.  
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
24 There is an example given in sutra, an analogy of a medicinal plant. If  the medicinal plant is 
supported by the four elements in the correct pr oportions, the medicinal plant will continuously 
give rise to fruits. In the same way, if one dedi cates one’s virtue towards achieving enlightenment, 
that virtue will give rise to positive effects until full enlightenment is achieved. Another analogy 
given in sutra: if a drop of water is added to th e ocean, the drop of water will not dry up until the 
ocean itself dries up. In the same way, if one dedicates one’s action towards enlightenment, the 
effects of that action will never cease. 
  Verse 12 says that all roots of virtue that are done with bodhicitta moti vation will continuously 
give rise to positive effects until the state of full enlightenment is achieved. At the end of any 
particular virtuous action, if one dedicates the virtue of that action to the state of full 
enlightenment, then those roots of virtue will ne ver be exhausted and will give rise to effects 
continuously and unceasingly till the state of full enlightenment.  It is very important to always dedicate one’s  root of virtue or merit towards achieving 
enlightenment for the benefit of sentient beings . Of all the kinds of dedication that one can 
make, this (i.e. dedicating towards full enlighte nment) is the supreme dedication as it covers 
every type of dedication including extensive dedications.  Verse 12 seems to be saying that if we do something beneficial in helping others in this life but  do not dedicate it, then whether this action will benefit us in our future lives or not remains to 
be seen. If we dedicate it for the sake of future rebirths, then it will only ripen for that purpose, 
i.e. something good will happen in future lives. If  we dedicate whatever virtuous actions we have 
done for our attainment of enlightenment, the effects of the actions will be seen right until 
enlightenment is reached. It seems that it is the motivation and the dedication that determine 
the result of a particular action. It’s the same with making or using nuclear energy or nuclear 
weapons – the effects of these actions depend on one’s motivation and one’s dedication. If we do 
an act that is motivated by bodhicitta and at the end we dedicate the root of virtue of the action 
towards achieving enlightenment, then until enli ghtenment is achieved, the root of virtue will 
continuously give rise to beneficial effects like  the perennial trees that bear fruit continuously; 
not like the plantain tree such as the banana tree which bears fruit only once.  Bodhicitta is likened to perennial trees in that th e virtue that is accumulated with the motivation 
of bodhicitta continually bears fruit and does not stop giving rise to beneficial effects. 
3B2B 1A2A 3D An analogy of the power for being protected from great fear 
[1,13] 
Like entrusting myself to a brave man when greatly afraid 
By entrusting myself to this (Awakeni ng Mind) I shall be swiftly liberated 
Even if I have committed extremely unbearable wrongs. 
Why then do the conscientious no t devote themse lves to this? 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
25  
Verse 13 says that bodhicitta is so powerful that even if one has committed very powerful 
negative actions; just by generating bodhicitta a ll these negative actions can be purified. Basically 
we can entrust ourselves to bodhicitta, like when we are afraid, we look for refuge and entrust ourselves to a brave man or powerful person for protection. Even if we have accumulated 
negativities that can throw us into the lower real ms, if we entrust ourselves to take refuge in 
bodhicitta we will be saved from the lower realms.  Verse 14 has two outlines: 
[1,14ab] 
Just like the fire at the end of an age, 
It instantly consumes all great wrongdoing. 
3B2B 1A2A 3E An analogy of destroying negativities without remainder 
According to the Buddhist cosmol ogy, at the end of an age when the world would be destroyed, 
destruction happens in stages – first, the world wi ll be attacked by fire, then water, followed by 
wind. At the beginning of the force of destruction during the end of this world, seven suns will 
arise and will burn up everything in samsara up to the form realm of the first concentration - the 
realm, the environment and the beings there will be completely destroyed. The form realm of the 
second concentration will be destroyed by water.  The form realm of the third concentration will 
be destroyed by wind. The form realm of the fourth concentration and the remaining four 
realms of the formless realm will remain as they are.   Just like the fiery conflagration at the end of this age that will burn everything in the form realm  
of the 1st concentration with no remainder, when one generates bodhicitta, the generated 
bodhicitta will consume all the great wrong doings  that includes all the negativities that will 
throw us into the lower realms.  There are different classification s of karma or actions – there is karma whose effects will be 
experienced very quickly and there is karma whose effects will not be experienced that quickly.  
By generating bodhicitta, it can even completely purify those powerful negativities that can give 
rise to effects very quickly.  This verse says that when we generate bodhicitta , even those powerful negativities that can give 
rise to effects very quickly will be completely purified. (To completely purify a negative action means that one will not experience the fruitional re sult of the negative action, i.e. one will not be 
born in the lower realm.) Therefore this verse is saying that even the five heinous actions (also 
called the five acts of immediate retribution) ca n be completely purified by the generation of 
bodhicitta.    Qualm: But aren’t the five acts of immediate retribution supposed to be actions whose effects 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
26 one will definitely experience? The effects or results here refer to the fruitional result, i.e. rebirth 
in the lower realms. Since one definitely has to  experience the fruitional result of the five 
heinous actions, how can we say that bodhicitta ca n completely purify these five heinous actions? 
 Reply: If we answer in this way, then there is no problem: If your generation of bodhicitta has not purified those five acts of immediate retribut ion, then you will definitely have to experience 
the effects. In other words, whet her it can be purified or not de pends on the strength of your 
bodhicitta.  These lines “Just like the fire at the end of an age, It instantly consumes all great wrongdoing.” 
are saying that even those heavy negative actions whose results one will definitely experience, can 
be purified by a single instant of bodhicitta.  Is there any significant difference between verse 13 and verse 14?  Verse 13 says that if one entrusts oneself to bodhicitta, one will be able to be liberated from the 
heavy negative actions. Verse 14 says that if one generates bodhicitta, one will purify all the heavy 
negative actions.  The analogy of verse 13 is that of entrusting onesel f to a brave man when one is afraid. Here it is 
talking about how bodhicitta overwhelms the fear of  having rebirths in the lower realms. It is not 
talking about bodhicitta destroying the fear or thoroughly eradicating the fear of going to the 
lower realms.  The analogy of verse 14 is that of  the fire at the end of the age instantly consuming everything in 
its path. By generating bodhicitta, it will instantly purify all those negative actions. This refers to 
the differences in the strength of  bodhicitta. Since bodhicitta on ce generated can be increased, 
this means that some bodhicitta can be more  powerful than others. There are cases where 
bodhicitta in some people’s minds is so powerful  that it can completely destroy the results of  
negative actions.  In some sutras, there is an analogy of lighting  up the butter lamp that instantly dispels the 
darkness of a pitch dark house. In the same w ay, when one generates powerful bodhicitta, one 
can instantly purify those negativities accumu lated over one thousand eons including those 
negative actions that can throw one into the lower realms and the five acts of immediate 
retribution.

To the disciple Sudhana by the wise Lord Maitreya.

This refers to what Lord Maitreya taught the wise disciple Sudhana in the Array of Stalks Sutra . In 
this sutra, bodhicitta is likened to the seed of all the buddha qualities: 
  
O Noble Son, the awakening mind is  like the seed of all the buddha s’ qualities. Since it increases 
the wholesome qualities of all beings it is like a fi eld; since it supports each and every creature, it is 
like the earth; since it perfectly severs all povert y, it is like the god of  wealth; since it affords 
complete protection to all bodhisattvas, it is like a father; since it perfectly accomplishes all aims, it 
is like the king of wish-fulfilling gems; since it completely fulfills all intentions, it is like an 
auspicious vase...O Noble Son, the awakening mind  is endowed with such excellent virtues as 
these as well as limitless other benefits. 
3B2B 1A2B Identifying the nature of bodhicitta 
1 Divisions of the nature 
2 Explaining the divisions by way of analogy 
3 Explaining the different benefits of aspiring and engaging bodhicitta 
3B2B 1A2B 1 Divisions of the nature (entity) 
[1,15] 
In brief, the Awakening Mind 
Should be understood to be of two types; 
The mind that aspires to awaken 
And the mind that ventures to do so. 
 
The entity of bodhicitta is divided into two:  the mind that aspires to awaken – aspiring 
bodhicitta, and the mind that ventures  to do so – engaging bodhicitta 
 Bodhicitta is the main mind that is accompanied by the mental fact or of aspiration that seeks to 
attain the state of full enlightenm ent for the benefit of all sentient beings. The mental factor that 
aspires to achieve enlightenment for others is the mental factor of aspiration; this mental factor 
of aspiration accompanies a main mind, and this main mind is called bodh icitta. Bodhicitta is 
sometimes referred to as an aspiration; technicall y bodhicitta is not a mental factor but is the 
main mind. 
[1,16] 
As is understood by the distinction 
Between aspiring to go and (actually) going, 
So the wise understand in turn 
The distinction between these two 
3B2B 1A2B 2 Explaining the divisions by way of analogy 
Aspiring bodhicitta is likened to wishing to go to  the destination. Engaging bodhicitta is likened 
to the actual going. The distinction between as piring bodhicitta and en gaging bodhicitta is 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
28 whether that aspiration is conjoined with the prac tice of the bodhisattva deeds (the practice of 
the six perfections) or not. Engagi ng bodhicitta is conjoined with an action, it is conjoined with 
the practice of the six perfections.  
 Both aspirational and engaging bodhicitta are main minds.  
3B2B 1A2B 3 Explaining the different bene fits of aspiring and engaging bodhicitta 
A The benefits of aspiring bodhicitta 
B The benefits of engaging bodhicitta 
3B2B 1A2B 3A The benefits of aspiring bodhicitta 
[1,17] 
Although great fruits occur in cyclic existence 
From the mind that aspires to awaken, 
An uninterrupted flow of merit does not ensue 
As it does with the venturing mind. 
 
Qualm: There is no benefit at all just to generate  aspiring bodhicitta. This is a waste of time if 
not conjoined with the practice of the six perfections.  This verse is saying that there are many benefits ju st by generating aspiring bodhicitta. It is true 
that by just generating aspiring bodhicitta, it is not conjoined with the practice of six perfections. 
But since one has generated the aspirating bodhici tta, it is a cause for enlightenment.  One can 
also derive temporal benefits of higher rebi rth of human and god. One can also eradicate 
samsaric poverty.  Since there are such benefits by generating aspiri ng bodhicitta alone, obviously the benefits by 
generating engaging bodhicitta conjoined with the practice of the six perfections would be 
d i f f e r e n t  a s  s h o w n  i n  “ A n  u n i n t e r r u p t e d  f l o w  o f  m e r i t  d o e s  n o t  e n s u e ,  a s  i t  d o e s  w i t h  t h e  venturing mind”.   
In the Array of Stalks Sutra , there is a quotation mentioning that bodhicitta is likened to a 
diamond. Even a small broken pi ece of diamond can surpass other ornaments such as gold, silver 
etc for eradicating poverty. Similarly, just by generating aspiring bodhicitta alone, one will 
overwhelm the qualities of the solitary realizer and the hearer although they do possess many 
qualities. This aspiring bodhicitta can he lp one to overcome poverty in samsara.  
 In the Advice to the King Sutra , the king, who was very busy with his duties and had no time to 
practice the six perfections, was told that it was enough for him to just generate aspiring 
bodhicitta. This king was advised to always reme mber bodhicitta whether he was walking, sitting 
down, eating etc and to always hold it in his mind.

29 Lesson 5 – chapter 1, verse 17-26 
[1,4] 
Leisure and endowment are very hard to find 
And, since they accomplish what is meaningful for humanity, 
If I do not take advantage of them now, 
How will such a perfect opportunity come about again? 
 
[1,5] 
Just as a flash of lightning on a dark, cloudy night 
For an instant brightly illuminates all, 
Likewise in this world, through the might of Buddha, 
A wholesome thought rarely  and briefly appears. 
 
We should contemplate  the meaning of these two verses over and over ag ain. At times when we 
are free, we can recite these two verses and spen d some time reflecting on the meaning of these 
two verses.   Our state of mind is basically as described in verse 5.  It is so rare and difficult for us to generate a 
positive state of virtuous mind. But this does no t mean that it will not happen. When it does 
happen, we must seize on it and engage in virtue.   This is quite obvious if we examine our own situat ion. It is very rare and difficult for us to 
generate any virtuous thought or engage in any vi rtue. By looking at our own mind, we can safely 
say that what we are really concerned about in our daily lives is bread and butter issues, and we 
work solely for the benefit of the happiness of this life. This is basically what we are working for. 
It is very rare for us to have any intention or the wish to engage in any kind of dharma practice 
or virtue.  
 On top of that, it is even rarer for us to develop some sort of intense yearning or aspiration to engage in the study of the profound teaching of the Buddha. The fact that  we produce some sort 
of aspiration or yearning of wanting to study th e great treatises and teachings of the Buddha is 
something that has happened thro ugh the power of blessing of th e Buddha that has entered into 
our continuum, as stated in verse 5.  But it is not due to this reason alone.  Obvi ously we have accumulated the karma and merit in 
our past lives, which when combined with the ble ssing of the Buddha, results in our having such 
an aspiration now. If we think about it, this is something that seems to be quite impossible. So it 
is kind of impossible that we have the wish or as piration to study the great treatises and difficult 
texts. But it has happened.  We should not leave it alone.

30 It is not sufficient just to develop the aspiration once. We must keep the aspiration going and

studying this text is difficult. Because of that and if we are not able to sustain this aspiration, we 
might give up our study. So it is important for us to sustain our aspiration to continue our study.  
 Practicing the dharma is difficult, especially tr ying to study and reflect on the great texts and 
treatises. The main thing is not to give up as it will be a waste. Reflecting on verses 4 and 5, we 
should set a long term goal and think of achievin g happiness in the future, especially our future

the story of the pigeon and Vasubhandu. The pi geon used to sit on top of the roof of 
Vasubhandu’s house. Vasubandhu, who was a grea t scholar of the  Abhidharma (the text on 
knowledge) used to recite the Abhidharma texts from his memory. Just by hearing Vasubhandu’s recitation, the pigeon was reborn as a huma n and later also became a great scholar. 
 W e  a r e  b e t t e r  t h a n  t h e  p i g e o n  a s  w e  n o t  o n l y  l i sten to the teachings but we are also able to

opportunity to continue to study the great treatise s. The fact that we have the chance to study  
this great text that teaches bodhicitta is defi nitely due to the result of having accumulated 
virtuous karma in the past. We should rejoice about this.   Verse 4 is talking about how rare and difficult it is for us to obtain a human life of leisure and 
opportunity. It is also implicitly  stated that once we achieve such  leisure and opportunity, we can 
achieve great purposes and higher goals with them . At the same time we have to recognize the 
f a c t  t h a t  i t  i s  n o t  e a s y  t o  g e n e r a t e  v i r t u o u s  t h o u g h t s ,  a s  s t a t e d  i n  v e r s e  5 .  W h e n  a  v i r t u o u s  
thought does arise in our mind or when the mind is in the virtuous state, we should seize the

A human life endowed with leisure and opportunity is  difficult to find and once found is able to 
fulfill great purposes. As virtuous thoughts rarely  arise in our mind, engaging in virtue is very 
difficult. Even if a virtuous thought does arise, it  is very weak and does not last for a long time. 
The negativities that we have ac cumulated in the past are very po werful. In order to overcome all 
these negativities, we need to generate equally st rong or even stronger antidotes to counter the
