3B2B 1A2B 3 Explaining the different bene fits of aspiring and engaging bodhicitta 
A The benefits of aspiring bodhicitta 
B The benefits of engaging bodhicitta 
 
When bodhicitta is divided, then there are two: aspiring bodhicitta or aspirational bodhicitta, 
and engaging bodhicitta or engaged bodhicitta. 
 The distinction between aspirational bodhicitta an d engaged bodhicitta is made on the basis of 
whether the mind generation of bodhicitta is conjoined with the practi ce of the bodhisattva 
deeds (the practice of the six perfections) or not. 
3B2B 1A2B 3B The benefits of engaging bodhicitta 
[1,18] 
And for those who have perfectly seized this mind, 
With the thought never to turn away 
From totally liberating 
The infinite forms of life, 
 
[1,19] 
From that time hence, 
Even while asleep or unconcerned, 
A force of merit equal to the sky 
Will perpetually ensue. 
 
If we adopt engaged bodhicitta and generate this in our mind – taking on the promise to liberate 
an infinite number of sentient beings from th e miseries and suffering of cyclic existence and 
place them in the state of unsurpassed and fully co mplete enlightenment - if we hold on to this 
form of bodhicitta, then it is  said that inconceivable and immeasurable benefits will follow. 
 
Besides merely wishing to attain enlightenment for the benefit of sentient beings, if one promises 
to train in the six perfections or bodhisattva deeds, one will accumulate immeasurable merit 
continually in each and every single instant whet her we are falling asleep, sitting, standing, lying 
down or whatever we are doing. On top of the aspirational bodhicitta, if our mind generation of 
bodhicitta is conjoined with the practice of the bodhisattva deeds, then all our virtue will be 
much more powerful and we will  continually coll ect virtue without a break and those virtues will 
also increase.   The difference in benefits between aspirational bodhicitta and engaging bodhicitta is explained 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
32 in the last two lines of verse 17: 
 
[1,17cd] 
An uninterrupted flow of merit does not ensue, 
As it does with the venturing mind 
 The  “venturing mind” is engaging bodhicitta. As great as these benefits are, however, an 
uninterrupted flow of merit does not arise from th e aspiring bodhicitta the way it does from the 
engaging bodhicitta. The benefit of having engaging  bodhicitta is that we continually accumulate 
virtue in each and every single instant and thos e virtues will also increase. What is the reason 
that there are such benefits th at can be derived from generati ng bodhicitta?  The reasons are 
mentioned in the following outlines. 
3B2B 1A2C The reasons there are such benefits 
1 Establishing [the benefits]by quoting scripture 
2 Establishing [the benefits] by means of logic and reasoning 
3B2B 1A2C 1 Establishing [the benefits] by quoting scripture 
The benefits of bodhicitta as established through scriptural authority, are explained in verse 20. 
 
[1,20] 
For the sake of those inclined towards the lesser (vehicle), 
This was logically asserted 
By the Tathagata himself 
In the Sutra Requested by Subahu  
 
The quotation from the  Subahupariprccha Sutra reads: 
 
If one perseveres for the sake of bringing happin ess and benefit to an infinite number of beings, 
then the infinite roots of virtue from this infini te aim that bears in mind the happiness and benefit 
of all sentient beings shall incr ease, expand and reach towards fulfilment every moment, day and 
night, regardless of whether one is unconscientious or even asleep. 
 
This quotation from the Buddha is specifically made to a person of indefinite Hearer lineage (of indefinite lineage means those who can change th eir lineage) and also for those who are afraid 
when they look at what they have to do in or der to achieve enlightenment; such as having to 
accumulate the two collections of merit and wisdom  for an inconceivable period of time. Because 
of this, they may fall into the Hinayana path. Th e Buddha talked about the benefits of bodhicitta 
to these types of persons to prevent them from fa lling into the Hinayana path due to the fact that 
they are afraid of having to sp end so much time finishing the a ccumulations. In this sutra, the 
Buddha was saying that when we generate engagi ng bodhicitta, whatever we are doing, whether 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
33 sleeping, lying down, working etc, we will accumulate merit continually without a break. So by 
generating engaging bodhicitta, there is no need to be discouraged or be disheartened about the 
time needed to finish the two accumulations.  
3B2B 1A2C 2 Establishing [the benefits] by means of logic and reasoning 
A The validity of obtaining benefits from aspiring bodhicitta 
B The validity of obtaining benefits from engaging bodhicitta 
3B2B 1A2C 2A The validity of obtai ning benefits from aspiring bodhicitta 
1 As the functions of accomplishing virtue are limitless, the benefits are great 2 There is no altruistic intenti on that surpasses [aspiring bodhicitta] 
3 How could such an intention to benefit sentie nt beings not bring about benefit for oneself as 
well? 4 Conclusion and praise 
3B2B 1A2C 2A1 As the functions of accomplishing virtue are limitless, the benefits are great 
The function of accomplishing virtue is limitless.  Since the perspective is big, the benefits are 
great which are covered in verses 21 and 22. (Wha t we are aiming for is something very big, 
therefore the benefit of having such a wide perspective is also great.) 
 
[1,21] 
If even the thought to relieve 
Living creatures of merely a headache 
Is a beneficial intention 
Endowed with infinite goodness, 
 
[1,22] 
Then what need is there to mention 
The wish to dispel their inconceivable misery, 
Wishing every single one of them 
To realize boundless good qualities? 
 
Verse 21 is saying that even the beneficial inte ntion to relieve sentient  beings from just a 
headache, just by generating this thought “may he be relieved from that headache”,  will produce infinite goodness. Of course it goes without saying  that if we wish all sentient beings be happy 
and  free from all suffering, it will produce greater goodness.    If merely having the wish to relieve one sentient  being of a particular sickness itself will bring 
about so much benefits and merits, then it goes without saying that when we wish for infinite 
countless sentient beings to be free from all phys ical and mental suffering or whatever miseries, 
and also want them to be endowed with all the qualities of the Buddha, then obviously we do 
not need to talk about the benefit of such a noble ideal and such a noble mind.

3B2B 1A2C 2A2 There is no altruistic intention that surpasses [aspiring bodhicitta] 
[1,23] 
Do even fathers and mothers 
Have such a benevolent intention as this? 
Do the gods and sages? 
Does even Brahma have it? 
 Let us examine the persons who are close to us such as our parents or  loved ones and check 
whether they have such benevolent intention or aspirational bodhicitta. How about the gods of 
the world, sages and those who have clairvoyance - do they have such a benevolent intention? 
Does even Brahma, who was said to have gene rated the four immeasurable thoughts  and 
controlled this world, have this ki nd of bodhicitta? This verse is saying that even the people who 
give us temporary happiness in this world and wh o can help us such as our parents, the gods, 
sages and even Brahma (the samsaric god who ru les the world) do not have this bodhicitta. 
3B2B 1A2C 2A3 How could such an intention to benefit sentient beings not bring about benefit for 
oneself as well? 
[1,24] 
If those beings have never before 
Even dreamt of such an attitude 
For their own sake, 
How would it ever arise for the sake of others? 
 
If we look at verses 23 and 24, verse 23 is qu estioning whether our pa rents, gods, sages and 
Brahma have this bodhicitta and verse 24 gives the answer that they do not have this bodhicitta 
as they never even have it for themselves, so there is no possibility that th ey think of it for other 
people.   What do people such as our parents, gods, sage s, Brahma and even ourselves hold close to our 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
35 hearts or cherish? Basically it is our own purpose.  What do we hold most dear? Basically ourselves 
and  our own purpose. Whatever we do is to fulfill the purpose of self-cherishing and our own 
purpose. Have we ever thought about ac hieving enlightenment for ourselves?  
 
When we are awake, obviously we have not though t about this. This also does not happen in our 
dream, as an object of fantasy. We do not dr eam about achieving enlightenment for ourselves. 
Even when we are awake, we do not plan to ha ve enlightenment for ours elves. This is already 
difficult to do for our own sake. How it is possible to wish, to have that kind of attitude for other 
sentient beings?  So verse 24 is saying how difficult  it is to generate bodhicitta and if somebody does generate 
bodhicitta, then it is an amazing and unbelievab le happening. We all are expert at cherishing 
ourselves and this is what we do all the time. Ev en those who are experts at cherishing themselves 
do not even think of achieving enlightenment fo r themselves, so how could it be possible for 
them to achieve enlightenment for the benefits of others? With this as the reason, therefore, this 
verse says that even fathers and mothers, gods, and sages do not have bo dhicitta. Even such a 
powerful god such as Brahma who is said to cont rol the world does not generate bodhicitta. This 
shows that bodhicitta is not a shared or common quality that can be accomplished by any 
person. It shows that bodhicitta is an un shared quality of the Mahayana vehicle.  
3B2B 1A2C 2A4 Conclusion and praise 
[1,25] 
This intention to benefit all beings, 
Which does not arise in othe rs even for their own sake, 
Is an extraordinary jewel of the mind, 
And its birth is an unprecedented wonder. 
 Such an intention to benefit all beings does not arise in others even for their own sake and even 
for an instant, so this precious mind of bodhicitta is difficult to  generate. It does exist in the 
continuum of bodhisattvas who generate it and ha ve the intention to relieve all the sufferings of 
sentient beings and wish to place all sentient beings in happiness. With  that thought in mind, 
bodhisattvas have the desire to achieve enlighte nment and work to generate its causes. When one 
sees that this mind is generated in the contin uum, it is like an amazing and wonderful thing. 
 If we ourselves happen to produce this kind of  thought in our own mind, then it will be an 
‘unprecedented wonder’ as this has not happened before. Once bodhicitta arises in our mental 
continuum, we should strive to maintain it an d increase it and not let it degenerate. When 
bodhicitta arises in the mental continuum of other living beings, then obviously it is also a 
marvelous and wonderful thing and those beings be come objects of veneration and respect. Since 
we consider it  marvelous and amazing when so mebody generates bodhicitta, we should also 
strive to produce it in our own mind.

36  
Bodhicitta is an extraordinary jewel of the mind; it  is very special. Even if somebody possesses the 
six types of clairvoyance or even  if the person has the wisdom th at directly realizes emptiness, 
these things do not make them  Mahayanist. Whether one ente rs the Mahayana path or not 
depends on whether one has this extraordinary jewel of the mind - bodhicitta. If one has bodhicitta, then one is a Mahayani st. Therefore, bodhicitta is called ‘an extraordinary jewel of 
the mind’. Basically verses 21 to 25 establish the benefits of aspi rational bodhicitta by means of 
reasoning.

If there are so many benefits that can be derive d from generating aspira tional bodhicitta, then 
obviously many more benefits will come by co njoining the aspirational  bodhicitta with the 
practice of the six perfections.  
 When we generate engaging bodhicitta, it is like ned to medicine that relieves the world of pain, 
and it is able to bestow on sentient beings the happiness of humans and gods. If we talk about 
the wonderful benefits of such medicine, one can never stop – there are so many benefits, it is 
immeasurable.  
 
In one of the sutras Buddha ta lks about the benefits of en gaging bodhicitta, saying: “ If the benefits 
of engaging bodhicitta were to take a form, even space wouldn’t be able to contain them ”.

37 Lesson 6 – chapter 1, verse 23-36 (end) 
   [1,23] 
Do even fathers and mothers 
Have such a benevolent intention as this?

This intention to benefit all beings, 
Which does not arise in othe rs even for their own sake, 
Is an extraordinary jewel of the mind, 
And its birth is an unprecedented wonder. 
 
Verses 23 to 25 are saying that this thought of bene fiting all sentient beings is extremely difficult 
to obtain. We know this from our own experience  on how extremely difficult it is to generate 
this kind of thought of wanting to benefit all sentient beings. 
 Look at the people who have benefited us in this  life, especially our parents, who have shown us 
such kindness, taken care of us and given us an education since we  were young. Even generating 
a thought to benefit them is sometimes so difficult . Of course, as it is already difficult even to 
think of benefiting those people who have been  kind to us, what is there to say about how 
difficult it is to generate some thought to bene fit those who have harmed us? Obviously, we don’t 
even have to talk about generating the th ought to benefit all  sentient beings.  
 From this, you can see how extremely  difficult it is to generate this bodh icitta. The teaching is 
not saying that we will be unable to or it is not possible to generate such a mind.  What it is 
saying is that it is an extremely difficult task. But if the mind is trained or one familiarizes oneself 
with this type of training, then it is  possible to generate such a thought.  
 Basically, verses 21 to 25 establish the benefits of  generating aspirational bodhicitta. One should 
have the aspiration to generate this intention to achieve enlightenment for the benefit of all 
sentient beings - “How wonderful it would be if I have bodhicitta. I will generate bodhicitta and I 
will do it by myself. I will put effort in generati ng bodhicitta. Please, Guru Deity, bless me to be 
able to do so.” We should always train our mind in this way.

38  
Generating bodhicitta is an extremely difficult task. Why? This is because we have been under 
the control of the self-cherishin g thought from time without beginning. Without getting rid of 
the self-cherishing thought,  there is no way for us to generate bodhicitta. Of course, bodhicitta is 
the supreme and most powerful anti dote to self-cherishing thought. 
 
[1,26] 
How can I fathom the depths 
Of the goodness of this jewel of the mind, 
The panacea that relieves the world of pain 
And is the source of all its joy? 
 This chapter talks about the bene fits of  bodhicitta. We should  understand the benefits of 
bodhicitta, know its extraordinary qualities. By  understanding the qualities of bodhicitta, one 
will naturally develop an aspiration to generate it  in our own mind. So it is important always to 
aspire and to pray, “How wonderful it would be if I were to generate bodhicitta, I will generate bodhicitta”. It is important to pray in such a w ay. If we generate bodhicitta in our own mental 
continuum, we will attain happiness for ourselves and others will be happy as well. This goes 
without saying. 
3B2B 1A2C 2B The validity of obtaining benefits from engaging bodhicitta 
1 The actual [benefits of engaging bodhicitta] 
2 The validity of those 
3 Dispelling doubts 
3B2B 1A2C 2B2 Establishing the validity through reason 
[1,27] 
If merely a benevolent intention 
Excels veneration of the Buddhas, 
Then what need to me ntion striving to make 
All beings, without exception, happy? 
 
This “benevolent intention” refers to the aspirati onal bodhicitta. Just having this intention to 
achieve enlightenment for the benefit of all sentie nt beings and if one were to produce such a 
thought, the merit that one will accumulate wi ll exceed the merit that one will accumulate by 
making countless offering to billions of buddhas. This is as quoted in the King of Concentrations 
Sutra , “The merit that one accumulates from just generating this bodhicitta exceeds making 
countless offering everyday to billions and billions of buddhas.”   Just by generating this mere in tention to achieve enlightenment fo r the benefit of sentient beings 
would bring forth such an inconceivable amount of  merit. Therefore it goes without saying that 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
39 for someone who puts a lot of effort into engagi ng in the bodhisattva’s deeds on the basis of 
having generated this bodhicitta, obviou sly the merit would be much greater. 
 
In short, the merit that is derived from engaged bodhicitta is far greater than the merit that one 
accumulates just by having aspirational bodhic itta. The difference between  these two is due to 
the practice of engaging in the bodhisattva deeds.  The mere generation of aspirational bodhicitta 
is not conjoined with the practice of the bodh isattva deeds whereas engaged bodhicitta is the 
generation of bodhicitta conjoi ned with the practice of the bo dhisattva deeds. Here one is 
actually putting effort into doing something to move towards the goal of enlightenment. 
Obviously the merit that comes from engaged bo dhicitta is far more superior and greater. 
3B2B 1A2C 2B3 Dispelling doubts 
[1,28 ] 
Although wishing to be rid of misery, 
They run towards misery itself. 
Although wishing to have happiness, 
Like an enemy they ignorantly destroy it  
 
[1,29] 
For those who are deprived of happiness 
And burdened with many sorrows, 
It satisfies them with all joys, 
Dispels all suffering, 
 
[1,30] 
And clears away confusion. 
Where is there a comparable virtue? 
Where is there even such a friend? 
Where is there merit similar to this? 
 Verses 28 to 30 are under the outline “dispelling qualms” – dispelling the qualms regarding the 
benefits of engaged bodhicitta.  The last two lines in verse 27 “Then what need to mention striving to make all beings, without 
exception happy?” is saying that because we are di rectly engaging in the practices to fulfil one’s 
aspiration of becoming enlightenment, the merit from engaged bodhicitta is greater than that 
coming from aspirational bodhicitta.  However,  there some doubts or qualms that needs to be clarified.   Qualm: When we have engaged bodhicitta, we are actually engaged in doing whatever is 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
40 necessary to fulfil the welfare of others.  Somebo dy might raise this objection saying “Aren’t we 
all expert in achieving our own happiness?  Aren ’t we expert in getting rid of suffering for 
ourselves? If that’s the case, sinc e we are expert in doing that, wh y do we need to work to make 
others happy? Why do we need to work towards eliminating the suffering of others?” In other 
words, why do we need buddhas? Why do we need bodhisattvas? 
 
Answer: The answer is in verse 28. There is a need  to benefit others. There is a need to have  
engaged bodhicitta to benefit others. Why? It is true that all sentient beings want happiness and 
do not want suffering. Motivated by the desire to avoid suffering, they   do whatever is necessary 
to achieve their goal of getting happiness and avoiding suffering. But because their minds are 
clouded by ignorance, especially the ignorance of the law of caus e and effect, even though they 
want happiness and do not want suffering, they enga ge in wrong actions in order to achieve their 
goal. Although sentient beings have the wish of wanting to be happy and to avoid suffering, they 
engage in unskillful or mistaken methods to ac hieve their goal. For example, when some people 
are tormented by the suffering of hunger and thirst , to remove this type of suffering, they might 
engage in killing another sentient being. In order to satisfy one’s desires, one will steal or engage 
in sexual misconduct. In short, sentient bein gs engage in the ten non-virtuous actions. 
 The situation of sentient beings is likened to a butterfly that, attracted by  the heat of the butter 
lamp, flies towards it and gets burnt by the flame. Similarly, sentient beings want to be happy but 
engage in mistaken actions. Sentient beings do not like suffering yet their minds are obscured by 
the ignorance that prevents them from seeing the causes of their sufferings. Sentient beings do 
not like suffering, yet they spontaneously run af ter the causes of suffering and engage in the 
causes of suffering.   All sentient beings would like to attain higher rebirths as humans and gods and may even have 
the intention to attain liberation from all suffe ring. Although they have such an intention or 
motivation, because their minds are clouded by th e ignorance regarding what is to be abandoned 
and what is to be cultivated to achieve their go al, because of this ignorance, they mistakenly 
abandon the causes of the happiness that they are looking for and engage in the causes that lead 
them to suffering. 
 As the text says, the way we strive to achieve our own happiness, whatever methods or approaches we take, it cannot even compare to th e way the bodhisattvas work in order to achieve 
the welfare of others. Basically, we are comparin g our abilities to work for our own happiness 
and the ability of the bodhisattvas to work for th e benefit of other sentient beings. Although we 
are working for our own happiness, we are unable  to achieve it. Whereas the bodhisattvas who 
are working only for others, they are able to gi ve us the happiness that we are looking for.  
 The bodhisattvas possess bodhicitta in their mind and have the intention to work for the benefit 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
41 of others to eliminate suffering and to achieve happiness for others. They are able to engage in 
activities to fulfil that goal. Bodhisattvas are able to do things that we are unable to do. 
Compared to bodhisattvas, we are sentient beings  with our minds clouded by ignorance of what 
is to be abandoned and what is to be cultivated. We do not know how to cultivate the causes of happiness and also do not know the causes of su ffering. Comparing our selves  to bodhisattvas, 
bodhisattvas are beings who possess bodhicitta and ar e able to engage in activities that  actually 
bring benefit to others.   Bodhisattvas, motivated by loving kindness and co mpassion, are able to satisfy the bodies and 
minds of sentient beings and enable them to achieve higher rebirths  as humans and gods. 
Bodhisattvas are motivated by compassion to help sentient beings  stuck in samsara, to cut the 
causes of cyclic existence. With  great wisdom, the bodhisattvas ar e able to show sentient beings, 
correctly, what is to be abandoned - especially the ignorance relating to the law of cause and 
effect - and what is to be cultiv ated. In short, bodhisattvas are able to bestow on sentient beings, 
who are deprived of happiness, the happiness that th ey are looking for. Bodhisattvas are also able 
to eliminate the suffering of sentient beings. Bodh isattvas are also able to eliminate the ignorance 
of sentient beings.   What is there that can compare to such a benevole nt intention or this bodh icitta? As in verse 30 
“Where is there a comparable virtue?” The bodh isattvas are able to give happiness, eliminate 
suffering and the ignorance of sentient beings because they possess bodhicitta. When we are 
comparing bodhicitta to other kinds of virtue, ther e is no other virtue that can be compared with 
bodhicitta. There is no other virtue that  has the same  benefit as bodhicitta. 
 Bodhicitta is also able to stop all harms and it  is something that enables us to obtain all the 
benefits. “Where is there even such a virtuous  friend?” There is none that can compare to 
bodhicitta. “Where is there merit similar to this?”  Bodhicitta is able to produce in the mind a 
special kind of joy in the short run and in the lo ng run. There is no merit that can compare to 
bodhicitta.   In essence, we should strive to  generate bodhicitta and maintain  that bodhicitta which has been 
generated. Basically, we need to generate bodhicitta. Of all the ki nds of virtue that one can find, 
bodhicitta is supreme. When we talk about virtuo us friends, bodhicitta is  supreme. When we 
look into the kinds of merit, especially in te rms of completing the accumulation of merit, 
bodhicitta is supreme.

42 3B2B 1A2D 1 They are worthy of praise because even though they have not been entrusted 
[with a specific task], by drawing upon great compassion, they act to assist others 
[1,31] 
If whoever repays a kind deed 
Is worthy of some praise, 
Then what need to mention the Bodhisattvas 
Who do good without it being asked of them? 
 
In this world, especially when we are down wi th suffering and then somebody comes along to 
help us. Later, when we see the person again, we will remember that this person has helped us 
before. This person is usually being praised as a good person.  What about someone who has grea t compassion and engages in activities to benefit sentient 
beings, without the hope of the favour being return ed or receiving any benefit from such actions? 
Or someone who looks at sentient beings without any bias? Such a person only works to benefit 
all sentient beings and all the activities of such a bodhisattva is only virtue. Obviously then such a 
person is worthy of praise by all humans and gods.   This verse is saying that if someone who benefits us in this life is worthy of praise and can be considered a good human being, it goes without saying that bodhisattvas who are always working 
for the benefit of all sentient beings with th e mind of great compassion and unconditionally, 
without any bias or hope of anything in return, su ch bodhisattvas are definitely objects worthy of 
praise by all humans and gods.   Therefore, bodhisattvas are suitable objects of homage and of prostration. Bodhisattvas help 
others without being asked or being requested. Wh en they help others, they don’t ask anything 
in return. Why are they working for sentient bein gs? This is because they are driven by great 
compassion. Of course, someone who accomplishes  something successful in this world is an 
object of praise. With reference to the bodhisa ttvas, it goes without saying that someone who 
accomplishes all benefit and bliss are worthy of the highest praise as seen in verses 32 and 33:

  [1,32]

What need be said then of one

43 Who eternally bestows the p eerless bliss of the Sugatas 
Upon limitless numbers of beings, 
Thereby fulfilling all their hopes? 
 
We are looking at the nature of the act of generosity in verse 32. The object of this act of 
generosity is just a few sentient beings who are lo wer in status than the giver. What they are being 
given is some plain food enough to satisfy them for half a day, just to keep them from starving; the food is not given all the time but only for a short period of time, and the food is given in a 
disrespectful way. Someone who performs such an ac t of generosity, with all these characteristics, 
is usually called a good person or a virtuous pers on in this world, such a person is considered 
s u i t a b l e  t o  b e  p r a i s e d .  I f  s u c h  a  p e r s o n  i s  a suitable object of praise, then what about 
bodhisattvas?  Bodhisattvas work not for one or two sentient be ings but for all sentient beings. The object of 
their action is limitless. They work all the time for sentient beings. They work until samsara ends. 
They work to bestow upon sent ient beings the unsurpassed bli ss of the Buddha. By doing so, 
they will be able to fulfil all the hopes of al l sentient beings. They are motivated by love and 
compassion and they have respect for sentient bein gs. Such are the characteristics of bodhisattvas 
working for sentient beings. Obviously, if someon e who performs an act of generosity in this 
world is considered a worthy object of praise, th en it goes without saying that bodhisattvas are 
worthy of the highest praise. As said in the text, the bodhisattvas are even able to give away their 
bodies to benefit others. It goes without saying th at giving away material possessions is nothing to 
them.  So bodhisattvas are the great benefactors.

A The reasons it is unreasonable to belittle a bodhisattva in even the slightest way 
B The reasons it is reasonable to cultivate faith 
C Thus, it is befitting to pay homage to them and go for refuge 
3B2B 1A2D 3A The reasons it is unreasonable to belittle a bodhisattva in even the slightest way 
 [1,34] 
The Buddha has said that wh oever bears a harmful thought 
Against a benefactor such as a Bodhisattva 
Will remain in hell for as many aeons 
As there were harmful thoughts 
 
Why is it incorrect to criticize bodhisattvas in ev en the slightest way? This is explained in verse 
34. In this world or society, if a person crit icizes someone who has fame and is also very 
generous, that person who criticizes him will be d eemed bad or uncivilized. If this is the case for 
such an ordinary person, then it goes without s aying that anybody who criticizes the bodhisattva 
will accumulate a lot of negative karma. Bodhis attvas are called great benefactors because they 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
44 able to give away everything for the sake of sentient beings. 
 
If some foolish sentient beings engage in ac tions with their bodies and speech to harm the 
bodhisattvas, obviously they accumulate very powe rful negative karma. Ev en if they do not do 
anything actively with their body and speech, bu t motivated by a mind of anger or even just 
entertain the thought of wanting to harm, hurt, put down or criticize the bodhisattva, that 
negative karma is already very powerful. As said  in the root text, a person who bears a harmful 
thought towards the bodhisattvas will  remain in the Avici hell for ma ny eons.  This is said by the 
Buddha himself. 
 In one of the sutras, Buddha said to Manjushri, “If anyone who just have the wish to harm the 
bodhisattvas or just have the wish to criticize, belittle or put down the bodhisattvas, that sentient 
being is making preparations to go to hell”. In  the same sutra, it was mentioned that, with the 
exception of bodhisattvas criticizing another bodhis attva, there is no other greater non-virtue that 
can throw us into the lower realms.   Why is there such strong negativity associated with criticizing or belittling bodhisattvas? What 
happens when you harm bodhisattvas? You are ac tually harming the causes of a buddha. When 
you are harming bodhisattvas, you are actually ha rming the causes of happiness of humans and 
gods. These are the two reasons why there is su c h   s t r o n g  n e g a t i v e  k a r m a   w h e n  o n e  h a r m s  
bodhisattvas. When you are harming bodhisattvas , you are damaging the causes of a buddha. 
You are harming the source of happiness of humans and gods.   
Chandrakirti composed a text, Engaging in the Middle Way , which starts with a famous verse of 
homage, where Chandrakirti pays respect to th e mind of great compassion. In that verse 
Chandrakirti says:  all the hearers and solitary realizers arise from the Subduer, the Buddha. 
Where does the Buddha arise from? The Buddha comes from the bodhisattvas. Where do the 
bodhisattvas come from? Bodhisattvas come from  bodhicitta. Bodhicitta is generated through 
great compassion. Therefore, great compassion is  important in the beginning, in the middle and 
at the end. Therefore, at the be ginning of this text, instead of paying homage to the Buddha and 
bodhisattvas as usual, Chandrakirti pays homage  and makes prostrations to great compassion.  
 In another sutra it is mentioned that the act of  harming or criticizing a bodhisattva has greater 
n e g a t i v i t y  t h a n  d e s t r o y i n g  s t u p a s  e q u a l  i n  n u m b e r  t o  t h e  g r a i n s  o f  s a n d  i n  t h e  G a n g e s  R i v e r .  Stupas are said to arise from the buddhas. Buddhas arise from bodhisattvas because of 
bodhicitta. Stupas therefore arise from  bodhicitta as their root cause.  
 So these are reasons why it is incorrect to criticize or belittle bodhisattvas.

45 3B2B 1A2D 3B The reasons it is reasonable to cultivate faith 
[1,35ab] 
However, if a virtuous attitude should arise (in that regard), 
Its fruits will multiply far more than that. 
 
Previously, we looked at the negativity of harmin g, criticizing or putting down bodhisattvas. This 
was on the negative side. What if you generate fa ith towards bodhisattvas? What results will arise? 
Those intelligent ones who genera ted the mind of clear faith towa rds bodhisattvas, the result of 
this faith will multiply even more strongly th an the power of the negativity for criticizing 
bodhisattvas.   If you compare these two actions: the act of criticizing bodhisattv as and the act of faith towards 
bodhisattvas, the fruit of the latter will multiply fa r more than the act of criticizing bodhisattvas. 
Let us say, all sentient beings in the worlds of th e ten directions would all be blind. If we were to 
restore their eyesight, this merit cannot even compare to just looking with eyes of faith 
respectfully towards a bodhisattva; the la tter action brings  even more merit.  
 Verse 34 is telling us the negativity of criticizin g or belittling bodhisattvas. Verse 35 is telling us 
the benefit we will obtain if we are able to cult ivate a clear mind of faith towards bodhisattvas.  
 The best means for avoiding criticizing bodhisa ttvas and the best method for cultivating faith 
towards bodhisattvas can be found in the four wh ite dharmas and  the four black  dharmas. The 
best antidote is generating the attitude that all sentient beings are buddhas.   One of the white dharmas involves generating the attitude that all sentient beings are buddha, 
and it is an antidote to one of the black dharmas of criticizing or disparaging bodhisattvas out of 
a n g e r .  A s  i t  i s  h a r d  t o  k n o w  w h o  i s  a  b o d h i s a ttva and who is not, it is your bad luck if you 
happen to criticize someone who is a bodhisattva.  But if one treats all sentient beings with 
respect and one of them happens to be a bodhisa ttva, then we can accumulate a lot of merit.  
 There are many ways to avoid the pitfalls of cr iticizing bodhisattvas. On e is by generating the 
attitude of regarding all sentient beings as buddha. Another is by focusing on the positive and good qualities of sentient beings instead of focu sing on their faults. Although sentient beings 
might have faults, if those sentient beings were to cultivate the antidotes, those faults can be 
removed. Since there is great danger of making a big loss and there is the possibility of making a 
big gain with regards to how we treat bodhisattvas, to avoid the pitfalls, the text gives this advice: 
1. cultivate the attitude that all sentient beings are buddha 
2. focus on the good qualities of sentient beings 
3. contemplate the fact that the faults of sentie nt beings can be removed if they meet with 
the correct antidotes.

(From Module 4, Middle Length Lam Rim    Lesson 10) 
Black dharma White dharma (as antidote to black 
dharma) 
1. Deceiving abbots, mast ers, gurus, and those 
worthy of offerings 1. Forsaking consciously lying to any living 
being whatsoever even in  jest or even for the 
sake of your life. 
2. Making others feel regret about something that is not regrettable 2. Causing the living beings that you are helping to mature to not want the modest vehicle but to adhere to perfect enlightenment. 
3. Speaking disparagingly, etc., to beings who have correctly entered the Mahayana. 3. Developing the idea that all bodhisattvas are the Teacher.  
4. In an absence of sincerity, using deceit and misrepresentation to get the service of others. 4. Not deceiving but remaining sincere to all living beings. 
3B2B 1A2D 3C Thus, it is befitting to pay homage to them and go for refuge 
With the reasons we have discovered , the conclusion, therefore, is that  it is befitting for us to pay 
homage to the bodhisattvas and go to them for re fuge. This is mentioned in the last two lines of 
verse 35 and verse 36. 
[1,35cd] 
When Bodhisattvas greatly suffer they generate no negativity, 
Instead their virtues naturally increase. 
 
[1,36] 
I bow down to the body of those 
In whom the sacred pr ecious mind is born. 
I seek refuge in that source of joy 
Who brings happiness even to those who bring harm. 
 Bodhisattvas can bear any kind of harm or sufferi ng they meet, even at the cost of their lives. 
They have patience with all kinds of harm an d sufferings. When they meet with conditions 
where they may have to suffer, instead of gene rating anger, these conditions will assist the 
bodhisattvas in their practice of patience and also increase the bodhisattvas’ patience. 
 Bodhisattvas, like wish-fulfilling jewels, are able  to fulfil the wishes and happiness of sentient 
beings and also to remove all sufferings of sentie nt beings. To such wish-fulfilling bodhisattvas, 
we should prostrate with our body, speech and mind.

47 3B2B 1B The chapter’s name 
This chapter is called the explanation of the benefi ts of bodhicitta. This fi rst chapter is basically 
on the benefits of bodhicitta. We should contemplate it and if we are able to see all the benefits 
of bodhicitta, naturally, an aspiration to de velop bodhicitta will arise. Once we have the 
aspiration to develop bodhicitta, we should do something about it. 
 
The last verse says bodhisattvas are suitable an d worthy to be objects of homage. We should 
prostrate to the bodhisattvas.  Within the category of bodhisattv as, there are some who are more senior than others. Is it all 
right for senior bodhisattvas to prostrate to juni or bodhisattvas? The answer is yes. By cultivating 
the attitude that the junior bodhisattva is a Bu ddha, the senior bodhisattva can prostrate to the 
junior bodhisattva. In general, it is all right for the senior bodhisattvas to prostrate to the junior 
bodhisattvas. But there are certai n restrictions, depending on th e place and time. Even in the 
situation where it is inappropriate for a senior bodhisattva to prostrate to the junior bodhisattva, the bodhisattva can still pay mental homage even though he does not show veneration with his 
body or speech.  Verse 36 is saying bodhisattvas are suitable object s of homage, suitable objects of prostration and 
suitable objects of refuge. The reason why a bodh isattva is a suitable object of refuge, praise, 
homage or prostration is not because of the person  himself. A bodhisattva is a special person, but 
what makes the bodhisattva special is the bodhicitta that a bodhisa ttva has. It is bodhicitta which 
makes the bodhisattva a suitable object of prostrat ion. What is it that ma kes bodhicitta such an 
amazing or marvellous thing? This is be cause of the benefits it can bring.  
 In the text, we talked about the difficulty of ge nerating bodhicitta. We don’t think of achieving 
perfect enlightenment even for ourselves. Obviously, if we can’t conceive of it for ourselves, then 
conceiving it for others and wish ing all other sentient beings to be happy and for them to achieve 
a state of perfect Buddhahood is inconceivable or  something that seems almost impossible. The 
main thing is to reflect on the benefits of bodhic itta. Once we see the bene fits of bodhicitta, then 
the wish to develop it will arise. We  should try to train in bodhicitta.

48 Lesson 7 – chapter 2, verse 1-9 
 
The prayer of refuge and generating bodhicitta that we just recited is called sangye chö tsog ma in 
Tibetan. It is important not just to recite this prayer but also to remind ourselves the purpose of  
reciting it. This prayer symbolises something very important. Since we consider ourselves 
Buddhists, then all our actions should always be conjoined with the two mental states of refuge 
and bodhicitta. It is important that these attitudes should be a part of us and we should make it a 
habit always to remind ourselves of this. 
 In the last session, we looked at the faults and disadvantages of criticising a bodhisattva. 
Criticising or belittling a bodhisattva is a very, ve ry serious negative action as seen in verse 34 of 
chapter I.  
[1,34] 
The Buddha has said that wh oever bears a harmful thought 
Against a benefactor such as a Bodhisattva 
Will remain in hell for as many eons 
As there were harmful thoughts 
 If one were to generate positive thoughts or to ac t in a positive way in relation to a bodhisattva, 
then one would  experience positive effects as  mentioned in the first two lines of verse 35: 
[1,35 ab] 
However, if a virtuous attitude should arise (in that regard), 
Its fruits will multiply far more than that. 
 In general, we can’t say for sure who is a buddh a, who is not a buddha, who is a bodhisattva and 
who is not a bodhisattva. In order to be safe , the supreme method to avoid the pitfall of 
generating negative actions towards them is to cu ltivate the pure perception of sentient beings, 
seeing these beings in their pure forms. Even at times when we see faults in others, we have to 
remind ourselves that those faults are conditione d phenomena  – that means, they are subject to 
change.  When those sentient beings apply the co rrect antidotes, those faults can be removed. 
 This practice of pure perception starts with on e’s guru, one’s spiritual master, followed by one’s 
friends, those who are around us all th e time, especially our Dharma friends. 
 There are many reasons why we sh ould practice this way of th inking, this pure perception. 
Looking at it in a simple way, we  are all from the same centre an d we need to have mutual love 
and affection for one another. At the next level,  since we are all followers of Lama Tsong Khapa 
and follow the teachings of Je Rinpoche, from that  point of view, since we are all the same, then 
we should also cultivate that attitude of  pure perception. Then, if we bring it up another level, 
we are all followers of the Buddha’s teachings, and if we bring it up yet another level, we are all 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  O n e  
 
 
49 human beings, we are all part of humanity. Bringing it up to the next level, we are all the same in 
that we all have consciousnesses and we are all sent ient beings. If we cultivate this perception by 
moving up the different levels as mentioned above,   then our ability to deal with more people 
increases. That means we will be able to make more friends and cultivate more relationships and 
so forth. Anger towards sentient beings will also decrease. 
 When one is practising pure perception, one pays respect with one’s body, speech and mind, 
firstly, to one’s spiritual master and then one extends it to all ordained persons and to all sentient beings.   In Singapore, in the local context, if you were  to look at the Chinese Mahayana community, you 
would generally find the respect that they show to wards ordained persons is very, very high – they

Mahayana tradition, we would  not show respect to such an extent.  Showing respect with one’s body, speech and mind is something good and is necessary. As long 
as a person bears the signs of ordination, that person is an object of homage. There are many 
reasons why it is important to remember this.  When I mention such things , people might think I am asking them to show me more respect –

see any ordained persons, you should show respec t in the proper way. This is a practice that 
benefits ourselves so I would like to say, please try to develop this practice.  If we compare ourselves to other Buddhist tr aditions, either locally or with the other 
organisations, we are different in that people here do study the teachi ngs of the Buddha and 
practise. I think we can say that we have bette r knowledge of the teachings of the Buddha than 
others; that we can explain the teachings of the Bu ddha with the help of reason and logic.  We 
have many good qualities that ot hers do not possess. In that se nse, we are better than others. 
 Our external behaviour should, therefore, reflec t whatever knowledge we might have. When one 
knows certain things, but that is not reflected in one’s actions of body, speech and mind by 
showing proper respect and so forth, then ther e is a discrepancy and disparity, between our 
knowledge and behaviour, isn’t it? In that case, one needs to close  the gap between the two.  
 
Specifically, we are now studying the te xt of the bodhisattvas’ practices called Engaging in the 
Bodhisattva Deeds . Since we are studying this text, at the same time, we need to have harmonious 
relations with one another, so that,  even if th ere should be no great benefit that  comes from

50 students and the members, we should cultivate loving kindness towards, respect for and pure 
perception of one another.  
 
I think I have mentioned this before in the past. For example, if some new people were to walk 
into the centre for the first time trying to look for something, we could  at least approach these 
newcomers, introduce ourselves and generally make th em feel at home – basically talk to them. If 
people came to the centre and they did not  feel  welcomed, nobody talking  to them or nobody 
welcoming  them or helping them out, then it’s not their fault, it’s our fault – the fault of the 
people who make up the centre. It is something we need to change.   Also, with regards to any ordained person who wa lks in to the centre, m aybe we don’t show them 
enough (at this point, Geshe-la in terjects: “Don’t show them black face!”) respect – we treat them  
as if they were lower in status than ourselves,  in a casual manner.  We need to change this. 
 If you think about it, there is a need to impr ove the centre and this is not just the sole 
responsibility of the teacher, the Exco (Executive Committee) or the staff of  the centre.  It is the 
responsibility of everyone who comes to the centre . Why do we need to do this? We are not like 
a business organisation that has the goal of ma king more money or other worldly concerns. The 
purpose of improving the centre is to provide the favourable conditions for us to spread the pure 
teachings, to help the teachings to be preserved and to flourish.   With regards again to ordained persons, if we cr iticise them or we do anything inappropriate to 
them, then we will accumulate nega tive karma. On the other hand, if we accord respect or relate 
to them in the proper way, then it serves to increase our own merits.  
 I would like to say that whatever I mentioned he re, we should try to change these things. We 
should not just  leave it at the level of listenin g.  We should try and see what we can all do to 
change the situation because if we don’t change the situation, then it is just listening.  
 Sometimes, people might feel that the teachings do not have an impact on their minds or they 
feel that the teachings are not beneficial and ther e is no feeling for the teachings and so forth. 
But whether we benefit from the teachings depend s on ourselves and how we listen, whether we 
see the reasoning behind what is said and whether we contemplate what we have heard. If we do 
such things, then, on an individual level, we will receive some benefit.   The example here of according proper respect to ordained persons and not putting them down -  
one should understand why we should not do th ese things. Not all Sangha members are perfect. 
All have faults. But what makes them special, what  makes them better is that they hold the vows. 
It is not because they are free of faults.

51 When we say you should not criticise an ordained  person or put down an ordained person, it 
does not mean that you are surrendering your in dependence, that your right to free speech is 
being suppressed, that you cannot complain, you ca nnot  do anything. That is not what is meant 
by ‘you cannot criticise an ordained person’ – it  does not mean that whatever ordained persons 
do, even if they were to do bad things, then we s ay,  “Oh, they are doing wonderful things or it’s 
a good thing they are doing.”   That is not what  is meant. Of course, when you see something, 
which   is not right, which n eeds correction,  then one can re spectfully approach and ask the 
ordained person,  ”I don’t understand why you are doing this. What is your reason for doing 
this?”  One can discuss the matter and seek a solution. That is th e meaning of not criticising and 
not belittling an ordained person.  
 This thing about having mutual respect – it a pplies to everyone. All the students and members 
within the centre should have mutual respect for one another. If that were not present, not 
developed, then obviously, one would go on to show disrespect to ordained persons and once 
that happened, one would also go on to show disres pect to one’s spiritual master, one’s guru. It is 
a progressive thing. And if that really happened , all the way up, then we would be the ones to 
suffer the loss.  The best advice is to cultivate the attitude th at all sentient beings are buddhas and regarding 
them as buddhas, to show respect with one’s bo dy, speech and mind. This should be applied to 
whoever comes to the Centre, wh oever walks into the Centre.

3B2B 2A1A 1A The need to make offerings 
[2,1] 
In order to seize that precious mind 
I offer now to the Tathagatas, 
To the sacred Dharma, the stainless jewel, 
And to the Sons of Buddha, the oceans of excellence, 
 
Why do we need to make offerings? This is indicated in the first line of verse 1, “In order to seize 
that precious mind”.  In the previous chapter we looked at the immeas urable benefits of bodhicitta. If one were to 
generate bodhicitta, one would accumulate i mmeasurable benefits. The purpose of making 
offerings is to generate this mind  of enlightenment or bodhicitta th at  is likened to a jewel that 
fulfils all wishes and is the source of all excell ence, the well-being and happiness of oneself and all 
s e n t i e n t  b e i n g s .  T h e  r e a s o n  w e  n e e d  t o  m a k e  o fferings is to generate this bodhicitta in our 
mental continuum.  
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
53 The objects towards whom these offerings are made  are special. They are: the Tathagata or the 
buddhas - those who have gone into the sphere of reality that possesse s the two purities; the 
Dharma: the true paths and true cessations; and the Sangha: this primarily refers to the arya 
bodhisattvas like Chenrezig and Manjushri 
 As said in the root text, “the disciples of Bu ddha, the oceans of exce llence”; this means they 
possess oceans of qualities. Here the sons or di sciples of Buddha refers to the arya bodhisattvas 
from the first ground upwards. When bodhisattvas  attain the first ground, they acquire twelve 
special qualities. Those qualities multiply a thou sand fold as the bodhisattvas move up to the 
next ground. For example, a bodhisattva on the secondnd ground has the qualities acquired on 
the first ground multiplied a thousand times.  There is also another explanation that affixes th e phrase, the Oceans of Excellence, individually 
to the Three Jewels. It can also be explained fr om that perspective. The phrase, the Oceans of 
Excellence,  can be affixed to the Dharma Jewel and also to the Buddha Jewel.  There is another explanation to this verse. In one commentary, it is said th at in order to seize or 
to generate this precious bodhic itta, this awakening mind, which is a mind that is very precious, 
very holy and very pure, we need to rely on different things.   W e  n e e d  t o  m a k e  o f f e r i n g s  t o  o r  r e l y  o n  p u r e  f i e l d s  o r  p u r e  o b j e c t s .  F r o m  t h e  p e r s p e c t i v e  o f  one’s object, the object to whom one is making the offerings – that is the object or field. We also 
need pure and perfect offering substances in term s of very pleasant offering substances. The pure 
objects that one offers can also include the Dh arma, one’s practice. Obviously, one’s attitude, 
one’s motivation or one’s intention behind the acti on should also be pure in the sense that the 
act of offering is not being influenced by, for example, miserliness. And the action of making 
offerings needs to be pure and not mixed with ne gativity, for example, not mixed with the act of 
killing or stealing.  We should include the word “well” (ie proper or pe rfect way) as in “I offer well”. This is in the 
Tibetan text but is not translated in the English te xt we are using. It shou ld be there. There is a 
commentary that explains the meaning of “I offer well to the Tathagata”. Why is the word “well” 
there? It is because one is making all these offe rings with the intention to achieve enlightenment, 
one is making all these offerings for the benefit of all sentient beings, and one is making very 
pleasing offerings in the sense that it is conjoi ned with the understanding of the emptiness of the 
three circles. It is not polluted by the stains or  by the class of adventitious phenomenon. Basically 
it is not polluted by the impure stains of phen omena. And one dedicates the act of offering to 
achieve enlightenment for the benefit of all sentie nt beings. These are the five reasons why an act 
of offerings would be considered well-done.  The three circles as applied to this act of offe ring are: the object involved – the offering 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
54 substances, the object of the action of offering  – the buddhas and bodhisattvas, and the agent or 
the person who is performing this action. One reflects on how all these three are empty of 
existing inherently while making the offerings. 
 When one is making offerings to the Three Jewe ls, one can also think how the Three Jewels are 
the unsurpassed objects of offering, how they are th e objects or fields that give us the greatest 
benefit, how the Three Jewels are supreme of al l sentient beings. In the Lamrin Chenmo the 
different ways of making offerings, the distinctio n in terms of thought or intention is mentioned. 
When we are talking about the distinction or di fference in terms of thou ght or intention, we 
should think about how the objects to whom we ar e making offerings , ie. the Three Jewels,  are 
the unsurpassed fields of merit, how they are the be st fields that give us the greatest benefit, how 
they are the supreme of  all sentient beings. Ba sically, you are not expecting anything from this 
offering and not hoping for any return or expecting to reap any benefit, that is not the intention.  
And when we talk about the distinction in terms of  intention or thought, we can also reflect on 
the purity of the three circles.  The meaning of the phrase, “I offer well to the Tathagata...” is explained through the distinction 
in terms of thought or intention. What we have been discussing so far is the kind of attitudes or 
thought that will make one’s act of offering well done.

3B2B 2A1A 1B1A  Offering substances 
[2, 2] 
Whatever flowers and fruits there are, 
And whatever kinds of medicine, 
Whatever jewels exist in this world 
And whatever clean, refreshing waters; 
 
[2, 3] 
Likewise gem-encrusted mountains, 
Forest groves, quiet and joyful places,

55 [2, 4] 
Fragrances of the celestial realms, 
Incense, wishing trees and jewel trees, 
Uncultivated harvests, and all ornaments 
That are worthy of being offered; 
 
[2, 5] 
Lakes and pools adorned with lotuses, 
And the beautiful cry of swan, 
Everything unowned 
Within the limitless spheres of space. 
 
Basically we are talking about the different kind s of offering substances that  are unowned: 
flowers, different kinds of medicines as mentioned in the text, precious thin gs in this world such 
as gold, silver and jewels, different bodies of clean, refreshing water, gem-encrusted mountains 
such as the seven golden mountains, forest gr oves, quiet and joyful places, heavenly trees 
ornamented with beautiful flowers, fruit trees that give harvests of good fruits, fragrances of the 
celestial realms which appease the gods, naga s and human beings and wish-fulfilling trees, 
uncultivated harvests which are  crops that grow  by themselves and need no farming, different 
kinds of ornaments, lakes and pools adorned with lotuses and the beautiful cry of swans. 
 Basically, we are talking about all the different kind s of substances, everything in this world that  
does not belong to ourselves. We can visualiz e them and make them objects of the mind. For 
example, we can offer places that have pools and gardens where we go for picnics. Also mountains contain many kinds of precious substa nces such as natural mines, etc. One takes 
them as objects of the mind, visualize them and o ffer them to the Three Jewels as extensively as 
one can. For someone who has an  active mind that jumps everywhere, one can take this 
opportunity to create virtue by visualizing all th ese different kinds of substances and using them 
as offerings. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B1B  How to offer them 
[2, 6 ] 
Creating these things in my mind, I offer them 
To the supreme beings, the Buddhas, as well as their Children, 
O Compassionate Ones, think kindly of me 
And accept these offerings of mine. 
 
“Creating these things in my mind”: visualise al l these offering substances and take them as 
objects of the mind and make all these offeri ngs of unowned substances to the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas with respect, faith and reflec ting on the purity of the three circles. 
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

56 “O Compassionate Ones, think kindly of me, and accept these offerings of mine” : make 
offerings to the compassionate ones who only act to benefit sentient bein gs, those who are always 
under the influence of great compassion. Make all these offerings to them and make requests to 
them to remember us, to think kindly of us. It is good to remember these different attitudes 
whenever we make offerings, and with whatever offerings we make. In this case, the objects of 
one’s offerings are the buddhas as well their chil dren, the bodhisattvas. The offering substances 
here are those things that are unowned and one ta kes all these substances as the objects of the 
mind, taking these objects and making them as belonging  to oneself and then one makes these 
offerings.  
3B2B 2A1A 1B1C  The reason one offers unowned substances 
[2, 7 ] 
Having no merit, I am destitute, 
And I have no other gifts to offer. 
O Protectors, you who think of helping others, 
By your power accept these for my sake. 
 Some people might wonder why we could not  just  offer the things that we own ourselves. Why 
do we have to offer unowned substances? The reas on is that since one has not created the roots 
of virtue that comes from the p r a c t i c e  o f  v e r y  p o w e r f u l  a c t i o n s  o f  g e n e r o s i t y  i n  t h e  p a s t ,  
therefore, at the present mome nt, one does not own excellent substances – very pure and 
wonderful substances  - that can be offered. We are poor in these beautiful, excellent substances. 
Since we do not possess such roots of virtue th a t  e n a b l e  u s  t o  o w n  s u c h  e x c e l l e n t  o f f e r i n g  
substances, the only way we can offer them is to  visualize all those that are unowned by us and 
make offerings of them instead. The only way is  to visualise the unowned substances and holding 
those substances in one’s mind and offer them  to the buddhas and bodhisattvas and request 
them to accept these offerings.  From the Buddha’s side, there is no need for any of  these offerings as the  Buddha does not lack 
of any of this offering substances. Buddhas do no t have self- grasping and also do not have the 
attitude that “these things belong to me an d they are mine”. The whole purpose of making 
offerings is to enable us to complete the accumu lation of merit. Basically, visualise extensively 
and then make unowned substances as offerings to the buddhas and bodhisattvas. The practice 
of making unowned substance is actually taught in the Sutra of the Clouds of Jewels .

Lesson 8 – chapter 2, verse 9-16 
 
You will have noticed that after reciting the Heart Sutra , we recited verses from the root text itself. 
I thought that this recitation may be beneficial for all of us in class. One benefit is that we will 
become more familiar with the verses with repeated  recitation over time. Secondly, since this is a 
special text composed by the bodhisattva Shantide va himself, just by reciting and reading the 
text, that act of recitation alone will generate vast stores of merit for all of us. 
 In the monastery back in South India, the monks will usually gather and recite prayers for a few 
hours before any debate session. The monks will make strong prayers for success in their studies 
and debates. Similarly, for us here to have su ccess in our studies, we need to make extensive 
prayers. We should make extensive prayers when we  recite the verses from the text during the 
preliminary ritual.  There is a saying that have been  passed down from generation to  generation back in the great 
monasteries that there is no need to perform sp ecial rituals or pujas to clear away obstacles for 
study for monks in the monastery. Usually, the monk s are advised to take their recitation of the 
common prayers seriously when they gather before the debates or when they have opportunity to 
do prayers together. The monks are advised to refl ect carefully when they are reciting the prayers 
and to set a good motivation and contemplate well.  By doing that alone, all the obstacles that 
might come about during the course of  study will naturally be removed.  
 Similarly, in the course of our study, when we are doing our prayers before class, we should have the same thought as well. We sh ould make strong prayers during the recitation and prayers 
thinking at the same time, “May this recitation practice remove all obstacles and unfavourable 
conditions that might come about in the course of our study and practice”. With this thought 
and respectfully with our body, speech and mind, we engage in the practice of recitation.  Even though we might have the wish to study and practice the teachings of the Buddha but there 
are so many kinds of obstacles such as outer ob stacles, inner obstacles and even obstacles that 
exist on the secret level that may arise along the way. When we meet with such obstacles, 
sometimes, things may get tough. The best way to pacify any of th ese obstacles is to make very 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
58 strong prayers combined with very strong determination to continue one’s studies.  
 
It is possible that, sometimes, when reciting th ese preliminary prayers, we may get bored or we 
may not be able to see the reason for doing them and see them as a chore. We do not recite these 
prayers for no reason, without any great purpose. Th ere are many benefits. In fact, since this text 
is composed by the great bodhisattva, Shantide va, the benefit and merit that one accumulates 
just by reciting this text is inexpressible. We should remind ourselves of this  when we recite the 
text or prayers. We should do them enthusiastically.   
From the time we start reciting the Heart Sutra  and all the way till the end of the session when we 
dedicate the merit, whatever goes on during the session is considered  virtuous action. Basically 
we should try to do whatever we can to make the whole session enjoyable from our own side and 
to do it enthusiastically; instead of thinking that  we are doing this with no choice, like paying 
taxes. When we talk about virtue when engaging  in dharma practice, the purpose is to remove 
suffering. If our dharma practice leads to suffer ing, then I don’t see much point in doing them. 
Dharma practice is basically done with the mind and it should contribute to the goal of removing 
suffering. It is something that should be done willingly from one’s own side. When one sees the 
point, one would want to do the practice. The main purpose of doing dharma practice is to 
change the mind. Basically, this practice should  be coming from our own side enthusiastically 
and not done through force.

3B2B 2A1A 1B2 Offering one’s own body 
This is shown in verses 8 and 9: 
[2,8] 
Eternally shall I offer all my bodies 
To the Conquerors and their Children,  
Please accept me, you Supreme Heroes,  
Respectfully shall I be your subject. 
 
[2,9] 
By being completely under your care, 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
59 I shall benefit all with no fear s of conditioned existence;  
I shall perfectly transcend my  previous transgressions  
And in the future shall commit no more. 
 
Here we are offering our bodies to the conquero rs and their children, the bodhisattvas. Basically, 
we are saying, “I am offering this body, please accept it”. Why are buddhas known as conquerors 
or sometimes also translated as the victorious ones  or victors? This is because they have achieved 
complete victory over the four maras2. 
 After offering our bodies to the conquerors and th eir children and asking them to accept us, we 
become part of their entourage or subjects. Then , one willingly follows the advice of the buddhas 
and bodhisattvas and undertake whatever tasks th ey have set out for us. From our own side, we 
offer ourselves to the victorious ones and bodhisattvas thinking, “I  will follow whatever advice or 
tasks you have for me and I will do it”. By entr usting ourselves in such a way to the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas, offering our bodies  and thinking, “I will accomplish  whatever you have set out for 
me,” one comes under the refuge or protection  of the buddhas and bo dhisattvas. Since one 
                                             
 
2 The four maras are: the mara of the afflictions, the mara of the aggregates , the mara of the Lord of Death  and 
the mara of Devaputra .  
 The mara of the afflictions: the two primary causes why we are still circling in cyclic existence are karma and 
afflictions. Karma is motivated by afflic tions and our afflictions are the primary cause of cyclic existence. Afflictions 
disturb our mental peace when they arise in the mind. Thes e afflictions are within us and are not external. In our 
mental continuum, they are posited as the mara of affliction. Motivated by different afflictions, we engage in 
different types of actions and then accumulate the karma that ca uses us to be in cyclic existence. As a result of this, 
we have to take on contaminated mental and physical aggreg ates over and over again in cyclic existence. This set of 
contaminated mental and physical aggregates is due to karm a and afflictions. This explains the second seal: that all 
contaminated things are miserable.   The mara of aggregates refers to the contaminated mental and physical aggregates that we have as the result of 
karma and our afflictions. The contaminated mental and physical aggregate are due to the process of karma and afflictions and cannot be good as th ey are in the nature of suffering. 
 The Devaputra mara (devaputra literally means son of deva ): this refers to the manifestation of the state of 
attachment that appears in our mind. When we see somethin g attractive, we get attached to it and this attachment 
manifests in our mind. Attachment is an affliction and when attachment ma nifests in our mental continuum, it 
disturbs the peace in our mind. It also  affects our mind and we lose our po wer of analysis and intelligence. We 
cannot think rationally and cannot differentiate what is good and what is bad.  The mara of the Lord of Death refers to the phenomenon of death itself. We have to be born and die over and 
over again under the control of the power of our afflictions.   These four maras are not external phen omena that are separate from our me ntal continuum but they are within 
us, they are in our mental continuum. Because we are unde r the bondage of these maras,  we are always suffering. 
(from ABC Basic Program Modu le 3, Tenets, Lesson 8) 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
60 comes under the protection of the buddhas and bo dhisattvas, then one has no fear. On the basis 
of this fearlessness, then one thinks, “I will act to benefit all sentient beings.” 
 
Even though one might come under the protec tion of the Three Jewels, due to having 
accumulated great stores of negativities in the pa st,  if we have not purified these negativities, 
then we can forget about fulfilling the purposes of others, as we won’t even be able to fulfil our own goals and wishes. Therefore, we need to enga g e  i n  t h e  p r a c t i c e  o f  c o n f e s s i o n  t o  p u r i f y  
whatever negativities we have accumulated in th e past and generate the power of restraint to 
restrain ourselves from committing any further negati vities in the future, ev en at the cost of our 
lives.  What happens when we offer the bodies that we  cherish so much to the Three Jewels? After 
completely entrusting ourselves to the Three Jewe ls and after taking refuge, we will come under 
the protection of the Three Jewels and become  part of the entourage of the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. Literally, we become the se rvants of the buddhas and bodhisattvas. 
 What happens after we become the servants of the buddhas and bodhisattvas? What should we 
do from then on? From then on, what we have to do is to avoid creating negative actions and we need to benefit others, because the buddhas’ acti vities are basically working for the benefit of 
sentient beings. Remembering that, we make the pr omise and think, “I will fulfil this and work 
for the benefit of all sentient beings”.   Everyday, in the morning, when we wake up, we can generate this attitude and offer ourselves 
wholeheartedly and completely to the buddhas and bodhisattvas. This  will make a lot of 
difference. If we offer ourselves wholeheartedly, the negativities that we have accumulated will 
definitely decrease with time. When we wake up, with the knowledge that it is the wish of the 
buddhas and bodhisattvas that no sentient beings  come to any harm or suffering, we completely 
entrust ourselves to the buddhas and bodhisattv as, offer ourselves completely and make the 
p r o m i s e  t h a t  “ I  w i l l  n o t  h a r m  o r  h u r t  a n y  s e n t i e n t  b e i n g s ” .  I f  w e  c a n  d o  t h i s  c o m p l e t e l y  a n d  wholeheartedly, this becomes the best protection for ourselves. 
 Among all the kinds of protections that one can medi tate on, it is said that entrusting one self 
completely to the buddhas and bodhisattvas and o ffering our bodies is the best protection. From 
then on, the Buddha will always be looking after us. If we can offer ourselves completely to the 
buddhas and bodhisattvas, without leaving anything  behind, then, it is said it will grant us 
protection and we will not be affect ed by any outer and inner obstacles.
