3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 1A Site for ablutions 
First we have to generate the ba th-house - the place where we o ffer ablutions to the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. This is mentioned in verse 10: 
[2,10] 
To very sweetly-scented bathing chambers,  
With brilliantly sparkling crystal floors  
And exquisite pillars ablaze with gems,  
With canopies above aglow with pearls, 
 
We generate the bath house through visualizing it in  the space in front of us. It is not the actual 
bath-house sitting in front of us. This is a mentally emanated offering. It is an offering that is done with the mind. One first visualizes the bath-house.  The practice of offering ablutions or a bath to  the Three Jewels is in  accordance with the 
traditions of ancient India. In the past, when one invited the king  or somebody very important to 
one’s home, one would usually wash their feet first.  This is  an act of reverence to the guest or 
the very important person when they arrive. Afte r that, scented water will be offered and lastly, a 
flower garland will be given.  The reason why we offer a bath to the Three Jewe ls is not because there is something wrong with 
their bodies, speech and minds. It is not that th ey possess faults that obscure their holy bodies, 
speech and minds. The reason that we offer ablution s to the Three Jewels is to purify the faults 
and negativities of our own body, speech and mi nd. The way to perform the ablution ceremony 
is shown in verse 11 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
62 3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 1B The way [to perform] the ablution ceremony 
[2,11] 
I beseech the Tathagatas  and their Children 
To come and bathe their bodies 
From many-jewelled vases, filled wi th waters scented and enticing, 
To the accompaniment of music and song 
 
We have already visualized the bath-house in the space in front of us. Next, we invite and 
visualize all the buddhas and bodhisattvas goin g to the bath house an d then we offer the 
ablutions or bath to them with many jewelled vases that are completely filled with scented waters, 
accompanied with music and song.   
How do we offer the bath to all these guests? However many buddhas and bodhisattvas there are 
in the space in front of us, we emanate the same nu mber of replicas of ourselves in front of these 
guests, making this bath offering. Or,  instead of visualizing replicas of ourselves, we can emanate 
from our hearts offering goddesses equal in number  to our guests. On this basis, we make the 
bath offering to all of them. All this happens in th e bath-house. The budd has and bodhisattvas 
are sitting there and all the goddesses or replicas of ourselves go up to them and offer ablutions 
or bath to the buddhas and bodhisattvas, holding  many jewelled vases filled with scented water. 
At the same time, the whole ceremony is accompanied with offerings of music and beautiful 
sound.   By performing this bath offering or offering ablutions to these buddhas and bodhisattvas, we 
should think that all the negative karmas and ob scurations of ourselves and all sentient beings 
have been purified. At the same time, we can also think that whatever  harm that we might 
receive from the landlords (the spirits who own th e place) and the host of  harm-givers (who are 
very nasty and can harm people residing at the pl ace),by offering this bath , we think that all the 
negative thoughts in their minds and their wish es to cause harm to others are completely 
purified. All kinds of contagious diseases are also  completely purified. At the end, we visualize 
that bodhicitta is generated not only in our minds but also in the minds of all sentient beings. By 
doing this bath offering, we can visualize that all the different kinds of negative minds in the 
continuum of different kinds of sentient beings are completely purified. Then all of us and all sentient beings generate bodhicitta.  It is also said that for those of us who have diffi culties when it comes to listening to or studying 
the teachings, who keep on falling asleep and wh o have an unclear mind due to the mind being 
completely obscured by the different layers of ob scurations, then doing this bath offering is one 
of the supreme means to get rid of these obscurations.    Most of us have seen how the bath offering ritual is done externally. Normally, we have a vase 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
63 container and a mirror. One will hold a kata at th e beginning of the ritual, at the time when one 
generates the bath-house. One then  pours some water from the vase onto  the mirror. This is 
what is being done externally. Internally,  we have  to visualize  what has just been explained so 
far. Of course, it would be ideal if we can have all the external substances such as the vase and 
the necessary instruments that are required to perf orm the ritual. Even if we don’t have  access to 
the external substances, however, we can st ill do the practice through visualization. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 1C Drying the holy body 
Next is the drying of the holy bodies as  shown in the first two lines of verse 12: 
[2,12] 
Let me dry their bodies with incomparable cloths 
Clean and well-anointed with scent,  
And then may I present these Holy Beings  
With fragrant garments of suitable colors. 
 “Let me dry their bodies with incomparable cl oths, clean and well-anointed with scent” - the 
drying of the holy body is done with a towel ma de of exquisite and precious divine cloth. This 
incomparable cloth or towel is ‘clean and well-anoi nted with scent’. We can visualize that the old, 
discarded garments of the merit field, together wi th the bath offering to wels, transforming into 
light. Some commentaries said th at we should visualise this light absorbing into the point 
between the eyebrows of oneself and all sentient beings. In other commentaries, it is mentioned 
that the discarded garments and the offered towels  absorb into the aspect of red light and yet 
other commentaries said that they  absorb in the aspect of yellow light. This doesn’t matter as we 
can choose any of these options.   The essence is that all the discarded garments and the offered towels absorb into light and 
dissolve at the point between the eyebrows of on eself and all sentient beings. After having 
perform the visualization of the discarded garments  and the offered towels dissolving  into light 
and absorbing at the point between the eyebrows of  oneself and all sentient  beings, at the end, 
we can think: “May I and all sentient beings achi eve the qualities of the ex alted wisdom of all the 
buddhas”. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 2 Clothing 
Then we make garment offerings after the offering of ablutions in the last two lines of verse 12: 
“And then may I present these Holy Beings with  fragrant garments of suitable colors”. ‘Of 
suitable colours’ means beautiful colours. There are different kinds of guests. For the members of 
the merit field who are in the aspect of fully ordained monks, we offer them the 3 saffron-
coloured robes. For the members of the guests wh o are in the aspect of the complete enjoyment 
body (sambhogakaya), we offer garm ents of different colours, shap e and of very soft and subtle 
material. We offer the necessary garments accordin g to the different aspects of the guests; some 
are in the aspect of the enjoyment body aspect  and some are in the aspect of the supreme 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
64 emanation body (nirmanakaya) .  
 
At the end of the garment offering s, we think that oneself and al l sentient beings have now put 
on the garments of shame and embarrassment. We al so make this prayer, “May I and all sentient 
beings become adorned with the garments of shame and embarrassment”. We put on the 
garment endowed with shame and embarrassment; what  we want to take off is the opposite of 
shame and embarrassment - what we do not want  is non-shame and non-embarrassment. Since 
this is the case, we should never put on the garment of non-shame and non-embarrassment. We 
need the two mental factors of shame and embarra ssment, therefore, metaphorically, we need to 
put on the garment adorned with shame and embarrassment.3 
 
At the end of offering garments to the guests of the merit field,  we make this prayer: “May I and 
all sentient beings always be adorned with excellent garments.” 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 3 Ornaments 
 [2,13] 
I adorn with manifold ornaments  
And various raiments fine and smooth,       
The Aryas Samantabhadra, Manjughosha,  
Avalokiteshvara and all the others. 
 
We have made garment offerings in the previous ve rse as raiments also mean clothes. This verse 
is making offerings of many suprem e ornaments. This is the practice  of offering a lot of different 
kinds of beautiful ornaments to the eight great bo dhisattvas (the eight close sons of the Buddha) 
such as Arya Samantabhadra, Manjughosha, Aval okiteshvara, Maitreya and others. When we see 
the representations of these eigh t bodhisattvas, we would be able to see that they are adorned 
with different kinds of ornaments such as earrings, necklaces, anklets, bracelets etc. At the end of 
making this ornaments offering, we think, “I and all sentient beings have now achieved the 
perfect signs and marks of the Bu ddha.” We think this way, and at  the same time, we also make 
the prayer:  “May I and all sentient beings achi eve the perfect signs and marks of the Buddha.” 
                                              
3 Shame is an avoidance of misdeeds on account of ones elf, embarrassment is an avoidance of misdeeds on 
account of others. What is the differe nce between shame and embarrassment? Although both of them are similar 
in terms of avoiding misconduct, shame is such that, when one is about to engage in misconduct, one avoids it, 
thinking, “This is not something I should do”. In the case  of embarrassment, when one comes close to engaging in 
misconduct, one avoids it on account of others, thinking, “I f others were to despise me [as a result of my engaging 
in misconduct], this would not be fitting.” One avoids misconduct due to concern about the displeasure of one’s 
gurus or teachers – the principal object [causing] this avoidance. ...If shame and embarrassment are absent, one will 
be unable to restrain oneself from any misconduct whatso ever, for, without fear about the fruition from one’s own 
side, and lacking concern about the displeasure of one’s gurus,  teachers, and so forth, there will be no way to cease 
misconduct. (from ABC Basic Program Mo dule 2 - Minds and Mental Factors)  
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
65 3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 4 Scents 
[2,14] 
Just like polishing pure, refined gold  
I anoint the Buddha's forms, which blaze with light,  
With the choicest perfumes, whose fragrance permeates  
A thousand million worlds. 
 
The literal translation is the offering of anoin tment. We offer different kinds of perfumes to 
different parts of the buddhas’ bodies. By anoi nting the buddhas’ holy bodies with different 
kinds of perfume, we should think and pray: “Due to these roots of virtue, may I and all  sentient 
beings obtain the golden complexion of the Buddh a’s holy body.” The Buddha’s body is in the 
colour of pure refined gold and this is  one of the holy signs of the Buddha. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 5 Flowers 
[2,15] 
And to the highest objects of giving, I offer  
Beautiful, well-arranged garlands,  
As well as enchanting, sweet-smelling flowers  
Such as lilies, jasmine and lotus blooms. 
 We offer different kinds of flowers, such as the utpala flowers, to the merit field sitting in front of us and we visualize scattering the flowers toward s each of the member of the merit field. In the 
root text, flower garlands are mentioned as well, so we offer flower garlands to the merit field 
that is  in the aspect of the complete enjoyment body. 
 In the commentary it is mentioned that it is ina ppropriate to offer flower garlands to the merit 
field in the aspect of the supreme emanation body  (nirmanakaya) because this is the aspect of 
non-attachment and non-desire. Because of this, we  should not offer flower garlands but instead 
we offer loose scattered flowers. We visualize th e scattered flowers transforming into the aspects 
of beautiful palaces, victory banners, parasols etc. , instead of just falling all over the place and 
making a mess. You might have noticed that previo usly we only talked about offering ornaments 
to the complete enjoyment bodies (sambhogakay a) and not to the supr eme emanation bodies 
(nirmanakaya). This is because the supreme emanation bodies are associated with non-
attachment and non-desire.   In one commentary, it is said that we should ma ke this prayer after we offer the flowers to the 
merit field: “May I and all sentient beings achiev e the 37 branches of enlightenment”. There is a 
whole set of qualities which are ca lled the 37 aspects of enlightenment or literally translated as 
the 37 branches/limbs of enlightenment. Within these 37 aspects there are different groups of 
different qualities; one group is called the eight- fold noble path (eight qualities belonging to 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
66 superior beings) and another group is called the seven branches (or seven limbs) of 
enlightenment. In the commentary, it is mentione d that after making the flower offerings, we 
should pray to achieve these seven branches of enlightenment.   However, I cannot find any explanation in the commentary on what is the connection between 
the practice of flower offerings and attaining thes e 7 branches of enlightenment. I think this may 
relate to the story of when Shakyamuni Buddha was born, when he walked seven steps and after 
each step, a flower appeared miraculously on th e ground. In total,  seven flowers appeared. At 
the end of the seven steps, the Buddha said,  “I am the supreme one and I will teach the Dharma 
in this world.” 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 6 Incense 
 [2,16] 
Also, I send forth clouds of incense  
Whose sweet aroma steals away the mind,  
As well as celestial delicacies  
Including a variety of foods and drinks 
 
The offering of incense is done in the first two li nes of  verse 16: “Also,  I send forth clouds of 
incense whose sweet aroma steals away the mind”.  The incense possesses a very sweet smelling 
aroma such that, when one comes in  contact with it, it captivates  the mind as mentioned in the 
words  “steals away the mind”. As said in the text, we offer “clouds of incense” and one  way of doing this is this: we can visualize  the incense  smoke rising to the sky like clouds. We can also 
interpret “‘clouds of incense” to mean many, in  terms of the number of incense offerings that 
one makes.   The entity of the incense is the smell itself. While retaining its entity, we can visualise that it 
takes on the aspect of different kinds of lette rs (eg. the syllables AH, HUM,...), vowels and 
consonants as well as transforming into the aspect s of the seven kinds of precious jewels that are 
carried by the wind to the ten directions, offeri ng  them to the buddhas and bodhisattvas.  The 
buddhas and bodhisattvas are pleased with this o ffering. This is one way of doing the offering.  
 Another way of doing this is according to the lite ral interpretation of “I send forth clouds of 
incense,” by visualizing clouds of incense smok e and making this offering to the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. At the same time, visualize that th e incense offering has the power to remove all 
mental anguish or suffering once one come s into contact with it or smells it.  
 When making this incense offering to the buddh as and bodhisattvas, think that any  sentient 
being who comes into contact with  this nice-smelling incense, immediately upon coming into 
contact with this offering, all th eir sufferings are eliminated and they are satisfied with great bliss 
and happiness. This visualization should be done  when we are making incense offerings. We 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
67 should put into practice what we have learnt here in class. It will be very good if we can perform 
the incense offering with this visualization.   
 
After making the incense offering, we should make this prayer :May I  and all sentient beings be 
adorned with the beautiful scent of morality th at pleases the holy minds of the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. There is a connection here. It is me ntioned, in reality, th at those who keep pure 
morality do smell nice naturally withou t need for putting on any anointment. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 7 Food 
The offering of food is done in the last two li nes of  verse 16: “As well as celestial delicacies 
including a variety of foods and drinks”.   We visualize different kinds of delicious foods of a hundred tastes. When we invite someone 
important, such as a king or minister to our ho me, we should offer them food and drink. In the 
same way, we should offer the best food and dr ink to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. At the 
end of a making the food and drink offering, we make the prayer: “May I and all sentient beings 
be sustained in future by the food of concentration”. Instead of living on gross food as we do as 
human beings, instead, we make a prayer that we may be sustained by the food of concentration.

 In the putline, we are at number eight from amon g the surpassable offerings, the first subdivision

 The light offering appears in the first two lines of verse 17: “I of fer them jewelled lamps arranged

within the golden lotuses are various kinds of pr ecious jewels such as vaidurya or lapis lazuli, 
which give out light. We offer these kinds of light.  Usually we offer butter lamps and oil lamps as light o fferings. This text says to visualize a very big 
container as big as the three thousand million world systems with a wick as big as Mount Meru. The amount of butter or oil is as much as the oc eans can contain. When we make light offerings,

3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 9 Offering an inestimable palace 
Next is the practice of offering an inestimable pa lace, which is the literal translation of “celestial 
mansion.” This outline is in accordance with G yaltsab Rinpoche’s commentary   but the outline 
of some other commentaries mentions “offering the ground” with a separate outline for “offering an inestimable palace”.  We will be following Gyaltsab Rinpoche’s commentary, which explains 
the last two lines of verse 17 and verse 18. 
[2,17cd] 
Upon land sprinkled with scented water 
I scatter delicate flower petals. 
 
[2,18 ] 
To those who have the nature of compassion, 
I offer palaces resounding with melodious hymns, 
Exquisitely illuminated by hanging pearls and gems 
That adorn the infinities of space. 
 
We first look at the practice of offering the ground. The ground is made of jewels, is very even and is anointed with sweet-smelling incense. Growing on the ground are different kinds of 
flowers of different colours. This is how we shou ld visualize when we are offering the ground to 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
69 all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. The jewelled ground  is very pleasant to walk upon. It is just 
like walking on a spongy surface. We offer this beautiful ground with the appropriate 
visualization to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas  and dedicate the merit and pray: Due to this 
merit, may I and all sentient beings achieve the mind generation like the earth. 
 If we look at bodhicitta in terms of the entity, it  is divided into two:- aspirational bodhicitta and 
engaged bodhicitta. If we look at  bodhicitta in terms of analogy (used to describe the different 
kinds or levels of mind generation), there are twenty-two examples. Of the twenty-two examples, 
the first one is called the mind generation of bo dhicitta like the earth, which is related to the 
Mahayana path of accumulation.  Next is the practice of offering an inestimabl e palace. The reason it is called an inestimable 
palace is because it is so huge that it cannot fit into our minds. It is obviously very beautiful and 
is made of different precious jewels. Inside th is inestimable palace or mansion, we should 
visualize many offering goddesses who hold in their hands musical instruments making beautiful music and singing with beautiful captivating voices . The different sides of the palace are adorned 
with hanging jewels. Light rays of different colo urs are emitted from these jewels that fill the 
entire space.  In short, we visualize light rays coming from th e inestimable palace adorned with jewels filling 
the entire space. We offer this to all the buddh as and bodhisattvas who are in the nature of 
compassion. After offering this inestimable palace  or mansion we pray: Due to this merit, may I 
and all sentient beings proceed to the city of grea t liberation. ‘Great liberation’ means the state of 
enlightenment or buddhahood. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 10 Offering Parasols 
Next is the offering of parasols in verse 19: 
[2,19] 
Eternally shall I offer to all the Buddhas 
Jewelled parasols with golden handles 
And exquisite ornaments embellished to the rims, 
Standing erect, and their sh apes beautiful to behold 
 Verse 19 is describing the type of parasols we should offer. We visualize that and we offer it to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. Afte r offering these parasols, we pray and dedicate that, due to 
this merit, may I and all sentient beings be free of the suffering of the  heat of cyclic existence in 
general and, specifically, the sufferings of the lower realms. Due to this merit, may I and all 
sentient beings come under the protection of the Three Jewels.  There is a historical account of how Brahma an d Indra offered 500 parasols made of different 
jewels, such as vaidurya, to the Buddha. The parasol protects us from  heat as we move under the 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
70 shadow of the parasol. If we un derstand the analogy, we will unde rstand this prayer. Sometimes, 
such parasols are offered to His Holiness wherever he goes. Traditionally, the parasol is offered to 
His Holiness and carried over him as he moves. Parasols are also traditionally offered to the 
Ganden Tripa, the throne holder of the Gelugpa tradition.   In short, we visualize beautiful and pleasant parasols and offer them to all the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. At the end, we make this prayer : May I and all sentient beings be free of the 
suffering of the heat of samsara and the lower realms. May all sentient beings come under the 
protection of the Three Jewels. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 11 Offering Music 
[ 20 ] 
In addition, may a mass of offerings 
Resounding with sweet and pleasing music, 
(Like) clouds that appe ase the misery of all, 
Each remaining (for as long as necessary) 
 
We are offering beautiful sounds, such as sweet and pleasing music coming from different musical instruments. This music is very powe rful. Immediately upon hearing this sound or 
music, all the sufferings of sentient beings are el iminated and their minds are satisfied.  We offer 
this type of sweet and pleasing music and pleasa nt sounds to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas 
and think that their holy minds are very pleased. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3A 12 An unbroken continuum of blessings in the form of offerings 
[2,21] 
And may a continuous rain 
Of flowers and precious gems descend 
Upon the reliquaries and the statues, 
And upon all the jewels of Dharma. 
 
“And upon all the jewels of Dh arma” means the Dharma Jewel which is the object of refuge and 
not referring to some jewels which are called th e jewels of dharma. There are the conventional 
Dharma Jewel and the ultimate Dharma Jewel. The conventional Dharma Jewel refers to the 
twelve categories of scriptures. The ultimate Dhar ma Jewel is the true paths and true cessations. 
 The other holy objects mentioned here are the re liquaries and the statues, which refer to the 
stupas that contain the relics of the buddhas and any paintings, statues or representations of the 
Buddha.   These are the objects to which we are going to ma ke the offering. The offe ring is the continuous 
rain of flowers and precious jewels. We think: “May this rain of flowers and jewels descend upon 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
71 all these holy objects continuously, without break, until the end of samsara”. In some 
commentaries, this is placed under the outline “Offerings arising from the power of prayers.” 
 
In short, we make this prayer: May this offering of flowers and jewels descend continuously upon the Three Jewels (referring to all the holy object s such as the Dharma Jewel, statues and stupas) 
until the end of samsara. We visualize a rain of flowers and jewels descending continuously upon 
the Three Jewels and make this prayer: May this rain of offerings continuously descend until the 
end of samsara.  In this text, when it talks about the making of actu al offerings, offerings that are actually set out, 
it does not talk about making offerings on an extensive scale. But when it comes to mentally 
emanated offerings, we are advised to vi sualize them in a very extensive way.  
 The reason is because the author here is the great bodhisattva, Shantideva. At that time 
Shantideva was abiding in the yoga without elaborat ion i.e. Shantideva lived in a very simple way, 
he did not have any jewels for offerings. If we can visualize making  offerings in the way 
explained earlier, we will accumulate a lot of me rit. This is how the wise ones, those who are 
skilled, make their offerings.  We should not look down or pay scant attention to the practice of mentally emanated offerings. 
There are a total of twelve kinds of mentally emanated offerings mentioned in this text. We 
should make these offerings as explained ea rlier continuously, over and over again.  
 As mentioned in the commentary, when we talk about the practice of deity yoga, whether we 
visualize ourselves as the deity or we visualize the deity in front of us, this practice  accumulates a 
lot of merit but this is something we have to do continuously. Comp aring that to this practice of 
making offerings, even if we we re to make those offerings as ex plained here just once, we will 
accumulate a lot of merit. These practices of making offerings accu mulate a lot of merit and  with 
less hardship. Even if we were to visualize these offerings to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas for 
just an instant in the mind, we will accumulate  a great amount of meri t. We will finish the 
accumulation of merit and wisdom and purify the ob scurations. This also he lps in our practice of 
listening, reflection and meditation.  Sometimes, we make offerings with what we actual ly own (external offering substances). If those 
offering substances are obtained through wrong livelihood or those substances are obtained 
through creating negative karma, then those o fferings that we offer will not become  pure 
offerings. Sometimes, these offerings are mingled wi th miserliness and pride. This is not the case 
when we make mentally emanated offerings as me ntally emanated offerings are said to be very 
vast, extensive and very pure.  
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
72 When we are doing some sort of practice everyd ay, we can perform the visualization of making 
offerings of these twelve kinds of mentally eman ated offerings. We can go through the offerings 
individually and then spend two or three minutes on  each of those offering s, visualizing it very 
extensively and making those offerings to the Th ree Jewels. If we do this, we will accumulate 
merit, purify our mind of obscurations and it also becomes the cause for our wishes to succeed. 
Ideally, when we recite the respective verses for the different kinds of offerings, we should stop 
for a while and do the appropriate extensive visu alization to the Three Jewels. After this, we 
move on to the next offering by reciting the corresponding verses. 
 
We should do this practice at our own pace and fa miliarize ourselves with this practice. If we do 
this practice, it will bring great benefit. We shou ld practice what we have learnt. The purpose of 
reciting different prayers is to have the meanin g of the prayers arise in our own mind. Here we 
are talking about offerings and the appropriate visu alization. If we are already familiar with the 
practice and are able to create this visualization in our own mind, then there is not much need to 
rely on the words of the prayers. We can si t quietly and perform this visualization.  
 Sometimes, when we are not doing anything or th e mind is spacing out or just thinking about 
this and that, an hour will just pass by. If we  do the appropriate visualization of the various 
offerings and make the relevant dedication prayers, even though we find an hour have passed by, we will reap the great benefit of doing this practi ce. When we do such pr actice of offerings and 
then finish the accumulation of merit, our mind s will become happy and we will achieve mental 
peace. When we talk about practicing the dharma , it is primarily done with the mind and not 
with the body and speech. This  is the connection between what is mentioned here and the 
practice of mentally emanated offerings as ment ally emanated offerings is done with the mind. 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3 Mentally emanated offerings 
A Surpassable offerings 
B Unsurpassable offerings 
3B2B 2A1A 1B3B  Unsurpassable offerings 
[2, 22 ] 
In the same way as Ma njughosha and others 
Have made offerings to the Conquerors, 
Similarly do I bestow gifts upon the Tathagatas, 
The Protectors, their Children and all. 
 
Great bodhisattvas, like Manjushri and Samantab hadra, emanate skies of offerings and offer 
them to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. This verse is saying, in the same way, I, like them, will 
emanate clouds of offerings filling the entire  space and offer it to all the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas.   
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
73 What kind of visualization is to be done here? First, we visualize the bodhisattva Samantabhadra. 
Then, we visualize, from the heart of this bodhisa ttva, emitting thousands of billions of light rays 
of different colours. The number of light rays is equal to the number of bu ddhas in existence. At 
the tip of each of these thousands of billions of  light rays emitted from the heart of bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra are replicas of bodhisattva Sama ntabhadra. From the heart of each of these 
replicas of bodhisattva Samantabhadra, again thou sands of billions of light rays are emitted from 
each heart. The offerings then come from these immeasurable manifestations of Samantabhadra. 
At the end, we have the whole sky filled with o fferings that  are made to  all the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas. This is how bodhisattva Samantabha dra made offerings to the buddhas. His way of 
making offerings is inconceivable and indescribable. 
 As mentioned in the verse, in the same way as bodhisattva Samantabhadra, we shall make 
offerings, filling the entire space, to all the bu ddhas and bodhisattvas. This is the practice of 
making unsurpassable offerings.  Previously, we  talked about meditating  on great compassion 
and meditating on bodhicitta.  Those practices are also considered unsurpassable offerings. 
 The method of making offerings like bodhisattva Samantabhadra: here this visualization is not 
done from our own heart but we visualize ligh t rays coming from the heart of bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra. Before that, we have to visualize first the objects to which we want to make the 
offerings – i.e. all the buddhas an d bodhisattvas in the space in front of us. Then we visualize 
bodhisattva Samantabhadra. If we don’t know what he looks like, we can visualise a figure in the 
bodhisattva aspect and think that th at is bodhisattva Samantabhadra.  
 Then we visualize thousands of billions of ligh t rays emitting from the heart of bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra. At the tip of each  of the light rays are replicas  of bodhisattva Samantabhadra. 
Again, from the heart of those replicas of bodh isattva Samantabhadra emit thousands of billions 
of light rays. At the tips of those emitted light rays are manifestations of bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra. From the heart of those mani fested bodhisattva Samantabhadra come forth 
clouds of offerings and those offerings are offered to all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. We should attempt to make this visualization clearer by familiarizing with it. This is how bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra made offerings. We should emulat e him and make offerings in the same way to 
all the buddhas and bodhisattvas. This is the reason why we visualize bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra.  Different great bodhisattvas have their own charac teristics and special features. We are told that 
Chenrezig is the nature of compassion, Manjushri is the nature of wisdom and Samantabhadra is 
famed for the practice of making offerings that arise through the power of his prayers. 
Bodhisattva Samantabhadra is famed for making extensive prayers and the offerings he made 
arose through the power of his prayers. Because of  this reason, in this context, we visualize 
bodhisattva Samantabhadra. Bodhisattva Samantabhadra composed the King of Prayers.  
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
74  
The practice of making extensive offerings like  bodhisattva Samantabhadra makes the mind big. 
When our mind is getting narrow, by doing this visualization, it will help to make the mind 
bigger. If we do such visualization in our own room and is able to sustain it, even though the room is small, we will feel that we have arrived in another big world. There are some people who lock themselves in a small room, surrounded by the four walls.  This condition makes their 
minds very small. This visualization may help them to make their minds more spacious. The 
main thing here is that by doing such visualiz ation, one can accumulate huge amounts of merit 
in an instant.   Here, we have finished the outline on offerings [2A1A a]

“With limitless verses of harmonious praise”: wi th very enchanting beautiful voices, we make

Lesson 10 – chapter 2, verse 24-26 
 We have already finished with the section on me ntally emanated offerings, of which there are 
twelve mentally emanated surpassable offerings, and the unsurpassable offerings.  For those who have time, one should familiarise oneself with those different kinds of offerings 
and try to make those offerings with one’s mind by  visualising them; try to create images of them 
and make those offerings. As said in the comment ary, just by making those emanated offerings 
even for a single instant, one can accumulate a lot of merit.  For the practice of unsurpassable offerings, we should think,  “In the way the great bodhisattva 
Samantabhadra made offerings by emanating countl ess replicas of himself and making offerings 
filling the entire space, to all the buddhas and bo dhisattvas, so, likewise, in the same way, I would 
like to make offerings, just like him.”  Samantabhadra is a bodhisattva and not only a bodhisattva but an arya bodhisattva, someone 
who is abiding on the grounds. If such a bodhis attva makes such extensiv e offerings to all the 
buddhas and bodhisattvas, it goes without saying th at we ordinary beings have to make  extensive 
offerings to them too.  
Bodhisattvas like Samantabhadra make such extensive offerings in order to finish the 
accumulation of merit. They make emanated o fferings filling the entire space in order to 
accumulate vast amounts of merit. Relying on the factor of wisdom alone is not sufficient. They 
have not yet achieved the final goal, although th ey are bodhisattvas, beca use they still have the 
obstructions to omniscience i.e. they have obsc urations in their mind that prevent them from 
having complete knowledge of all phenomena. In order to remove such obstructions to 
omniscience, they need to accumu late vast stores of merit. Bodhisattvas have to do that.  
 
Since  this is the case for these high level bodhisa ttvas, then it goes without saying that for us as 
o r d i n a r y  b e i n g s ,  w h o  a r e  b o u n d  b y  a f f l i c t i o n s ,  s o  w e  h a v e  t o  s t r i v e  i n  t h e  p r a c t i c e  o f  m a k i n g  offerings, like the bodhisattvas , by mentally emanating skies of  offerings and by making these 
offerings continuously, day and night non-stop.   The reason why we need to make extensive offerings is already stated in the first line of verse 1 of 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
76 chapter two. “In order to seize that precious mi nd” means in order to generate this precious 
mind; this precious mind is bo dhicitta, the altruistic intention to achieve enlightenment for the 
benefit of all sentient beings. In order to genera te this precious mind within our own continua, 
we need to make these offerings. It is a very difficult thing  to generate bodhicitta especially if we 
do not purify our minds of obscurations; obviously, it is going to be difficu lt. What prevent us or 
create obstacles for us from developing bodhicitta  are the obscurations and negativities which we 
have accumulated. We need to purify those nega tive obscurations, and we need to accumulate 
the collection of merit that will provide the favo urable conditions for developing bodhicitta.  
 So we need to gather the necessary conditions and eliminate the opposing ones. Therefore, we 
need to make these offerings, different kinds of offerings to the Buddh a, Dharma and Sangha, 
the Three Jewels. The text speaks of many different kinds of offeri ngs, that is, offering unowned 
substances, offering one’s own body and the practi ce of mentally emanated offerings. We have 
already dealt with those, we have completed the outline on offerings. We are now at the outline 
of offering homage. 
3B2B 2A1A 2 Homage 
A Verbally expressing homage 
B Physically prostrating 
 
When we talk about homage or prostrations, there are different kinds of prostrations - 
prostrations that are done with the body, sp eech and mind. Based on the outline of this 
commentary, first is the practice of making prostr ations with one’s speech or paying homage that 
involves reciting praises to the buddhas and bodh isattvas. With  melodious and beautiful voices, 
pleasant tunes and with  minds of faith, we offer these by reciting different verses of praise. There 
are many different kinds of praises that one can recite and offer to the Three Jewels. There are 
some praises that use the names of the buddhas and there are some prayers that talk about the 
qualities of the buddhas, their compassion, wisd om and power. There are many different kinds 
of prayers and verses of different praises that we  can do. Whatever pr aises we know, we can 
recite them with a melodious tune and offer th em to the buddhas and bodhisattvas and we can 
think: “Due to all these praises, the buddhas experience great bliss in their holy minds.”  
 
The next outline deals with the physical prostrations or paying physical homage. 
3B2B 2A1A 2B Physically prostrating 
1 [To the] Three Jewels ∗ 
2 The basis for the arising of bodhicitta and so forth 
3 Prostrating to the abbot, acharya, master and so forth 
3B2B 2A1A 2B1 [To the] Three Jewels 
The practice of paying physical homage to  the Three Jewels is shown in verse 24: 
 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
77 [2, 24 ] 
With bodies as numerous 
As all the atoms within the universe, 
I prostrate to all the Buddhas of the three times, 
The Dharma and the Supreme Community. 
 
We make physical homage to the Three Jewels: th e buddhas of the three times (past, present and 
future), the Dharma Jewel (comprising of the sc riptural Dharmas and the realisational Dharmas) 
and the Sangha Jewel which refers to the suprem e community. These are the objects to whom we 
make physical prostrations.  
 How should we perform such a prostration? We visualise making prostrations with bodies as 
numerous as all the atoms that can be found in th e universe of the ten directions. We then show 
our respect by putting both of our hands togeth er in the mudra of prostration and pay homage 
to the Three Jewels.  There are two kinds of physical prostration: th e short prostration involves touching the five 
p o i n t s  o f  o n e ’ s  b o d y  t o  t h e  g r o u n d  ( t w o  h a nds, two knees and the forehead) and the long 
prostration is, basically, laying  one’s body flat on the floor. 
 What about making mental prostrations? While on e is physically prostrating, one’s mind is 
reflecting on the qualities of the Three Jewels. When we reflect on the qualities of the Three 
Jewels, then faith and respect will arise for th em. Within this mental state, we perform the 
prostration. 
3B2B 2A1A 2B2 The basis for the arising of bodhicitta and so forth 
[2, 25 ] 
Likewise I prostrate to all the reliquaries, 
To the bases of Awakening Mind, 
To all learned abbots and masters 
And to all the noble practitioners. 
 Literally, we are physically prostrating to the bases for the generation of bodhicitta. This is 
indicated in the first two lines of verse 25: “Likewis e I prostrate to all the reliquaries, to the bases 
of the awakening mind”. Literally, ‘reliquaries’ refe rs to the base of offerings. What is meant by 
prostrating to the bases for the generation of bodhicitta? We ge nerate bodhicitta within our 
mental continuum; what is it that helps us to generate bodhicitta or what are some of the 
conditions? They are the representations of the Buddha’s holy body, speech and mind such as 
the physical representation of a statue of the Buddha, the Mahayana script ure etc. These are the 
bases for the generation of bodhic itta, so we make prostrations to them. One pays homage with 
physical prostrations to the person who is the condition for us to develop bodhicitta in our 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
78 mental continua as well as the place where we develop bodhicitta. 
3B2B 2A1A 2B3 Prostrating to the abbot, acharya, master and so forth 
This is shown in the last two lines of verse 25: “ To all learned abbots and masters and to all the 
noble practitioners”. “To all learned abbots and masters”: we make prostrations to the abbots 
and the masters. Here the word is “masters” but th e Tibetan word is lopon, which in Sanskrit is 
acharya. The abbots and masters or acharyas refe r to those who conferred on us ordination vows 
of monks and nuns be they novice vows or vows of full ordination. There is a person who acts as 
the abbot in the ceremony. There is also an ac harya who performs the ac t of transmitting or 
giving the vows. We make prostrations to these two persons.  “To all the noble practitioners”: in Tibetan, the noble practitioners are called  tulshug,  which 
literally means someone who has abandoned the form er conduct of a lay life and now engages in 
the lifestyle or conduct of ordination or becomes an ordained person. Tulshug can also be interpreted from another perspective: it can refer to any practitioner or person who is correctly 
following the footsteps of the Buddha, i.e. taki ng the Buddha as the example and doing what has 
been done by the Buddha. From this perspective, this can include lay people as well. Of course 
ordained people are still included. Whether as l ay practitioners or ordained persons, they have 
abandoned the way and the mode of conduct of non-Buddhists and adopted the way or mode of 
conduct of Buddhists.  In short, we make prostrations to all the abbots , masters and to all practitioners, those who are 
following in the footsteps of the buddhas. We are saying that any person who is a follower of the 
Buddha, who is within the communi ty of Buddhist practitioners, becomes an object of homage, 
an object of prostration of one’s body, speech and mind. This implies mutual respect for anyone 
belonging to this group.   We are like one family because all of us are the followers of the same teacher and it is important 
to have mutual respect and to practise pure perception towards one another, as mentioned 
earlier at the end of chapter one. When we talked  about the need to practise perfect perception, 
the advice is “to see all sentient beings as the Buddha”.   When we put our hands together in the mudra of prostration, our two palms should not be 
pressed completely flat against each other. It is  said that making prostr ations with both palms 
completely pressed against each other is actually  the tradition of non-Bu ddhists. We should place 
the palms of our hands together to form the shap e of a lotus, leaving some space in the middle. 
Buddha explained this practice in the Great Nirvana Sutra . We should put our palms together, 
making the shape of a blossomed lotus with spac e in the middle. We are advised to visualise 
holding a wish-fulfilling jewel inside the palms, with the thumbs touching and tucked inside the 
palms.

79 When we prostrate, we should straighten ou r  b o d i e s  u p r i g h t  w h e n  w e  g e t  u p  f r o m  e a c h  
prostration. If possible, we should touch our fo reheads, throats and hearts with both of our 
palms in the mudra of the blossoming lotus. Firs t, we touch our crowns with our palms. At the 
same time, we contemplate the qualities of the ho ly body of  the Buddha. We generate faith by 
doing this. Then, we touch our palms together to our throats and reflect on the qualities of the 
holy speech of the Buddha and generate faith. Fi nally, we touch both of our palms to our hearts 
and, at that time, we contemplate the qualities of the Buddha’s holy mind . This is a method for 
us to generate faith in the Buddha. 
 At the same time when we touch our palms to our crowns, we make the prayer: ‘May I and all 
sentient beings achieve all the qualities of the Buddha’s holy body.’ When we touch our palms to 
our throat, we make the prayer, ‘May I and all sentie nt beings achieve all the qualities of the holy 
speech of the Buddha.’ When we touch our palms to  our heart, we make the prayer, ‘May I and 
all sentient beings achieve the qual ities of the Buddha’s holy mind.’ 
 There are some who contemplate, when they touch their palms to the crowns, that all negativities 
they have accumulated with their bodies are comp letely purified. When they touch their palms to 
their throats, they visualise that  all negativities they have a ccumulated with their speech are 
purified. When they touch their palms to their hearts , they visualise that all negativities they have 
accumulated with their minds are purified.   When we perform short prostrations, there are five  points of our body touching the ground: the 
two knees, the two palms and the forehead. When these five points of our bodies touch the 
ground, we make the prayer, “May I and all sentient  beings be freed from the five obscurations.” 
  It is extremely important to prostrate correctly. Th e number of prostrations is not that important 
but it is important that the quality of the prostr ation is done very well. We should not rush but 
contemplate. As already explained earlier, we ca n think of many things when we prostrate. We 
should not do them hastily. When we do prostr ations, we should remember those visualizations 
and do them with very clear minds. We sh ould do them with a mind of faith.  
 It is important to do prostrations that are of good quality. It is said that it is better to do good 
quality prostrations than many but mindless pr ostrations. Compared to making many of those 
mindless prostrations, where the mind is not thinking of anything, doing a fewer number of good prostrations creates far more merit – immeasur able merit. In fact there are many things to 
think about when we touch the three points of ou r body with our palms. If we do them very 
quickly, there is  nothing to co ntemplate as there is not enough time. We should do them  at a 
comfortable pace.   When we put our palms together at our crowns, we  reflect on the inconceivable qualities of the 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
80 Buddha’s holy body. When we touch our palms to  our throats, we think of the inconceivable 
qualities of the Buddha’s holy speech. The ho ly speech of the Buddha is said to possess 60 
different qualities. When we touch our palms to our hearts, we think of the inconceivable 
qualities of the Buddha’s holy mind. For exampl e, how the Buddha is someone who has perfect 
compassion. We do these prostrations with faith and make this prayer, “How wonderful it would 
be if I and all sentient beings ac hieve these inconceivable qualities.” 
 
By doing this reflection, we do long prostrations  with our whole bodies in contact with the floor. 
One will accumulate a lot of merit. The temporal  benefit is that however many number of atoms 
is covered by our bodies on the ground, that is the number of rebirths as a wheel turning king for which  we have created the causes  for  in the future. Ultimately, these prostrations act as the  
causes for us to achieve the state of full enlighte nment. Whether we experience those benefits or 
not depends on how well we do our prostrations , such as contemplating the qualities of the 
body, speech and mind of the Buddha with a mind of  faith. If we were to do the  prostrations 
with all these contemplations, we will ob tain all these benefits in the future. 
 While we are prostrating, we visualise our fath ers on the right, our mothers on our left, our 
enemies in front of us, and surrounding them are all other sentient beings. We visualise we are 
making prostrations together. We visualise making  prostrations with all  sentient beings and 
prostrating to the Buddha, Dharma  and Sangha, the Three Jewels.  
3B2B 2A1A Explaining the chapter’s material

3B2B 2A1A 3 Refuge 
A Causal refuge B Resultant refuge 
  
Both causal and resultant refuges are covered in verse 26: 
 
[2, 26 ] 
I seek refuge in all the Buddhas 
Until I possess the essence of Awakening; 
I seek refuge in Dharma 
And in the assembly of Bodhisattvas. 
 
The meaning of refuge is this: one makes the me ntal commitment to take refuge in the Three 
Jewels, motivated or driven by the desire to be  free from fear and suffering. On the basis of 
having fear and wanting to be free from sufferi ng, one makes the mental promise or commitment 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
81 to the Three Jewels. The entity of refuge is making the mental commitment or promise to the 
Three Jewels on the basis of having a wish to be free from suffering and having fear. 
 
Before undertaking this commitment, it has to be  preceded by two causes of refuge. There are 
two causes of refuge: the fear of having to unde rgo the sufferings of cyclic existence in general 
and the suffering of the lower realms specifical ly and combined together with the faith of 
conviction in the Three Jewels’ ability to protec t us from those fears. These two causes must 
precede the mental promise or mental commitme nt which is the real entity of refuge.  
 In the outline on refuge, there is causal refuge and resultant refuge. 
3B2B 2A1A 3A Causal refuge 
What is causal refuge? We are going for refuge  to something that is external and not found 
within us and is not part of our consciousnesses. We are taking refuge in something that  can be 
found in the mental continua of those who ha ve already accomplished (or established or 
achieved) the Three Jewels themselves. With th at as the basis, we make a commitment or 
promise. We make the commitment or promise to somebody.   We are making a commitment to something that exists outside of our mental continua; an 
external object i.e. the external Three Jewels . We have Shakyamuni Buddha who has achieved 
enlightenment. We have the Dharma Jewel that  refers to the true paths and true cessations. 
Then we have the Sangha Jewel which refers to the arya bodhisattvas and Superior Beings like Manjushri or the 8 close sons of the Buddha. We hold them as objects of refuge to protect us from our fear and suffering. We take them as the base for generating this mental commitment, 
making this promise based on the desire  to be free from suffering and fear.  
 What are the objects of resultant refuge? The object s of resultant refuge are the Three Jewels that 
have yet to be achieved by oneself but which will be achieved by oneself in the future. They are 
the Three Jewels that one will become (one will manifest, one will achieve) in the future. These 
become the objects of refuge or the base for deve loping this mental promise. We are saying, ‘ I 
am making a mental promise or commitment to become the Three Jewels.’ The practice of 
resultant refuge is actually going for refuge in th e Three Jewels that we wi ll become in the future. 
We are relying on them as the base.  Aspirational bodhicitta is generating the intent ion to achieve enlightenment for sentient beings. 
When one generates aspirational bodhicitta , one makes a commitment, ‘ I will become a 
Buddha; I will achieve enlightenment for the bene f i t  o f  a l l  s e n t i e n t  b e i n g s . ’  T h a t  k i n d  o f  
intention is aspirational bodhicitta and it is also resultant refuge.  
 Aspirational bodhicitta is an act of generating  the intention to achieve enlightenment for the 
benefit of all sentient beings and, at the same ti me, it is also a practice of resultant refuge. In 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
82 reality they are one. Aspirational bodhicitta is  both. If we isolate them conceptually, we can 
designate them with different names.  
 
If we are looking at aspirational bodhicitta from  the perspective of “for the benefit of sentient 
beings, I will achieve enlightenment,” it is given the name generating aspirational bodhicitta or 
mind generation.  If we look at aspirational bodhicitta from the pe rspective of making a promise that “I will achieve 
enlightenment for the benefit of sentient beings”,  then it is given the name resultant refuge.  
 Actually they (mind generation and resultant refuge) are both the same mind; we are just 
isolating them conceptually. We are talking about th e same thing; it is just that they are being 
given different names depending on how we look at it.   If you look at refuge from the perspective of mo tivation, you can have Hinayana refuge (refuge of 
individual liberation vehicle) and Mahayan a refuge (refuge of the great vehicle). 
 With further elaboration, we can also have the re fuge that is associated with the persons of the 
three different capacities, small, medium and gr eat. For persons of small capacity, the person 
makes the commitment saying: “I will go for refu ge to the Buddha, Dhar ma and Sangha for the 
rest of this life out of fear of the suffering of  the lower realms.” The person of medium capacity 
has a slightly different motivation for going fo r refuge. That person makes a commitment to go 
for refuge to the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha for as long he lives out of the fear of having to undergo the sufferings of cyclic existence. The person of grea t capacity makes the commitment 
saying,  ‘I go for refuge until I am enlightene d to the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha motivated by 
the fear of falling into the extr eme of cyclic existence and the extreme of individual peace or 
liberation, for oneself and all sentient beings.’  There is another kind of refuge called worldly refuge. This is the refuge we take. It is called worldly refuge as it is associated with the affair s or happiness of this life. One goes for refuge to 
the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha out of fear of sickness, obstacles and problems in this life 
alone. In some texts, it is uncertain whether this kind of mental attitude or this kind of refuge is a refuge or not - one needs to chec k. This is because it is not on e of the three that are mentioned 
above. At the very least, in order for our refuge to become refuge we have to be motivated by a 
fear of suffering of the lower realms.  In the food offering prayer, Buddha is the un surpassed (which means no n-higher) teacher. The 
unsurpassed teacher is the Buddha. The unsurpa ssed refuge is the Dharma and the unsurpassed 
guide is the Sangha.

83 Lesson 11 – chapter 2, verse 26-31 
 
It is important for us to set the correct motivati on for listening to the te achings. This time, we 
have  attained a human life of leisure and opportunity and we have also met the teaching of  the 
Buddha which is difficult to meet . At such a time, when all the outer and inner conditions are 
present and complete, we need to prac tise the teachings of the Buddha.  
 Before we can practise the teachings of the Buddha, we need to know how to go about doing such a practice. In order to understand the way to  practise the teachings of the Buddha, we first 
need to receive the teachings. We need to listen to the teachings. It is for this reason that we 
n e e d  t o  e n g a g e  i n  t h i s  s e s s i o n .  W e  s h o u l d  r e f l e c t  o n  h o w  f o r t u n a t e  i t  i s  f o r  u s  t o  h a v e  t h i s  opportunity to actually engage in the activity of  listening to the Buddhadharma. We should try to 
generate a sense of happiness and enthusiasm.  In the last session, we looked at  the practice of making prostrat ions. We can make prostrations 
with our bodies, speech and minds. With our speec h, we offer praises or recite praises with a 
melodious voice. With the mind, one reflects on  the qualities of the Buddha and generates a 
sense of faith in them. With one’s body, one can do the short prostration that involves touching 
the five points of one’s body to the ground  or one can do the full length prostration. 
 Before we make any prostration, we sh ould recite the prostration mantra:  
Om namo manjushriye namo sush riye namo uttama shriye soha  
 It is taught that there is inconceivable benefit if we recite this mantra wh en we prostrate. If we 
prostrate after reciting this prostration mantra, ev ery prostration we perfor m  is equivalent to one 
thousand prostrations. The benefit of prostratin g with this mantra is similar to hearing and 
reflecting on the meanings of th e three scriptural collections. It is said that if we prostrate 
continuously after reciting this mantra, we can achi eve the path of seeing in this very life itself. 
 Whether we  benefit in this way from prostratin g, as mentioned in the previous lesson, depends 
on how well we perform our prostrations. The qual ity of the prostration is most important. I do 
not think that the number of prostrations is the main thing. It is the quality that determines 
whether we will obtain the benefit earlier mentio ned . A lot of contemplation is involved when 
we perform a prostration - what do we need to  contemplate when we place  our palms at our 
crowns, at our throats and at our hearts?   When we perform a  prostration, th e main thing is to generate fait h in the Three Jewels. If we are 
able to perform our prostrations with this kind of reflection, then we will be able to obtain all the 
benefits mentioned before. The text also mentions  temporal benefits such as pacifying sicknesses 
and harms. It is said that we should also visualise countless replicas of ourselves when 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
84 prostrating. If we are able to sustain our visualisation, and keep our minds focussed on what we 
need to do with our bodies, speech and minds th roughout the prostrations, then we will gain 
immeasurable benefits. Usually when we perform pr ostrations, we are only performing them with 
our bodies,  but our minds are distracted and thinking  of  something else. Yes, it is true that we 
can still accumulate merit from performing the pros trations with our bodies but if we can sustain 
our focus on performing  proper prostrations with our bodies, speech and minds, then obviously the merit we accumulate will be much greater.   We are performing prostrations everyday and even if we cannot do  many of them at the moment, 
we can, at least, make three pros trations in the morning  and three prostrations at night. In this 
way, we  perform  six prostratio ns every day. We should make a commitment  to do this number 
of prostrations as a daily minimum . When we perform prostrations, we should not feel that it is an obligation, like paying taxes. We should in stead contemplate and understand  the benefits 
and prostrate voluntarily from our own side to  the Three Jewels. Even though we are only 
making six prostrations a day, multiplied by whatever number of days we have left in this life,  by the end of this life, we will have accumulated thousands of prostrations.   Usually, when we are asked about the practice of prostration, we explain how  we do it with our bodies in such and such a way -  this is only a limited explanation. The whole purpose of studying 
this is to understand this practi ce so that when someone asks us what is a prostration and why we 
are doing it, we shall be able to explain to them the different ways of performing prostrations, involving not only the body but also the speech and the mind .  
3B2B 2A1A 3 Refuge 
A Causal refuge 
B Resultant refuge 
 
What is the meaning of refuge? Refuge is a ment al promise or commitment that is made on the 
basis of the desire to be free from suffering an d fear.  In order for our mental commitment or 
promise to be pure and strong, we mu st possess the two causes of refuge. 
 
Before undertaking this commitment, it has to be  preceded by two causes of refuge. There are 
two causes of refuge: the fear of having to unde rgo the sufferings of cyclic existence in general 
and the suffering of the lower realms specifically, combined together with the faith of conviction 
in the Three Jewels’ ability to protect us from those fears. These two causes must precede the 
mental promise or mental commitment which is the real entity of refuge.  Without the two causes of refuge, one’s mental commitment or one’s refuge cannot be pure and 
strong; one’s refuge is only at the level of wo rds. What we are discussing here is basic and 
therefore accords with this ‘Basic’ Program. There are some of us who have been calling ourselves 
Buddhists for many years and say,  “I have re ceived my refuge vows”, and might have even 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
85 received the bodhisattva and tantric vows, but if we were asked, “What is  refuge? What is the 
entity of refuge? What are the different kinds of refuge? Why do we go for refuge?” we would  be 
stuck for answers to these questions. 
  As discussed in the previous lesson, there is causal refuge and resultant refuge. 
3B2B 2A1A 3A Causal refuge 
What is the object of refuge in the context of causal refuge? It is something that is external to one’s mental continuum. We are go ing for refuge to the Three Jewe ls, which are external to our 
mental continua. This means the Three Jewels that  have already been achieved or are present in 
the mental continuum of someone other than oneself.  We have the fear and the desire to be free 
from suffering. With that as the bases, one go es for refuge to the Three Jewels with the 
conviction in their ability to protect us from  those fears. That constitutes causal refuge. 
3B2B 2A1A 3B Resultant refuge 
What are the objects of refuge in the context of resultant refuge? It is the Three Jewels that one 
will become and achieve in the future. Again, the ba ses of refuge are the fear of and the desire to 
be free from suffering. One then goes for refuge to  the Three Jewels that one will become in the 
future with the conviction that those Three Jewels  have the ability to protect oneself and others 
from suffering. By recognizing this, one then makes a mental commitment. That constitutes the 
resultant refuge.  Discussion: The Three Jewels that we will become in  future, do they exist or  not? If they do not 
exist, then they are not objects of refuge. An d the Buddha that one will become in the future, 
does that exist or not? If yes, is that a Buddha or  a sentient being? If it is a Buddha, shouldn’t it 
exist now?  Let us take the example of Bu ddha Shakyamuni or any other pa st buddhas . Those buddhas are 
the teachers of refuge. The true path and the true cessation that one will achieve in the future is 
the Dharma Jewel. That is the actual refuge. Th e superior beings and superior bodhisattvas are 
the Sangha Jewel. They assist us in the practice of refuge. Causal refuge and resultant refuge do not apply here as both are mixed together.  [In response to a question from a student:] It is  possible that causal refuge may arise in one’s 
continuum before resultant refuge but it is not ne cessarily so. It is possible that resultant refuge 
arises first. One makes the commitment based on  the understanding that  one has the potential 
to become the Three Jewels. In that case, resultant refuge arises first.  If we look at refuge from the perspective of mo tivation, then we have: refuge associated with 
persons of small capacity, refuge associated with persons of medium capacity and refuge 
associated with persons of great capacity. These are different kinds of refuge based on different 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
86 motivations. And even if one’s objects of refuge  are the Three Jewels wh o have already achieved 
their own levels of perfection, but if one’s motiva tion for taking refuge is worldly, then one’s 
practice of refuge will not be included in any of the practices of refuge listed above. 
 With reference to the Sugata (the One Gone to Bliss, or the Buddh a), there are different 
meanings of the Buddha depending on whether we are explaining it  from the perspective  of the 
Hinayana or the Mahayana. They both have thei r own  explanation for  the term ‘sugata’. 
According to the Hinayana, the Buddha’s body is  not the Buddha Jewel. Why? This is because 
the Buddha’s body is the same body that he had before he achieved enlightenment. According to 
the lower tenets, the Buddha achieved enlighte nment in one sitting. From the moment he 
entered the path of preparation, he went th rough the different paths until he achieved 
enlightenment. Since that is believed to be the case, the body of the Buddha is true suffering. 
Since it is true suffering, it cannot be a Buddha Je wel. The Hinayana posits the realizations in the 
continuum of the Buddha as the Bu ddha Jewel. This refers specific ally to the true paths in the 
mental continuum of the Buddha.  The same applies to the Sangha Jewel. When we talk about the sangha, we have the sangha who 
are trainees and sangha who are non-trainees (ie. they have gone beyond or have completed their  
training). Their holy bodies again are not posite d as the Sangha Jewel, according to the Hinayana 
because they are deemed to be true suffering. According to the lower or Hinayana tenets, the realisations, specifically the true  paths, in the mental continua of the sangha (both trainees and 
beyond trainees) are posited as the Sangha Jewel. That is the object of refuge.  
 What about the Dharma Jewel? The Dharma Jewe l is posited as true cessation that is the 
abandonment of any obscurations, which can eith er be an obstruction to liberation or an 
obstruction to omniscience. The true cessation is  the abandonment of any afflictions (be it an 
obstruction to liberation or an obstruction to  omniscience) in the mental continuum of a 
Superior Being. That state of abandonment that is  true cessation is posited as the Dharma Jewel. 
 That is how  the Three Jewels are posited by the  Hinayana.  According to the Mahayana, the complete enjoym ent body and the supreme emanation body of 
the Buddha are posited as the Buddha Jewel. The true paths and true cessations in the mental 
continua of the  superior beings are posited as  the Dharma Jewel. The superior bodhisattvas 
abiding on the grounds are posited as the Sangha Jewel.  We have the unsurpassed teache r who is the Buddha, the unsurp assed refuge, the Dharma and 
the unsurpassed guide,  the Sangha. Of thes e three, the Dharma is  the actual refuge. 
 The Buddha said, “The Dharma is the actual refuge. I have already shown you the path to 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m                C h a p t e r  T w o  
 
 
87 liberation. Whether you achieve the path of libera tion or not depends on your effort. We must 
actualise the actual refuge ourselves. That is wh at will protect us from our fear and suffering”.  
 
When we actualise the Dharma Jewel, we will achi eve liberation,  the state that is completely free 
from all sufferings. Whether we can achieve the state of liberation or not depends on our own 
effort. If we did not  achieve that state of libera tion, we would never be free from suffering. If we 
did not  actualise the actual refuge, the Dharma , then neither the Buddh a nor the Sangha Jewel 
could do anything about our suffering.   The Buddha has said he could not wash away our negativities with water. He could not  remove our suffering with his hands. He protects us by showing us the true meaning of reality, 
emptiness. Freeing ourselves from the sufferings of the lower realms and cyclic existence is not 
something that can be achieved by prayers alone. We need to engage in virtue that can actually 
bring about freedom from those sufferings. We can take the pratimoksha vows or the 8 
Mahayana Precepts. When we take these precepts and keep them purely, we are abiding in pure 
morality. It is this practice of pure morality th at protects us from the fear and suffering of the 
lower realms.   The Buddha is posited as an object of refuge because, in order for us to enter the path, we must be shown the correct path. It is the Buddha wh o performs that function of showing us the 
unmistaken path. Because of this, the Buddh a is  posited as an object of refuge. 
  It is the same with the sangha. Wh y is the sangha  posited as an object of refuge? In order for us 
to actualise the practice of refuge, we need a helper and friend. Not just anyone but a good helper and friend to help us in the practice of refuge. Because of this, the Sangha Jewel is posited 
as the object of refuge. 
 
The Buddha is the founder or teacher of refuge. The Dharma Jewel is th e true paths and true 
cessations,  which is the actual refuge. More impo rtantly, the true paths and true cessations that 
we will attain in the future is the actual refuge. In order to actualise this refuge, we need friends 
or people to support  us along the way. This is where the Sangha Jewel comes in.

3B2B 2A1A 4 Confessing negativities by means of completing the four powers 
A A general presentation 
B An extensive explanation 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

88 3B2B 2A1A 4A A general presentation 
Next is the practice of confession through appl ying the four powers: the power of eradication, 
the power of applying remedies, the power of turning away from faults and the power of the 
foundation or support.  From the very start, it is important for us to try our best not to be stained by any negativity. As we 
are completely under the power and influence of  the three mental poisons, we accumulate 
negativities and negative actions. When this  happe ns, we need to purify those negativities. It is 
s a i d  t h a t  e v e n  v e r y  p o w e r f u l  o r  v e r y  h e a v y  n e g a tivities – even those negative actions that will 
usually bring about very quick suffering results - can be purified through the proper application 
of the four opponent powers.   
There are many reasons why negativities can be pu rified. This is because the nature of the mind 
is clear light. The mind by nature is free from afflictions and has never been polluted by them. 
The afflictions are adventitious. When an antidote is applied, they can be removed. These are 
some of the reasons that prove that negativities can be purified.   The first opponent power is the power of eradic ation. Sometimes, the power of eradication is 
translated as the power of regret, but in Tibetan, it is translated as the power of eradication,  which means generating regret for the negative actions that one has done.

1 An extensive explanation of the first power 
2 The second power: The power of the support 
3 The third power: The power of applying the antidote at all times 
4 The fourth power: The power of turning away from wrongdoing

3B2B 2A1A 4B1A 1 Requesting the attention of the object to whom you confess negativities 
[2, 27 ] 
With folded hands I beseech 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds
