6. Abiders in the state of a never returner,  
7. Approachers to the state of a foe destroyer, and  
8. Abiders in the state of a foe destroyer.  
 A stream enterer is on the path of seeing an d will never again be reborn in the three lower 
realms; a once returner will return to the desire realm only one more time; a never returner will never again return to the desire realm. A hearer is a Hinayana disciple who strives for nirvana on 
the basis of listening to teachings from a teacher,  while a solitary realiser is a Hinayana disciple 
who strives for nirvana in solitude, without relying on a teacher.  We rejoice in the effects -  what they have attain ed - and we also rejoice in the virtues that led 
them to such a state.  Next is the rejoicing in the person of great ca pacity. There are two effe cts:temporal effect and 
ultimate effect (final effect). 
3B2B 2A2A 1A3 Rejoicing in the causes for and effect of unsurpassable enlightenment 
[3, 3 ] 
I rejoice in the Enlightenment of the Saviours ∗ 
And also in the spiritual levels  of the Conqueror’s Children 
 
[3, 4 ] 
And with gladness I rejoice 
In the ocean of virtue from developing an Awakening Mind 
That wishes all beings to be happy, 
As well as in the deeds th at bring them benefit. 
 
“Saviours” refers to the buddhas. “I rejoice in th e enlightenment of the buddhas”  is rejoicing in 
the final effect. “Conqueror’s children” refers to the bodhisattvas. That  is rejoicing in the 
temporal effect. We are specifically rejoicing here in what the superior bodhisattvas have 
attained. Superior bodhisattvas are those bodhisattv as abiding in the first ground up to the tenth 
ground. We are rejoicing in the states that they have attained.  “Rejoicing in the causes for unsurpassable enligh tenment” – What are we rejoicing in here? We 
rejoice in the fact that these bodhisattvas have generated bodhicitta. From the time of generating 
bodhicitta, they enter into the pr actices of the six perfections. They train in the six perfections 
and, along the way, they perform limitless works and activities for the benefit of sentient beings. 
We rejoice in all these activities.

F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
123 The generation of aspirational bodhicitta yields   immeasurable benefits and it goes without 
saying that there are many more benefits that come from generati ng engaged bodhicitta. This was 
already explained in Chapter 1 of this text. We should reflect on these benefits and rejoice in 
them.  The practice of rejoicing falls into the category  of practices for accumulating merit. We have the 
accumulation of merit and the purification of nega tivities. Of these two categories, the practice of 
rejoicing falls into the category of accumulating  merit. Various texts have explained the many 
benefits of  rejoicing. By  rejoicing, one subdue s pride. When we practise rejoicing, we will see 
there are many practical benefits. When we rejo ice in the virtues,  good things, possessions, 
qualities and the practices of others,  it serves to  weaken our negative emotions such as jealousy, 
pride and competitiveness.  Sometimes, when high lamas such as Kyabje Zopa Rinpoche visit us , everyone vies for Rinpoche’s 
time and attention. People become unhappy with and jealous of one anothe r, with thoughts such 
as, “You got the consultation with Rinpoche but I didn’t get the chance to see him.” It is a good 
time to practise rejoicing  in th e  virtue of others. When we do  this, we experience happiness for 
ourselves and other people are also happy with us. If everybody were to rejoice  in everybody else’s good fortunes, then there would not  be pr oblems like this and everyone would  feel happy. 
If not, jealousy and competitiveness would arise. Then it becomes messy!.   We should put this teaching about rejoicing into practical use. If we disc over that we fall into 
that group of people, we should try to reduce our negative emotions such as jealousy and competitiveness through rejoicing. If we were not acting in that way, we should rejoice that we 
have,  so far, not acted in those ways. 
3B2B 2A2A 1B Requesting [the teacher] to turn the wheel of dharma 
[3, 5 ] 
With folded hands I beseech 
The Buddhas of all directions 
To shine the lamp of Dharma 
For all bewildered in misery’s gloom 
 
There is a historical account of how Shakyamu ni Buddha was beseeched to teach the Dharma. 
Shakyamuni Buddha had sat unde r the bodhi tree and manifested the deed of achieving 
enlightenment. After he achieved enlightenment,  he did not teach and he did not say anything. 
He did not teach for 49 days. After the 49 d ays, the gods Indra and Brahma requested the 
Buddha to start teaching the Dharma. Indra o ffered a white conch shell to the Buddha and 
Brahma offered a thousand-spoked golden Dhar ma wheel to request the Buddha to teach the 
Dharma. In the same way, in vers e 5: “With folded hands I beseec h”, one requests the Buddha to 
teach the Dharma. With folded hands we beseech all the buddhas to shine the lamp of Dharma 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
124 for the benefit of sentient beings who are envelo ped in the darkness of ignorance, who do not 
know the essential points of what is to be practised, what is to be achieved and what is to be 
abandoned. 
 We can also practise requesting the Buddha to tu rn the wheel of Dharma by requesting the same 
of our spiritual masters, those with whom we have a Dharma connection.  We request them to 
turn the wheel of Dharma and, at the same time ,  we can also visualise holding in our hands a 
thousand-spoked golden Dharma wheel. When we make this requ est, we think that all the 
buddhas and gurus gladly accept our request.   Why is there a need to do this practice? Firstly, th is is to request the Buddha to turn the wheel of 
Dharma. If we were to do this, then in all our lives to come, we  would  not generate wrong views 
and we would  not come under the influence of  bad views. In addition, by requesting the 
buddhas to turn the wheel of Dharma, in all our lives to come, we would not be separated form 
the teachings of the Buddha.   
Lesson 16 – chapter 3, verse 5-12 
 I would like to offer another advice from Gung tang Rinpoche regarding how we should conduct 
our studies. Rinpoche said that, from the very onse t of our studies, if we aspire to be a scholarly 
expert on these topics immediately, that is errone ous and a mistake. It is also a mistake for us to 
forget whatever we have studied, i.e. we begin to forget everything we have studied after three 
days. If we conduct our studies wi th such an attitude, there is no  way we can succeed. He said we 
need to put in effort and our efforts should be constant like the flow of a river.   
Guntang Rinpoche gave this advice specifically for people who are engaged in the study of the 
vast and profound teachings of the Buddha, espe cially the great treatises of the philosophical 
teachings. When we study these gr eat treatises, we must put in continuous effort because these 
teachings are very deep and profound. If our effort s are erratic, there is no way for us to succeed 
in our studies.  Whatever we have learnt, we need to refresh our recall  of these teachings.  We should try to 
remember these teachings every now and then. If no t, we will learn something and then forget it 
three or four days later. This will not be helpful at all to our studies.  What we have studied so far is interrelated and there are connections in the subjects between  the earlier and later 
modules. We might be studying, but as soon as we receive new informatio n, we tend to forget 
what we have learnt earlier. Rinpoche said that  there is not much meaning and purpose in that, 
as one will not achieve anything. It is important to  keep reading,  thinking about and reflecting 
on the topics covered,  continuously. 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
125  
The best way to really learn is through discussion. If during the course of discussion, we are able 
to come to an established conclusion regarding a certain topic that comes from the use of logic 
and reasoning, it will remain in the mind for a very long time. I may give more time for discussion sessions in the future. In terms of th e information that we can get from our studies, 
25% comes from listening to the teacher, another 25% comes from self-study and the remaining 
50% comes from discussion. 
3B2B 2A2A 1B Requesting [the teacher] to turn the wheel  of Dharma 
[3, 5 ] 
With folded hands I beseech 
The Buddhas of all directions 
To shine the lamp of Dharma 
For all bewildered in misery’s gloom 
 
There is a historical account of how Shakyam uni Buddha, at the time when he achieved 
enlightenment, was requested to teach the Dh arma. The gods, Indra and Brahma both made 
offerings to request the Buddha to turn the wheel  of Dharma. Indra offered a white conch shell 
to the Buddha while Brahma offere d a thousand-spoked golden Dhar ma wheel. They said to the 
Buddha, ‘While you were training on the differe nt paths, you have endured many hardships for 
the sake of acquiring a single verse of the Dhar ma. For the sake of acquiring one verse of the 
Dharma, you have endured the hardship of having  1000 nails being pierced into your body and 
1000 candles  placed and lit on your body as   light offerings. Now you have achieved full 
enlightenment and you know everything. Why ar e you not teaching the Dharma?’  Indra and 
Brahma made repeated requests and beseeched th e Buddha to turn the wheel of Dharma for the 
benefit of his disciples.  In the same way, we should also put our hands to gether and make this request to all the buddhas 
and bodhisattvas and beseech them  to turn the wheel of Dharma. 
3B2B 2A2A 1C Requesting him not to pass into nirvana 
[3, 6 ] 
With folded hands I beseech 
The Conquerors who wish to pass away, 
To please remain for countless aeons 
And not to leave the world in darkness. 
 
When the Buddha was about to pass away into nirvana, a layperson named Chunda requested the Buddha not to pass away. As the result of his request, the Buddha extended his life for 
another three months. The Buddha extended his lifespan due to the request of this layperson. Similarly, we should put our hands together and beseech our gurus and buddhas of the ten 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

126 directions, who wish to show the aspect of passing  away into nirvana, to remain till the end of 
samsara for the benefit of their disciples, especi ally those who are blinde d by ignorance. At the 
end, we should think that they  have accepted our request. 
3B2B 2A2A 1D Dedicating virtue 
1 Dedicating in general 
2 Dedicating for th e sake of the ill 
3 Dedicating in order to alleviate hunger and thirst 
4 Dedicating [so that virtue acts] as the cause for all that one wishes 
5 Dedicating in general 
3B2B 2A2A 1D1 Dedicating in general 
[3,7] 
Thus by the virtue collected 
Through all that I have done, 
May the pain of every living creature 
Be completely cleared away. 
 
When we talk about dedication, there is a difference between dedicating and making aspirational prayers. There is a common locus between the two of them. All dedication are necessarily aspirational prayers, but this does not necessarily apply the other way round. Not all aspirational prayers are dedication. The difference between de dication and making aspirational prayers is 
posited on the basis of whether there is something to be dedicated or not. If there is something 
to be dedicated and if we dedicate it, then it beco mes the act of dedication and as well as  the act 
of making an aspirational prayer. But if there is nothing to be dedicated and we just generate the 
thought, ‘I will become a buddha  for the benefit of sentient be ings, ‘ then that  becomes an 
aspirational prayer and not a dedication.   In this context, we are dedicating the merit that we have accumulated through the earlier 
practices of making prostrations, making offering s, confessing negativities, rejoicing in virtue, 
beseeching the buddhas to turn the wheel of Dharma and requesti ng them not to pass away into 
nirvana. We have accumulated merit by doing th ese different practices . So there is something 
for us to dedicate.   We dedicate the virtues that we have accumulated from the earlier practices,  together with all the 
merit accumulated from the past, present and futu re. We dedicate them all, making the wish, 
‘May all the sufferings of the lower realms and the sufferings in cyclic existence of all sentient 
beings be completely cleared away.’ This is dedication in general - dedicating the merit for the purpose of eliminating the sufferings of all sentient beings.  
3B2B 2A2A 1D2 Dedicati ng for the sake of the ill 
[3, 8 ] 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

127 May I be the doctor and the medicine 
And may I be the nurse 
For all sick beings in the world 
Until everyone is healed. 
 
Here we are dedicating specifically for those wh o are sick.  In some other commentaries, it is 
mentioned that it is not enough to pray, ‘May I be the doctor.’ We should pray: ‘May I be a good doctor’ and also, not just an ordinary nurse, bu t one who is gentle and caring. For the medicine, 
we should pray, ‘May I be the powerful medicine  which will be effective for the patients.’  
 The practice here is just making a prayer, but, in  reality, when we see a sick person or someone 
who needs help, we should try to do something an d not  ignore that person. We should try to 
help as much we can when we encounter such people. In our Centre, we have a CARE group that takes care of those who are sick and a hospice group that take s care of those who are dying. 
As much as we are able, it is good to  volunteer to help in these activities.

 [3, 9] 
May a rain of food and drink descend 
To clear away the pain of thirst and hunger , 
And during the aeon of famine

W h e n  p e o p l e  a r e  d e p r i v e d  o f  f o o d  a n d  d r i n k ,  w e  c a n  d e d i c a t e  i n  t h i s  w a y :  ‘ M a y  t h i s  m e r i t

are also predicted to happen  in the future. The eon of famine will happen when the lifespan of 
human beings will be about thirty years only. Duri ng this time, famines will occur for seven years, 
seven months and seven days and all the beings of  that time will be on the verge of death.  
 During the eon of sickness , the lifespan of human beings will drop to twenty years. During this 
time, there will be epidemics and contagious diseases will spread everywhere for seven years, seven months and seven days and a lot of people will die.   The eon of  weapons will happen during the time when human beings will have a lifespan of ten 
years. For seven days during this period, people will kill one another whenever they meet. 
Whatever  they pick up - be it a stone, a twig or tree branch - will turn into a weapon and they 
will start killing one another.  This in formation can be found in the text, the Treatises on the 
Bhumis composed by Arya Asanga.

We should dedicate the merit now so that, in  the future, during the time of famine, as 
mentioned in the root text, one will become a ra in of food and drink to eliminate the suffering 
of those beings. We can become a doctor, nurse an d medicine for them when contagious diseases 
will affect human beings. We should dedicate our merit to be a cause for us to be medicine during the eon of sickness and to transform oursel ves into the entity of food and drink during 
the eon of famine. Due to the powe r of that merit that we have accumulated, during the eon of 
weapons, whatever is picked up, as a weapon, will be transformed into flowers.  The Buddha mentions  in one of the sutras that, in order not to be reborn during the eons of

• by offering food to the sangha, we will create  the cause not to  be reborn in the eon of

• and by meditating on love, we will create the cause not to be reborn in the eon of 
weapons.  
It is also mentioned in some other commentarie s that if we offer different weapons to the 
Buddha, then we will create a cause for no t being born in the eon of weapons.   
3B2B 2A2A 1D Dedicating [so that virtue acts] as the cause for all that one wishes 
[3,10 ] 
May I become an inexhaustible treasure 
For those who are poor and destitute; 
May I turn into all things they could need 
And may these be placed close beside them. 
 
The main point here is the practice of bodhicitta. What we are trying to do here is to generate 
bodhicitta. In order to do that, we first have to  accumulate merit and then dedicate the merit for 
the purpose of others. This merit will act as a cond ition for us to generate bodhicitta. In order to 
generate bodhicitta, we have to train our minds in  equanimity and in the practice of compassion 
and so forth.   Before we can proceed to train our minds in th e different practices, we need to generate 
bodhicitta. The training in  the dedication of merit precedes this. The practice of dedication 
becomes a  branch of mind training and also be comes the cause of success in such mind training 
practice. Due to these merits, we will be able  to generate equanimity, compassion, loving 
kindness and so forth. Once we generate these qualit ies, all these different realisations will act as 
a cause for the development of bodh icitta. For example, in verse 8 of  this chapter, “May I be the 
doctor, the medicine...”, we pray and dedicate that  we may also become natural medicine and so

3B2B 2A2A 1E Training in the mind that gives away body, material wealth and roots of virtue 
before practicing the branch of the perfection of giving  
1 Training in the thought to give away body, material wealth and roots of virtue 
2 Aspiring for virtue to become a cause that does not go to waste 
3 Aspiring for virtue to become a cause for material resources, body, etc. 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1 Training in the thought to give away body, material wealth and roots of virtue 
A The way to give them away 
B The reason it is reasonable to give away in a definite manner C How to practice once one has given them away 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1A The way to give them away 
[3,11 ] 
Without any sense of loss 
I shall give up my body and enjoyments 
As well as all my virtues of the three times 
For the sake of benefiting all. 
 
We are giving away our bodies and enjoyments as well as our roots of virtue of the past, present 
and the future. We are giving away all these to fulf il  the temporal and ultimate goals of sentient 
beings. When we give them away, we do so with out any miserliness, expectation of reward and 
sense of loss. This practice of giving away our bo dies, our possessions and our roots of virtue is a 
method to train our minds in bodhicitta. In order to generate bodhicitta, we need to remove our 
self-cherishing attitude and we need to in crease the mind that cherishes others.  
 
Of all the different things that we own, we cherish our bodies the most followed by our 
possessions and our roots of virtue. So, first, we need to give up our bodies. Because of 
cherishing our bodies, we need to take care of them. Then we go about accumulating wealth and 
merit. In order to develop bodhicitta, we need to remove our self-grasping and self-cherishing 
attitude. When we are able to give away our bodies , wealth and virtue completely from our hearts 
without any sense of loss, then there will be no pr oblems in training in the perfection of giving. 
 
We have studied this topic in the la m rim module. It is mentioned in the Lam Rim Chen Mo that 
the absence or the abandonment of miserliness does  not constitute having achieved or perfected 
the generosity of giving. For example, the so litary realiser and the arhat have abandoned 
miserliness but they have not achi eved the  perfection of giving . The perfection of giving or 
generosity comes about only when there is a comp lete whole-hearted wish to give and dedicate 
everything.  One is verbalising here one’s intention to give up  one’s body, wealth and the roots of virtue of 
the past, present and  future fo r the sake of accomplishing th e purposes of others. We are 
verbalising this intention but it should not be  done just at the level of speech - we should also 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
130 reflect on this over and over again. If we are able to reflect on this repeatedly, from the depths of 
our hearts,  it will serve to weaken our self-grasping attitude. 
 
The text mentions that sometimes we may experience  different kinds of fear - the fear of losing 
our bodies, our wealth and possessions or our me rit. When this happens, we should remember: 
“I have already offered these (my body, wealth  and possessions and merit) to other sentient 
beings. There is actually nothing to hold on to and  there is nothing to cherish.” When we are 
able to reflect well in this way,  then it will help  to reduce anxieties and stress when meeting with 
such problems and fears.   We experience the fear of losing our bodies  be cause we cherish our bodies and have very strong 
attachment and  self-grasping. It is the same with our wealth, possessions, our friends and close 
ones.   We fear losing them because of our attachment an d strong self-grasping. The main point is that 
when we are able to give up all these - our bodies , wealth and roots of virtues - from our hearts,  
there is no longer any basis for the arising of su ch fears. In fact, our minds would be at peace 
when we had given all these things away. Should se lf-grasping arise again, we should meditate on 
this,  giving everything away from the depths of our hearts.   
This is basically the practi ce of those who engage in chod – literally the practice of ‘cutting,’ where 
the practitioners  visualise giving away their bodi es, flesh and blood  and possessions to all the  
flesh-eaters and the different classes of spirits,  as sources of sustenance. The main purpose of 
practicing ‘chod’ is to cut  self-grasping and self-cherishing.  It is said here that this practice  of giving up our bodies, wealth, enjoyments and roots of virtue is 
a method for training the mind and is the pr eliminary to developing bodhicitta. Without 
training the mind, it is impossible to develop bodhicitta. Without reducing or weakening self-
cherishing, there is no way to generate the thou ght of benefiting others, in other words, to 
generate bodhicitta.  When we say we have given up all these things, si ncerely from our hearts, for others, the question 
may arise: How are we going to use these things th at we still have? The answer is that we should 
utilise our bodies, wealth and possessions for the be nefit of others. We dedicate them to others 
and use them for their benefit. On ce we have dedicated them to ot hers, then we should use them 
sincerely and purely for others.   
A qualm related to this is mentioned in the Lam Rim Chen Mo,  in the section on the perfection 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
131 of generosity4: when you use something that you have given to and completely dedicated to 
others, wouldn’t that be an act of stealing? Wo uldn’t that person commit the act of deceit or 
stealing? 
  
                                              
4 Lam Rim Chen Mo   Vol 2,  page 119-20: 
If you use food, clothes, shelter and so on that you ha ve sincerely given away to all beings, and you do so 
with craving for your personal welfar e, forgetting the thought, “I will use them for others’ welfare,” then 
you commit a major infraction. When you have no craving but forget to apply the idea of focusing on all 
living beings, or if you use those resources for a pa rticular living being out of attachment, you commit a 
minor infraction.  
 
With regard to the material goods that  you have turned over to others, the Compendium of Trainings states 
that when you use them for your own welfare fully  cognizant of their being the property of others, you 
are stealing, and if the total value is enough, yo u commit a cardinal transgression of the vows of 
individual liberation. In response to  this, some say that since you have turned over your food, etc., to all 
living beings, it is impossible for the total value of  any one being's portion to be enough, so you cannot 
commit a cardinal transgression. Others say this is  not correct because you have turned over your 
belongings as a whole to each being individually. Others argue that even though you have mentally 
surrendered them to others, they do not take pers onal possession of them, so there is no cardinal 
transgression. 
 
The intended meaning behind the Compendium of Trainings  statement is that you incur a cardinal 
transgression (given that the total value requiremen t has been met) when you sincerely turn your food, 
etc., over to a human being, and this person knows it and takes possession, whereupon you, fully 
cognizant of their being another's pro perty, appropriate them for your own use. Therefore, the positions 
stated by the others are wrong. 
 
There is no fault in using some living beings’ resource s if you think, while using them, “I do this for their 
welfare.”  The Compendium of Trainings  states: 
There is no fault in using things if you think,  “I am taking care of my body which is owned 
by others with these resources that are owned by others.” Slaves have no material goods of 
their own with which to survive. 
 
You may think, “I incur a fault because, after I have turned over these belongings to living beings, I use 
them without their permission”, but there is no fault. The Compendium of Trainings  says: 
A servant who labors hard on a master’s beha lf might use the master ’s belongings without 
permission when the master’s mind is unclear  due to illness and so forth, but incurs no 
fault. 
 
Do not lack faith and think, “Mentally giving everything  away to living beings while not actually giving it 
is tantamount to a lie, therefor e, is without real substance.” 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
132 There are two other reasons mentioned here as to why it is appropriate to give away body, wealth, 
possessions and roots of virtue:  
3B2B 2A2A 1E1B The reason it is reasonable to give away in a definite manner 
1 Due to abandoning attachment to all and giving away [such things], one will attain nirvana 
2 As sentient beings are the supreme field, it is reasonable to give [such] away to them 
 
[3,12 ] 
By giving up all, sorrow is transcended 
And my mind will realize the sorrowless state. 
It is best that I (now) give everything to all beings 
In the same way as I shall (at death). 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1B 1 Due to abandoning attachment to all and giving away [such things], one 
will attain nirvana 
This is shown in the first and second line of verse 12: “By giving up all, sorrow is transcended 
and my mind will realize the sorrowless state.”   Once we have given away and dedicated everything to  others, we need to use  these things for the 
benefit of others. Whenever we enjoy food, clothes or our other possessions, we should conjoin our enjoyment with the attitude of benefiting others. In order to  sustain our bodies for the 
benefit of others,  we should think: ’I am going to  eat this or use this for the benefit of others.’ 
We reduce our attachment to the things mentione d here by mentally giving them away . We will 
attain nirvana once we reduce our attachment to them. By giving up attachment, we will achieve 
liberation.   Once we have this  mind that wishes to achiev e the sorrowless state of liberation,  we become  
Buddhists and take refuge with the purpose of ac hieving such liberation. The state of liberation 
that we want to achieve comes from giving up all the things that were mentioned earlier.  
3B2B 2A2A 1E1B 2 As sentient beings are the supreme field, it is reasonable to give [such] 
away to them 
The third and fourth lines of verse 12 show the se cond reason why it is appropriate to give away 
these things: “It is best that I (now) give everythi ng to all beings in the same way as I shall (at 
death).”  Giving away everything now to all sentient beings is  like leaving everything  behind at the time of 
death. Since this is the case, it is best to give them away before that happens. The same applies to our merits and roots of virtue. If we do not  dedi cate our roots of virtue, they might be destroyed 
by hatred.  If we do not dedicate our roots of virtue for the benefit of others, they  will be 
exhausted as soon as they ripen. Since giving away all things now is the same as leaving them 
behind at the time of death, we should dedicate all these merits for the benefit of sentient beings 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
133 now as they are the best and supreme field.  
 
The above are the two reasons why it is appropri ate to give away our bodies, our possessions and 
our roots of virtue to others.   
Lesson 17 – chapter 3, verse 13-32 
 Before we start this session, it is very important to generate the correct motivation for listening to 
the teachings. We should remember the reason that we are gathered here together is to engage in 
the activities of listening to, reflecting and medita ting on the teachings of the Buddha. It is very 
important to remind ourselves of these practices.  Sometimes, it is possible that our minds are not here, although we are physically present. When 
that is the case, then it is probably not going to  be of any benefit  attending this session.  We 
come to class with the intention of listening to the teachings. We should have this intention: 
while we are here, we should try our best to list e n  t o  wh a t  i s  b e i n g   s a i d .  W e  s h o u l d  r e m i n d 
ourselves, on the way to the class, of the reason why we are attending class; we should think, ‘I 
am going to go to class to listen to the teac hings because I want the teachings of the Buddha.’ 
This is the intention and is the reason why we come to class.  
 When we are in class, we should try to  transf orm our goal into reality. When we are here, we 
should try to listen, to the best of our abilities, and not let our minds get distracted or  fall asleep. 
It is possible that sometimes these things happen; we get distracted or feel sleepy because we are 
tired, and so forth. But it is important not to le t this happen all the time. If this happens all the 
time, then at the end of the five years, we will be sleeping for the five years.  
 
At the end of the class, if you were to meet  someone who asks you, “What have you learnt 
today?” Your answer should not be, “I don’t know , I can’t remember.” It is possible again that 
sometimes this happens because the body and mind  are tired. But it should not happen all the 
time. At least,  you should be able to say that  you have learnt something at the end of each 
session.   At this time, we can say that the conditions are ideal for study; eg. when you come to class, you 
have classmates who attend the class with you. While the conditions are here, you should try to 
make use of this opportunity and listen without being distracted because, in the future, it is 
possible that there will be no teacher, translator or  classmates. Then, even if you have the interest 
to study and  learn, because the conditions are not there, you will not be ab le to do so. Now,  you 
should make use of this opportunity and try not to be distracted.

134 In the last session, we completed  verse 12 of  ch apter three and looked at the reasons why it is 
appropriate to give away our bodies, wealth and our roots of virtue to sentient beings. As 
mentioned in the text, it is appr opriate for us to give away our bo dies, wealth and roots of virtue 
to sentient beings as,  by doing so, we will achieve nirvana. What  i s  t h i s   g o a l  o f   a c h i e v i n g  
nirvana? Nirvana is achieved by abandoning atta chment to these things (our bodies, possessions 
and roots of virtue). It is appropriate for us to give away our bodies, wealth and roots of virtue to 
sentient beings because sentient beings are the supr eme field. By giving these things  to sentient 
beings, we will achieve enlightenment. It is appr opriate,  because whether we give these things 
away or not, at the time of death,  we will have to leave all these things be hind.  Since that is the 
case, would it not  be better to give all these things away before that happened?   
 Here it is said that by giving away our bodies, we alth and roots of virtue to others, we will achieve 
nirvana and the state of full enlightenment. We must ask  how this happens; how does nirvana 
and enlightenment come about as the re sult of giving away these things?  
 Once we have given away our bodies, wealth and ro ots of virtue to sentient beings, what are we 
going to do? The text talks about  how to practi se once one has given away these things.  Three 
points are  covered in the following outline: 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1C  How to practice once one has given them away 
1 Discarding, from this day forward, the mind that [thinks] “My body is independent” 
2 Explaining the meaning of that extensively 
3 Applying that to appropriate activity ∗ 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1C 1 Discarding, from this day forward, the mind that [thinks] “My body is 
independent” 
Once we have given our bodies away to others, they  no longer  belong to us. So we should not 
think, “This is mine”, as we have already given them away.   This particular outline is about what we should do after we have given away our bodies, wealth and roots of virtue.  In the case of our bodies, on ce we have dedicated it to other sentient beings, 
what are we going to do and how should we think?  In essence, once we have given our bodies  
away, we should  also discard the thought that this body is mine, which is shown in verse 13: 
 
[3,13] 
Having given this body up 
For the pleasure of all living beings, 
By killing, abusing and beating it 
May they always do as they please. 
 
Having given up this body to sentient beings for the purpose of achieving the state of full 
enlightenment for all sentient beings, even if some one were to kill us, put us down,  abuse, scold 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
135 and beat us, we should think: “They can do anything  they want with my body, it is fine.” After 
we have dedicated our bodies to others, we give up thinking, “This body belongs to me,” or “This 
body is mine.” Once we have given up the thought, “This body is mine,” even if someone were to 
kill us, abuse or do something nasty to us, because we have given it to others, we do not have a  
sense of attachment to it. We will not generate anger when such things ha ppen. This is the main 
advice here. The next verse, verse 14  expa nds on the meaning of the previous verse. 
3B2B 2A2A 1E1C 2  Explaining the meaning of that extensively 
 [3,14] 
Although they may play with my body 
And make it a source of jest and blame, 
Because I have given it up to them 
What is the use of holding it dear? 
 
When the text says, “They may play with my body”:  this is not in the sense of fun or enjoyment . 
It means “play” in a very bad sense of the word - others can do anything they want to us in the physical sense or they may make our bodies  an  object  of jest and reproach. They may make 
nasty jokes about us. But regardless of what they do, we should never have the thought to oppose 
them and we should stop the idea  that “this bo dy is mine.” The commentaries that we are using 
say, “People can do anything to us.” They can do anything to our bodies, joking about or  
criticising them. As we have dedicated our bodies  to others, we are not go ing to oppose or stop 
them. In the root text, it says, “What is the use of  holding it dear?”  but,  in the commentary, the 
idea is that we do not stop or oppose these peop le from doing these things. Literally, it means:  
“Because I have given it up to them, what is  the point of stopping or opposing them?”  
3B2B 2A2A 1E1C 3 Applying that to appropriate activity 
[3,15] 
Therefore I shall let them do anything to it 
That does not cause them any harm, 
And when anyone encounters me 
May it never be meaningless for him 
 
This is shown in the first two li nes of verse 15: “Therefore I shall let them do anything to it 
that does not cause them any harm”.  “Therefore I shall let them do anything to it” – In  the short as well as in the long term, they can 
do anything they please to our bodies, good or  bad. Once we have de dicated our bodies  to 
sentient beings, if we are able to abandon the thought  that “This body is mine and it belongs to me,”  then,  we will be  able to do all the th ings that are mentioned here. Even if someone were 
to kill us, beat us up, abuse or doing anything to  our bodies, we would  no t get angry. We will no 
longer have  attachment to our bodies. 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
136   
All the faults, such as anger and attachment, come from the self-gra sping conception that  grasps 
at an “I” and grasps at things  as being “mine”. It is advisable to subdue our anger and 
attachment. This is something th at we can understand from ou r own experiences in life. The 
more we cherish our bodies or  the greater th e attachment we have for our bodies, the more 
suffering we will experience. Even if our bodies are harmed  only to a very small extent, because of our great attachment, grasping and cherishing  our bodies, we experience great suffering. Our 
suffering corresponds to the strength of the attachment for or grasping at the body.  
 It is the same  with our friends, loved ones an d relatives. When  we ha ve strong attachment  
towards them, even when a  small harm happens to  them, we suffer a lot and feel we are unable 
to bear it. This boils down to holding on to th ese two kinds of self-grasping attitudes – grasping 
at the  “I” and grasping at “mine,” such as “m y friend, my entourage.”  Because we have great 
attachment to our bodies, when we put on a littl e weight, we get very worried. If we have no 
attachment to our bodies, it does not matter how fat we may  become. It will  not  bother us. It is 
the same with pimples. Because we have great atta chment to our faces, one pimple causes us so 
much suffering. We may be  reluctant to go out ju st because of one big pimple. If we think that 
everyone will stare at our face because of the pimple, we will worry about this and  will not be able to go out. It all  depends on how we see things.  Here we are talking about the preliminary practices for training the mind and preparing the mind to develop bodhicitta. From our side, we ha ve to train our minds. We cannot stop people 
from doing whatever they want as it is beyond ou r control. Later, we will cover points concerning 
the practices of giving away the bo dy and so forth; as to when we can give our bodies away and 
when it is inappropriate to give our bodies.  
3B2B 2A2A 1E2 Aspiring for virtue to become a cause that does not go to waste 
A Aspiring for [virtue] to act solely as a cause for the benefit of others 
B Aspiring for [virtue] to act as a cause preventing such thoughts from going to waste 
C Aspiring for virtue to act as a cause preventing the action from going to waste 
3B2B 2A2A 1E2A  Aspiring for [virtue] to act solely as a cause for the benefit of others 
This is shown in the last two lines of verse 15: “And when anyone encounters me may it never be 
meaningless for them.” 
3B2B 2A2A 1E2B  Aspiring for [virtue] to ac t as a cause preventing such thoughts from going 
to waste 
[3,16] 
If in those who encounter me 
A faithful or an angry thought arises, 
May that eternally become the source 
For fulfilling all their wishes. 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
137 3B2B 2A2A 1E2C  Aspiring for virtue to act as a cause preventing the action from going to 
waste 
[3,17] 
May all who say bad things to me 
Or cause me any other harm 
And those who mock and insult me 
Have the fortune to fully awake. 
 
In essence, we are dedicating our virtues to beco me the causes for such things to happen to us 
and for them to never become meaningless. What ever connection others make with us, be it 
positive or negative, in the form of hearing about us, talking to us or  touching us, may all these 
become the causes for the accomplishment of their own temporal and ultimate goals. When 
people say nasty things in front of us or behind our backs, we pray for all these actions to become 
causes for them to achieve enlightenment.  It may sound very nice but that is  not the point. The point is for us  to be able to contemplate in 
that way. If we are able to think in this way,  when  so met hin g like t hat  really happen s - when  
someone says something nasty directly to us or be hind our backs - because of having such sincere 
and virtuous thoughts, we will not be affected or suffer and others will also not suffer. If we have 
this virtuous thought, sincerely from our hearts,  then,  even if we hear from someone else that 
someone is criticising us behind our backs, th ere will not be any suffering or problems.  
 
Otherwise, when someone criticises us or we he ar that somebody is criticising us behind our 
backs, we will feel very angry and agitated. We will keep reminding ourselves of those things that 
people have said. Doing this  over and over again will only serve  to perpetuate our anger. Then, 
we will start to think about how we can  respond effectively in the most negative way possible and 
what are some of the nastiest words that we can use to counter that pers on’s accusations. When 
this happens, there will be no peace fo r ourselves and no happiness for others. 
 The point is that when such  things happen, we should try, as much as possible, to forget that they have happened and not remind ourselves of th e unpleasant words that we have heard. Then 
we dedicate sincerely from our hearts,  ‘Whate ver connections others may  make with us, may 
those connections only become the causes for them  to achieve enlightenment.’ If we are able to 
reflect well on this point, it will bring great benefit.

May I be a protector for those without one,

 Q: Where does the mind (which is consciousness) go when we aspire to become an object (which is physical) like a bridge?  A: First, we have a bridge that is emanated by Buddha. In this case, the bridge is not Buddha. Then we have  a  bridge that is an emanation of Buddha. In this case, the bridge is Buddha. We have to differentiate between the two - the brid ge that is emanated by Buddha and the bridge 
that is an emanation of Buddha. In  the latter instance, if  we main tain that the bridge is Buddha, 
then where the consciousness goes is not an issue.  
 We are aspiring for our virtues to become the caus es for whatever things sentient beings need. If 
they need wealth, may our virtues  become caus es for wealth. If they need a drink, may our 
virtues become causes for that. In short, whatever  t h e y  n e e d ,  w e  a s p i r e  t h a t   o u r  v i r t u e s  w i l l  
become the causes for all of th ose things to come about.

[3,21] 
Just like space 
And the great elements such as earth, 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds

139 May I always support the life 
Of all the boundless creatures. 
3B2B 2A2A 1E3C  Aspiring for no discontinuation in object and times 
[3,22] 
And until they pass away from pain 
May I also be the source of life 
For all the realms of varied beings 
That reach unto the ends of space. 
 
We are not only dedicating in a very extensive way   but we are dedicating our virtues so that they 
will continue to bear fruit for a very long time . It shows the importance of making prayers. We 
s h o u l d  m a k e  v e r y  e x t e n s i v e  p r a y e r s  s o  t h a t ,  in the future, when we become a buddha or 
bodhisattva, we will be able to offer extensive bene fits to all sentient beings in accordance with 
our extensive prayers. It is said that there is a connection between the prayers that we make now 
and the benefit we will be able to offer to sent ient beings when we become a bodhisattva and a 
buddha. Due to the strength of the prayers that the buddhas made  while they were on the paths 
of training, after they  achieved enlightenmen t, due to the difference in the power of their 
prayers, there is also a difference in their functi ons and their work for sentient beings. When we 
say this,  a qualm may arise : are we saying that   the buddhas do not have the same qualities, i.e. 
some buddhas are more powerful or faster than others?  It does seem to suggest  that  different buddhas have different qualities.   We have completed  the preliminary section. Now, we go on to  the actual or main body of the 
chapter. This is the taking of the bodhisattva vows. 
3B2B 2A2A 2 The actual 
[3,23] 
Just as the previous Sugatas 
Gave birth to an Awakening Mind, 
And just as they successively dwelt 
In the Bodhisattva practices; 
 
[3,24] 
Likewise for the sake of all that lives 
Do I give birth to an Awakening Mind, 
And likewise shall I too 
Successively follow the practices. 
 
We are saying here, “Just as the buddhas of the pa st, such as  Shakyamuni  Buddha and so forth,  
generated bodhicitta and  trained in the practices of the bodhisattvas, likewise for the sake of all 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
140 sentient beings, may I also generate bodhicitta and train in the practices of the bodhisattvas (ie.  
the  practices of the  six perfections) as well as I am able.” This is the ritual for taking both the 
aspirational and engaged bodhis attva vows at the same time.  
 There is also the tradition of taking these two vows in a gradual way, in stages. First, we take the 
aspirational bodhisattva vows with its commitments . Then, we investigate and check to see if we 
have the ability to train in the six perfections. If  w e  f i n d  t h a t  w e  a r e  a b l e  t o  d o  s o ,  t h e n  w e  
proceed to take the engaged vows. Th is is taking the vows in stages. 
 Here, the two vows are taken simultaneously. In th is context, we are talking about someone who 
has the ability to practise the six pe rfections as mentioned in the text, Engaging in the Bodhisattva 
Deeds . But there is also a tradition of taking the vows in stages.  
 
The way of taking the bodhisattva vows has been already explained in the Lam Rim Chenmo . 
When it comes to taking the bo dhisattva vows, we can take it from or in the absence of a 
spiritual master. If a spiritual master is not pres ent, we can take the vows in the presence of the 
merit field. The taking of the bodh isattva vows has to be preceded by   going for refuge. If there is 
a master, in the presence of the master, with folded  hands, we first take refuge. Then we take the 
bodhisattva vows. We normally recite the words of the text three times  after the spiritual master. 
At the end of the third repetition, we have to think, “Now I have received the vows.” 
 If there is no one from whom one can take the vows, then we can take the vows in the presence 
of the merit field by visualising all the buddhas and bodhisattvas in front of us.  First, we go for 
refuge and then we recite the words for taking the bodhisattva vows. While we are reciting the 
words, we should think that we are actually ta king the vows from the buddhas and bodhisattvas 
and repeating the words after them. At the end of the third repetition, we also have to think, 
“Now I have received the bodhisattva vows.”   
As a beginner, it is best to take the vows from a  spiritual master. Sometimes people might 
wonder, “Since we can take the vows without a sp iritual master,  why do we need a spiritual 
master at all?” There is a difference in taking the vows from a spiritual master as it helps with  the 
generation of shame and embarrassment.   We go for refuge and then we take the bodhisattv a vows. It says in the words for taking the vows, 
“I will generate bodhicitta and successively follow  the practices of the six perfections in stages.” 
This implies doing things in stages  in a gradual way. So it is not as if we have to give away our 
bodies immediately once we receive the vows. Ther e are certain criteria for determining when it 
will be appropriate to give away our bodies, how to  check that we have the ability to give away 
our bodies and so forth.

[3,25] 
In order to further increase it from now on,

We have gone through the preliminary practices for developing bodhicitta and have taken the 
bodhisattva vows. After taking the bodhisattva vows, in order not to let the bodhicitta  
degenerate and in order to increase it, we cultiv ate joy by uplifting the mind. We cultivate joy by 
reflecting on how fortunate we are that we have  received the bodhisattva vows, we have been 
born into the family of the buddhas and we ha ve now become Buddha’s child. We generate joy 
in the fact that we are fortunate enough to be  able to generate bodhicitta and to take the 
bodhisattva vows.   When do we become a bodhisattva? Different texts say different things at different times. Here, it 
mentions we become a Buddha’s child or bodhisattv a after we take the bodhisattva vows. In this 
text, at the very beginning of this chapter, in verse 3, it says: “I rejoice in the Awakening of the 
buddhas and also in the spiritua l levels of the Conqueror’s chil dren.” This indicates that we 
become a bodhisattva after entering the first grou nd, while in other contexts it says that we 
become a bodhisattva once we have achiev ed the Mahayana path of accumulation. 
 Let us look at the very first verse of  this text - chapter one, verse 1:

 [1,1] 
Respectfully I prostrate myself to the Sugatas 
Who are endowed with the Dharmakaya,

“Noble Sons” here refers to the superior bodhisa ttvas who are on the first ground and above. I 
think that the difference in positing bodhisattvas is made on the basis of  whether we are looking 
at them from the perspective of conventional or  ultimate bodhicitta. In  the context of taking 
bodhisattva vows for ordinary bein gs, I think it is talking about one becoming a bodhisattva after 
one has generated conventional bodhicitta. In th e context of the above verse, “noble children” 
refers to superior bodhisattvas, who are  posited as bodhisattvas from the perspective of having 
generated ultimate bodhicitta. We will see diffe rent things in different texts. Sometimes, 
bodhisattvas refers to ordinary bodhisattvas and sometimes, bodhisattvas refers specifically to 
superior bodhisattvas.  Please read the remaining verses of  this chapter, paying special a ttention to second subdivision of

3B2B 2A2A 3A2  The accomplishment of others’ welfare

Then please check: how does bodhicitta enable on e to remove the obstructions to omniscience as 
indicated in the first two lines of verse 32? 
[3,32ab] 
It is the great sun that finally removes 
The misty ignorance of the world, 
 
While checking, consider the following: in the li ne “It is the great sun that finally removes”,  
“finally” indicates the removal of the obstructions  to omniscience and becoming a buddha. If we 
say that bodhicitta alone can remove the obstruct ions to omniscience, then in order to achieve 
enlightenment, the factor of method alone s eems to be enough. Why would we still need 
wisdom? But if we say that bodhicitta does no t help us to eliminate the obstructions to 
omniscience, we are going against what is said here in the commentary and the root text; it is 
stated very clearly in the commentary that bodhic itta has the power to dispel and eliminate both 
the obstructions to liberation and the obstructions to omniscience.

143 Lesson 18 – chapter 3, verse 33-34 (end) 
 
Gungtang Rinpoche advised that when we stud y the teachings of the Buddha, we must study 
texts that are unmistaken and free of error. With  such texts as the bases, we investigate and 
analyse the teachings. Whatever teachings or texts we may read, they must be valid, error-free and 
unmistaken. We should check whether what is mentioned in the texts accords with the great 
philosophical treatises of Buddhism.  When we simply rely indiscriminately on any text s that we come across, taking them to be true 
and if those texts do not accord with the intent  or meaning found in the valid texts and great 
treatises, then relying on such texts will only cause our wisdom to decline. When we study the 
teachings of the Buddha, we must base our studie s and rely on the error-free, completely valid 
and reliable great philosophical treatises and the valid commentaries. We are not saying here that 
we cannot read commentaries or texts that offer a more accessible explanation. But whatever we 
read, what is taught by those texts must accord with what is found in th e great treatises and the 
great texts.   Sometimes, we may find that certain texts are easier for us to understand without checking 
beforehand whether they are valid or not. What ever we study, texts we read, information we 
receive, we should be able to trace them all th e way back to the teaching s of the Buddha himself. 
Whatever we read should accord with the grea t commentaries composed by the great Indian and 
Tibetan masters of the past, the valid ones. We sh ould not rely on anything that comes along. 
This is advice on studying given by Guntang Rinpoche. In essence, when we need to study, we should rely on the valid texts.  We are studying in this particular module th e text composed by the great bodhisattva,  
Shantideva,  called Engaging in the Bodhisattva D eeds. Shantideva  is obviously a great master; a 
completely valid Indian master who composed th is text based on the teachings of the Buddha. 
He gathered all the important points from the various sutras regarding the practices of the 
bodhisattvas. He put all these main points in the r oot text itself. We are now  studying this text 
and using the commentary composed by Gyaltsab Je, who was one of the foremost disciples of 
Lama Tsong Khapa. In this case, both the author and commentator are valid.  
 As usual, we need to adjust our  motivation to  have the correct motivation for listening to the 
teachings. At the very beginning of  this text, in verse 2 of chapter one, Shantideva says: “There is 
nothing here that has not been explained before”:  
[1,2] 
There is nothing here that ha s not been explained before 
And I have no skill in  the art of rhetoric;

144 Therefore, lacking any intention to benefit others, 
I write this in order to acquaint it to my mind. 
 
He is not composing something of his own; his te xt is based on the teachings of the Buddha, on 
whatever teaching that Buddha has taught. He  is using that as the basis for his text. 
 In the last lesson, we looked at the main body  of chapter three,  which is taking both the 
aspirational and the engaging bodhisattva  vows. In  this particular text, these two vows are taken 
together at the same time. We started at the ou tline “concluding actions”, or the conclusion of 
this particular chapter. We are now looking at “c ultivating joy oneself.”  We are talking about  
the things that have to be done after taking the bodhisattva vows. 
3B2B 2A2A 3A Cultivating joy oneself 
1 The accomplishment of one’s own welfare 
2 The accomplishment of others’ welfare 
3B2B 2A2A 3A1 The accomplishment of one’s own welfare 
A Cultivating joy by uplifting the mind 
B Having found that mind, cultivating conscientiousness C Cultivating joy by gaining the mind that is difficult to find 
 
Last week we stopped at “cultivating joy by upliftin g the mind”. It is not sufficient just to take the 
bodhisattva vows. We must ensure that they do not degenerate. We should try to increase our 
practice of bodhicitta. For that reason, we cultivat e joy. We do that by ge nerating enthusiasm and 
uplifting the mind. We uplift the mind by contemplating verse 26:  
[3, 26 ] 
Today my life has (borne) fruit; 
(Having) well obtained this human existence, 
I’ve been born in the family of Buddha 
And now am one of the Buddha’s Children. 
 
If we are able to rejoice and to generate enthusia sm, then we are able to uplift the mind and this 
will make a difference to our prac tice of bodhicitta,  of keepin g those vows and strengthening our 
practice. 
3B2B 2A2A 3A1B  Having found that mind, cultivating conscientiousness 
[3, 27 ] 
Thus whatever actions I do from now on 
Must be in accord with the family. 
Never shall I disg race or pollute 
This noble and unsullied race.

After taking the vows, we must cultivate cons cientiousness by contemplating “Thus whatever 
actions I do from now on, must be in accord with  the family.” The “family” here refers to the 
Mahayana lineage. We are saying to ourselves, “We must follow the example of the buddhas and 
bodhisattvas by  not disgracing or polluting th is Mahayana lineage. We will not disgrace or 
pollute this very noble race.” How do we do that? By ensuring that we do not come under the control of the self-cherishing thought,  of doing things for the benefit of oneself alone. We must 
ensure that  we are not stained by committing any of the 18 downfalls and the 46 faults of the 
bodhisattva vows. In essence, whatever action s we do should accord with this noble and 
unsullied race by not disgracing or polluting it. 
3B2B 2A2A 3A1C  Cultivating joy by ga ining the mind that is difficult to find 
[3, 28 ] 
Just like a blind man 
Discovering a jewel in a heap of rubbish, 
Likewise by some coincidence 
An Awakening Mind has been born within me. 
 
If a blind man were to find a jewel in a heap of  rubbish, that would be considered very amazing 
and unbelievable. In the very same way, we are under the control of the three mental poisons all 
the time. We are under the control of karma and our negative emotions.  Suddenly,  one day,  
we are able to generate this bodhicitta in our minds. We somehow managed to obtain the 
bodhisattva vows. This will also  be considered  amazing and marvellous.   If we managed to generate bodhicitta, we should rejo ice. This is because bodh icitta will enable us  
to eliminate all our sufferings and eliminate cycl ic existence and samsara.  Once we generate 
bodhicitta, we are able to eliminate  the suffer ing of samsara and nirvana and attain individual 
liberation. The generation of such a precious mind in the continuum of someone who was 
always under the influence of negative karma an d  emotions would be considered amazing and 
marvellous. We are looking here at the practice of  rejoicing at the generation of bodhicitta,  the 
altruistic intention to become enlightened  for the benefit of all sentient beings.   Even if we have not yet generate d bodhicitta in our own minds bu t we have already received the 
bodhisattva vows (either the aspirational or engage d vow), we can rejoice in the same way.  Even 
if we have not yet  received the bodhisattva vows but hold only a refuge or precept vow, this can 
still be the cause for rejoicing. This is due to  the power of the Buddha combined with our own 
virtue and merit. We have managed to take re fuge with some sort of faith in the Buddha, 
Dharma and Sangha and interest in wanting to le arn the teachings of the Buddha. That is also 
something very amazing and marvellous.   There are some people who have met the teachings of the Buddha for a relatively long time and 
Amitabha Buddhist Centre       Engaging in the Bodhisattva Deeds 
F P M T  B a s i c  P r o g r a m              C h a p t e r  T h r e e  
 
 
146 been practising but they  get discouraged along th e way.  People may get so  discouraged  that 
they want to give up. If this happens to us, we really need to use what we have learnt. At those 
times when we get discouraged or depressed, we n eed to uplift our minds by rejoicing. We need 
to praise ourselves.  Here,  we are learning how to cultivate joy af ter taking the bodhisattva vows and generating 
bodhicitta. But this can be applie d elsewhere as well. This is  no t confined to the teachings of 
bodhicitta. It can be applied to other aspects of the teachings. The main point is to learn how to 
cultivate joy.

3B2B 2A2A 3A2A  The potential [power] to alleviate the suffering of sentient beings 
1 Destroying the  lord of death ∗ 
2 Destroying poverty

[3,29] 
It is the supreme ambrosia 
That overcomes the sovereignty of death, 
It is the inexhaustible treasure 
That eliminates all poverty in the world. 
3B2B 2A2A 3A2A 1 Destroying the  lord of death 
This is indicated in the first two lines of verse 29: “It is the supreme ambr osia that overcomes the 
sovereignty of death”. Bodhicitta is the supreme el ixir or ambrosia that can overcome the lord of 
the death. The lord of death refers to the mara  of death because we have to die without any 
control or choice. Why is bodhicitta  the supreme elixir or ambrosia that can overcome the lord 
of death? This is because,  with bodhicitta, one will attain the state of full enlightenment which is 
free from aging and death. Due to the power of th e mind of bodhicitta, one will achieve the state 
of full enlightenment.   The Buddha is also called the Vict orious One. Why? This is beca use he has gained victory over 
the four maras. One of the four maras is the mara  of death. Once we have achieved the state of 
full enlightenment, then one is beyond death, one does not have to die nor does one age.  
3B2B 2A2A 3A2A 2 Destroying poverty 
Bodhicitta has the power to destroy poverty as well. This is indicated in the last two lines of verse 
29: “It is the inexhaustible treasure that eliminat es all poverty in the world.” After we generate

147 bodhicitta and progress along the path, we move from one ground to the next. Bodhisattvas who 
are on the eighth ground achieve the sky treasure which means that from that time onwards, they 
w i l l  e x p e r i e n c e  n o  p o v e r t y .  I n  f a c t ,  t h e y  a r e  a b le to eliminate the poverty of limitless sentient
